On traitera également de la \ndocumentation dans les dossiers du bénéficiare et la rédaction de rapports.  \nNew course \nPSSTC 110 – Structure and Function of the Human Body, Health and \nChronic Illness  \n*3(fi 6)(either semestres, 3-0-0) \n This course introduces the student to the anatomy and physiology of the 12 \nsystems making up the human body throughout the ages.  The most common \nillnesses encountered by Health Care Aides will be discussed and information \non how to provide safe care based on best practices according to the client’s \ndiagnoses, needs, and care plans will be developed.  Students will also review \ncare strategies on how to provide compassionate end-of-life care to support the \nclient, the client’s family, and yourself through the process of a client’s dying and \ndeath.  \nModules: \nBody Systems and Function, Theory Hrs: 18 \nHuman Growth and Development, Theory Hrs: 3 \nHealth, Aging and Independence, Theory Hrs: 6 \nChronic Conditions, Theory Hrs: 12 \nMeeting Care Needs at the Time of Death, Theory Hrs: 3 \nEnd of Life Care, Theory Hrs: 6 \nPSSTC 110 – Structure et fonction du corps humain, la santé et la maladie \nchronique \n*3(fi 6)(l’un ou l’autre des semestres, 3-0-0) \nCe cours vise à initier les étudiants à l’anatomie et à la physiologie des douze \nsystèmes composant l’organisme humain en fonction de l’âge.  On discutera \ndes pathologies les plus fréquentes qui se présenteront aux préposés aux soins \nde santé et on transmettra l’information sur la façon de prodiguer des soins \nsécuritaires selon les pratiques exemplaires et en fonction des diagnostics du \nbénéficiare, de ses besoins et des plans de soins seront élaborés.  Les \nétudiants examineront aussi les stratégies de soins en fin de vie fondées sur la \ncompassion et l’accompagnement du client, le soutien à la famille et \nl’encadrement du soignant tout au long du processus menant à la mort du \nbénéficiare.   \nNew course \nPSSTC 121 – Meeting Complex Care Needs  \n*2(fi 4)(wither semestres, 1-0-1.5) \nThis course provides advance skills needed to support care such as wound, \nostomy, respiratory, catheter and tube feed care.  The student will also acquire \nskills to accurately measure vital signs.   \nModules: \nAssisting with Wound Care, Theory, Hrs: 1.5, Lab Hrs: 1.5 \nAssisting with Nasogastric and Gastrostomy Care and Tube Feeds, Theory Hrs: 1.5, \nLab Hrs: 1.5 \nAssisting with Urinary Catheters and Drainage Systems, Theory Hrs: 2, Lab Hrs: 2 \nAssisting with Ostomy Care, Theory Hrs: 2, Lab Hrs: 2 \nMeasuring Vital Signs, Pain, Height and Weight, Theory Hrs: 3, Lab Hrs: 9 \nAssisting with Specimen Collection, Theory Hrs: 1.5, Lab Hrs: 1.5 \nAssisting with Respiratory Care and oral Suctioning, Theory Hrs: 3, Lab Hrs: 3 \nPSSTC 121 –Satisfaire aux besoins complexes \n*2(fi 4)(l’un ou l’autre des semestres, 1-0-1.5) \nCe cours porte sur l’enseignement des compétences spécialisées nécessaires à \nl’administration de soins dans des cas de blessure, de stomie, de problèmes \nrespiratoires, de cathéters et d’alimentation par sonde.  Les étudiants \nacquerront également des compétences lui permettant d’interpréter les signes \nvitaux avec précision.  \nNew course \nPSSTC 122 – Special Activities for Diverse Clients \n*3(fi 6)(either semestres, 3-0-2/14) \nThis course prepares the students to work with a diverse client group in a \nvariety of care settings.  The students will here develop skills to assist with the \ncare of clients facing dementia, mental health disorders, developmental delays, \nand physical disabilities.  The care for infants and children will also be discussed \nin this course.  \nModules:  \nWork with Clients with a Diagnosis of Dementia, Theory Hrs: 18 \nAssisting with the Care of Infants, Theory Hrs: 2, Lab Hrs: 2 \nAssisting with Child Care, Theory Hrs: 3 \nAssisting Clients with Physical Disabilities and developmental Delays, Theory Hrs:5 \nCaring for Clients with a Mental Health Diagnosis, Theory Hrs: 10 \nPSSTC 122 –  Activités spéciales pour  clients ayant diverses conditions.  \n*3(fi 6)(l’un ou l’autre des semestres, 3-0-2/14) \nCe cours prépare les étudiants à travailler auprès d’un groupe diversifié de \nbénéficiares dans un éventail de contextes de soins.  Les étudiants acquerront \ndes compétences leur permettant d’aider à l’administration de soins à des \nbénéficiaires aux prises avec la démence, des troubles psychiques, des retards \nde développement et des limitations physiques.  Les soins aux nourrissons et \naux enfants seront également abordés dans le cadre de ce cours.   \nNew course \nPSSTC 150 – Integrated Clinical Practicum I \n*2(fi 4)(either semestres, 0-8-0) \nUnder the direction of a regulated health-care professional, students will work in \na designated assisted living (DAL) site providing care and support for clients.  \nThis clinical practicum may be completed under the direct supervision of a \nprofessional nurse who is fulfilling the role of clinical instructor or under the \nindirect supervision of a clinical instructor through a mentored practicum \nexperience. Prerequisite:  Successful  completion of PSSTC 120  \nPSSTC 150 – Stage clinique intégré I \n*2(fi 4)(l’un ou l’autre des semestres, 0-8-0) \nSous la direction d’un professionnel de la santé réglementé, les étudiants \ndispenseront des soins et une assistance aux bénéficiares d’un centre de soins \nde longue durée.  Ce stage en clinique peut être réalisé sous la supervision \ndirecte d’un infirmier professionnel qui assume le rôle d’enseignant clinique ou \nsous la supervision indirecte de l’enseignant responsable de l’encadrement d’un \nstage en clinique. Préalable :  La réussite de PSSTC 120.      \nNew course \nPSSTC 151 – Integrated Clinical Practicum II \n*4(fi 8)(either semestres, 0-12-0) \nUnder the direction of a regulated health-care professional, students will work in \na continuing care setting providing care and support for clients.  The practicum \nmay be completed under the direct or indirect supervision of an instructor or an \nonsite mentor. Prerequisite:  Successful completion of all courses including \nPSSTC 150.   \nPSSTC 151 – Stage clinique intégré  II \n*4(fi 8)(l’un ou l’autre des semestres, 0-12-0) \nSous la direction d’un professionnel de la santé réglementé, les étudiants \ndispenseront des soins  et une assistance aux bénéficiaires d’un centre de \nsoins continus.  Le stage peut être réalisé sous la supervision directe ou \nindirecte d’un enseignant ou d’un mentor sur les lieux. Préalable :  La réussite \nde tous les cours du programme incluant PSSTC 150.  \nAppendix C: Letters of support \nJune 16, 2016  \nMme Nisrine Mokri  \nDirector of Health Programs   \nFaculty Service Officer \nCampus Saint-Jean  \nUniversity of Alberta  \nOn behalf of Saint Elizabeth Health Care, I am pleased to provide this letter of support for the \ncreation of a Health Care Aide Program at Campus Saint-Jean.  \nSaint Elizabeth is a diversified health care company and not-for-profit charitable organization \nthat has been opening the door to new possibilities and care experiences for more than a \ncentury. Recognized as Canada’s largest social enterprise, we employ 8,000 people who \nspread hope and happiness through 18,000 care exchanges daily. Since 2014, we have been \nworking with Covenant Care to provide direct care services at Saint-Thomas Health Centre. \nSaint-Thomas has 200-units and is committed to meeting the health care needs of the \nEdmonton francophone community.  \nAs the population ages and the demand for health and social care services continues to grow, \nthe availability of qualified, well-trained Health Care Aides is vital to meeting the needs of \nAlberta’s French speaking population.  As a leader in person and family centred care, Saint \nElizabeth is committed to providing exceptional care through a holistic approach that meets \nthe unique linguistic, cultural and health needs of the people and communities we serve.  \nUniversity of Alberta Campus Saint-Jean is a leader in bilingual education, and as such, is \nwell situated to train bilingual and French-speaking Health Care Aides.  We wish you great \nsuccess and look forward to hearing more about the program.  \nSincerely,   \nRheta Fanizza  \nChief Business Officer & Senior Vice President, Innovation  \nSaint Elizabeth  \nCc: Paul Denis Board Chair, Community Board, Saint Thomas Health Centre  \n90 Allstate Parkway, Suite 300  \nMarkham, ON L3R 6H3  \nT 905.940.9655  \nF 905.940.9934  \nwww.saintelizabeth.com  \n    To support our charitable programs, please visit our website.  \nMay 29th. 2016 \nMme Nisrine Mokri \nDirector of Health Programs  \nFaculty Service Officer \nCampus Saint-Jean, U of A \nI want to personally support the creation of a Health Care Aide Program at \nCampus Saint-Jean. Such a program would be welcomed by the francophone \ncommunity as the need for Health Care Aides has become evident over the past \nfew years and it will continue to grow at a rapid pace.  \nIn a publication prepared by the Francophone Seniors Federation of Alberta \npublished in 2012, the following statistics on the francophone population of \nAlberta was reported. Most of this information comes from Statistics Canada. \nThere are 68,500 Albertans whose first language is French.  \nEdmonton accounts for 42% or 28,700 Francophones.  \nSt-Paul/Bonnyville has 8% or 5,500 Francophones. \nPeace River area has also 8% or 5,500 Francophones. \nAccording to statistics Canada the Baby Boomer population will almost double \nbetween 2011 and 2036, going from 14% to 24% of the general population. \nHowever the Francophone group has a higher number of seniors per capita \nthan the general population.  \nIn Edmonton alone the Francophone seniors will number 6,900 people. If we \nadd the numbers from St-Paul/Bonnyville and Peace River, an additional 2,600 \npeople, the total number of francophone seniors is close to 10,000. Many will be \nlooking for continuing care health services in French. \nSome 15% to 20% of these people will require supportive living services. \nConservatively, as many as 1,300 seniors from Edmonton are potential clients \nfor the St-Thomas Health Center operated by Covenant Care, situated across \nthe street from Campus Saint-Jean. Add an uncertain number from areas North \nof Edmonton and Calgary and this number could be as many as 2,000.  \nEdmonton has seen its francophone population increase considerably with \nimmigration from French speaking African countries. The needs for continuing \ncare will become more evident as this population grows older. It would be \nimportant to find students from these countries registered in this Health Care \nAide Program.  \nGraduates from the Campus Saint-Jean Health Care Aide Program could find \nemployment immediately at the St-Thomas Health Care Center which could \nalso provide practicums for the students.   \nThis is an exciting situation which can lead to the creation of a great example of \nhealth care that meets the needs of a growing population, taking into account \nthe need for linguistically & culturally adapted health care.  \nIt would also highlight the contribution to the francophone community of the \nUniversity of Alberta Campus Saint-Jean. The francophone community created \nCollège Saint-Jean, now part of the U of A, now known as Campus St. Jean. It \nis fitting that the University provide this program which would meet an ever more \nimportant need in the francophone community.  \nAs President of the Community Board of St-Thomas Health Center, I can \nconfirm the unreserved support of all the members of our board. St-Thomas \nalso houses close to 100 francophone seniors who occupy apartments in the \nindependent living area. These people would like to know that when the time \ncomes, should they need supportive living services, they will have access to \nqualified and well trained Health Care Aides. It would bring great comfort to \nthem if we could tell them that Campus Saint-Jean will soon be offering a \nprogram that will meet that need.  \nAlso, the Calgary area has a considerable number of francophone seniors who \nneed or will need supportive living health care services over the next decade \nand more. Trained health care aids graduating from Campus Saint-Jean may \nvery well be able to fill positions in all parts of Alberta wherever the need is \nmanifest.    \nShould you require more information or assistance in promoting the Health Care \nAide Program at Campus Saint-Jean, please do not hesitate to communicate \nwith me at your convenience. \nRegards; \nPaul Denis \nPresident \nCommunity Board \nSt-Thomas Health Center     \nAppendix D :  Prior Learning Assessment and Recognition  \nPréposé aux soins de santé(PSS), Prior Learning Assessment and Recognition(PLAR) \nThe PSS PLAR process is a certification process for individuals who have had directly related \ncontinuing care work experience in the past three years but do not currently have a certificate that is \nrecognized I Alberta.  These would include: \n1.  individuals who have been working as a nursing attendant or personal support worker, but do \nnot have a certificate currently recognized in Alberta.  \n2. nurses or nursing attendants trained in another country who have experience in geriatric care \nand believe they have the skills and knowledge to meet the standards for the CCA  Préposé \naux soins de santé certificate. \nIndividuals requesting that their prior experiences be recognized, they must    \n1. meet the Admission Requirements for the PSS certificate program and   \n2. have 600 hours of experience in the last 3 years as a healthcare worker.  These must be \ndocumented with a proof-of-employment letter from the employer(s). \nOnce individuals have been approved for the PSS PLAR process, they will receive an individualized \nlearner plan.    The CCA’s PSS qualified staff will evaluate the profile of each individual requesting \nadmission to the PSS PLAR certificate.  All candidates will take the following mandatory course:  \nPSSTC 120  Prodiguer des soins aux clients.   \nDepending on their individualized plan, candidates may challenge the other courses in the program \nwhile taking the mandatory courses.  \nClasses will be offered in a hybrid online model including face to face classes and labs.   \nAppendix F : Market Analysis Report by Axion \n UNIVERSITY AND COLLEGE \nPROGRAMS IN HEALTH  \nMarch 2016 \nMARKET STUDY PREPARED FOR CAMPUS SAINT-JEAN \nOttawa, ON \nTel.: 613-369-4333 \nwww.axionplus.biz \n MARKET STUDY: UNIVERSITY AND COLLEGE PROGRAMS IN HEALTH  2  \n FINAL REPORT – MARCH 2016 \nPROJECT COORDINATION: Axion  \nPROJECT ADVISORS: \nDenis Fontaine, Campus Saint-Jean  \nDolorèse Nolette, Campus Saint-Jean  \nThis study was conducted in cooperation with the Campus Saint-Jean component of the Consortium national \nde formation en santé (national health education consortium). \nMARKET STUDY: UNIVERSITY AND COLLEGE PROGRAMS IN HEALTH  3  \nFINAL REPORT – MARCH 2016 \nTABLE OF CONTENTS  \n PAGE  \nI. DANS L’OUEST ET \nLE NORD DU CANADA \n107 \nMARKET STUDY: UNIVERSITY AND COLLEGE PROGRAMS IN HEALTH  7  \nFINAL REPORT – MARCH 2016 \nI. SUMMARY \nCampus Saint-Jean is studying the possibility of creating eight new college and university programs to \ntrain Francophone and bilingual professionals in the health sector.  \nThe market study targets the health service needs of Francophones in relation to each proposed program. \nParticular attention is paid to the college Health Care Aide program.  \nThere are three distinct elements to the methodology used in carrying out this study, namely demographic \nresearch regarding Francophones in Alberta, consultation with the province’s Francophone communities, \nand a market study for eight distinct college and university programs. \nDemographic data \nAccording to the 2011 National Household Survey, Francophones represent 2.1% of the total population of \nAlberta, or 74,075 people whose first official language spoken (FOLS) is French.  \nBetween 2006 and 2011, the population of Alberta with French as their first official language spoken \nincreased by 10.6%, from 67,000 to 74,074 people. In 10 years (2001-2011), the population of Alberta with \nFrench as their first official language spoken increased by 19.5%, or 12,070 people. The sharp increase in \nthe number of people with French as their first official language spoken is explained in large part by the \narrival of recent immigrants whose first official language spoken was French. In fact, three out of four new \nresidents of Alberta (76.7%) with French as their first official language spoken were recent immigrants who \narrived in Canada between 2001 and 2011. \nThe Francophone population of Alberta is older than the province’s population. In fact, 27.8% of \nFrancophones were 55 or older in 2011, compared to 21% of the population as a whole. As well, according \nto the 2006 census by Statistics Canada, 80% of Francophone seniors aged 65 and over were located in \nfour census divisions: 42% in the Edmonton metropolitan area, 22% in the greater Calgary area, 8% in \nnortheastern region, and 8% in the northwest.  \nThis situation could have an impact on the demand for health services over the short, medium and long \nterms. In fact, several Canadian and international studies show a sharp decline in the second language \nlearned (in this case English) among seniors after their retirement. Moreover, studies clearly show that, in \nstressful situations, seniors tend to turn to their mother tongue. Finally, seniors with a cognitive disability \nsuch as dementia gradually lose their ability to communicate in the second language learned. \nAnother characteristic of the Francophone population in Alberta is the sharp rise in the number of \nFrancophone immigrants who arrived in the province between 2001 and 2011. It must be noted that 41% of \nvery recent immigrants have indicated only French as their first official language spoken. In general, \nimmigrants who arrive in Canada are in relatively good health. However, several studies note that the \nhealth of immigrants tends to deteriorate.  \nConsultations with Francophone communities  \n22 NOMBRE DE JEUNES INSCRITS À UN PROGRAMME D’IMMERSION FRANÇAISE 107 \n23 RECOMMANDATIONS DES PROGRAMMES 108 \nMARKET STUDY: UNIVERSITY AND COLLEGE PROGRAMS IN HEALTH  8 \n FINAL REPORT – MARCH 2016 \nIn order to identify the need for Francophone and bilingual labour to better serve Francophone \ncommunities in Alberta in terms of health care, consultations were organized in four regions (Calgary, \nEdmonton, Northeast and Northwest). A total of 68 people participated in the 6 consultations. \nA discussion template with four main questions was prepared to gather the same information from the \nvarious communities:  \n Health services currently available in French; \n Health services that should be available as a priority in French; \n The relevance of creating a health care aide program; \n The order of priority for the programs previously identified. \nIn light of the profile of the Francophone population and the results obtained from the consultations, the \nproject managers selected five college programs and three university programs, for which Axion \ndeveloped targeted market studies.  \nCollege Programs University programs \n Health care aide (NOC 3413)  Nurse practitioner (NOC 3152) \n Licensed practical nurse (NOC 3233)  Psychologist (NOC 4151) \n Mental health worker (NOC 4212)  Dietitian (NOC 3132)  \n Emergency medical personnel (NOC 3234)  \n Speech language assistant (NOC 3235)  \nCollege program – Health care aide \nIn Alberta, 31,083 people were working as nurse aides, orderlies and patient service associates as of \nDecember 31, 2014, making that category of profession the second largest in the number of employees in \nthe province’s health sector.  \nGiven the average age of 44 for nurse aides, orderlies and patient service associates (NOC 3413), \nretirements are expected to increase considerably over the next few years. \nAccording to data from the Alberta Learning Information Service, obtained through the 2013 Alberta Wage \nand Salary Survey, 40% of employers seeking to hire health care aides had difficulty finding employees and \n12% still had positions available after 4 months. According to the data, the vacancy rate for health care \naides was at 4% in 2013.  \nAll respondents believe that there will be a moderate expansion of the job market for graduates from a \nFrancophone or bilingual health care aide program over the next five to seven years. Although health \nagencies that hire these professionals are not seeking bilingual personnel, the demand for health care \naides will continue to increase, as health professionals are ageing and retiring and the Francophone \npopulation is continuing to age. As well, it is very difficult to find Francophone or bilingual health care \naides.  \nLike everywhere else in Canada, the profession of health care aide is not regulated in Alberta. Educational \ninstitutions issue a certificate to attest to the student’s success, but there is no final program examination. \nMARKET STUDY: UNIVERSITY AND COLLEGE PROGRAMS IN HEALTH  9  \nFINAL REPORT – MARCH 2016 \nHowever, in 2001, Alberta Health established a competency profile for health care aides. An updated \ncompetency profile should be released in 2016.  \nAt this time, the program is offered by a large number of private institutions, community colleges and \nemployers certified by the province. No institution offers the health care aide program in French.  \nIn the other western provinces, two programs are offered in French: a health care aid certificate at \nUniversité de Saint-Boniface and the distance and classroom resident care attendant program from \nÉducacentre College. According to Éducacentre College’s website, its program is not recognized by the \nGovernment of Alberta, unlike the vast majority of programs offered by private and public institutions in \nAlberta.  \nGiven the promising number of positions for health care aides that will be created in the province over the \nnext few years, the high percentage of elderly Francophones in Alberta (primarily in Edmonton and the \nnortheast and northwest of the province), the difficulty finding bilingual health care aides at this time, the \ninterest of new Francophone immigrants in taking such training, and the lack of French-language training \nprograms approved by the Province of Alberta that are offered in the province or elsewhere in the West, \nAxion recommends that Campus Saint-Jean create a health care aide program to be offered in French. \nCollege program – Licensed practical nurse \nIn Alberta, 13,915 people were working as licensed practical nurses as of December 31, 2015. That \nprofession grew by 51% between 2009 and 2014. At this time, 5.3% of licensed practical nurses working in \nthe province obtained their diploma abroad. \nAccording to data from the Alberta Learning Information Service, obtained through the 2013 Alberta Wage \nand Salary Survey, 23% of employers seeking to hire licensed practical nurses had difficulty finding \nemployees and 6% still had positions available after 4 months.  \nAlmost all respondents believe that there will be a moderate expansion of the job market for graduates \nfrom a Francophone or bilingual licensed practical nurse program over the next five to seven years. \nRespondents indicated that the greatest demand for licensed practical nurses would be from hospitals, \nlong-term care facilities, and Primary Care Networks. To a lesser degree, community centres and home \ncare services could also recruit licensed practical nurses. \nIn Alberta, the profession of licensed practical nurse is regulated by the Health Professions Act and the \nLicensed Practical Nurses Profession Regulation. To exercise the profession, a licensed practical nurse must \nhave successfully completed a training program approved by the Education Standards Advisory \nCommittee, a committee of the College of Licensed Practical Nurses of Alberta (CLPNA), and have passed \nthe Canadian Practical Nurse Registration examination administered by the CLPNA.  \nAt this time, 10 colleges offer a licensed practical nurse program in English across the province. No \ninstitution offers the licensed practical nurse program in French. Only one program is offered in French in \nWestern Canada, in Winnipeg. However, it seems that that program is not recognized by the College of \nLicensed Practical Nurses of Alberta.  \nGiven the promising forecasts regarding the number of positions for licensed practical nurses that will be \ncreated in the province over the next few years, the high percentage of elderly Francophones in Alberta \n(primarily in Edmonton and the northeast and northwest of the province), the difficulty finding bilingual \nMARKET STUDY: UNIVERSITY AND COLLEGE PROGRAMS IN HEALTH  10 \n FINAL REPORT – MARCH 2016 \nlicensed practical nurses at this time, the interest of new Francophone immigrants in taking such training, \nand the lack of French-language training programs approved by the CLPNA that are offered in the province \nor elsewhere in the West, Axion recommends that Campus Saint-Jean create a licensed practical nurse \nprogram to be offered in French. \nCollege program – Mental health worker \nIn 2013, more than 12,000 people were working as social and community service workers (NOC 4212) in \nAlberta. Job prospects are above average. This group is expected to see growth of 3.3% per year by 2017. It \nis expected that 396 positions for social and community service workers will be created each year, in \naddition to the positions that will become available due to changes in positions, promotions or \nretirements.  \nAccording to data from the Alberta Learning Information Service, obtained through the 2013 Alberta Wage \nand Salary Survey, 29% of employers seeking to hire mental health workers had difficulty finding \nemployees and 6% still had positions available after 4 months.  \nMost respondents believe that there will be a sharp or moderate expansion of the job market for graduates \nfrom a Francophone or bilingual mental health worker program over the next five to seven years. \nRespondents indicated that the greatest demand for mental health workers would be from the educational \nsector, community health centres and Primary Care Networks.  \nThe profession of mental health worker is not regulated in Alberta. The qualifications for mental health \nworkers can vary considerably. Some are nurses, some are psychologists, and some are social workers.  \nNo college in Alberta offers a mental health worker program as such. Four institutions offer a community \nservice worker or community support worker program, which seems similar at first glance to the mental \nhealth worker program found in other provinces. No French-language or bilingual institutions offer such a \nprogram in French in Western Canada.  \nGiven the promising forecasts regarding the number of positions for social and community service workers \nthat will be created in the province over the next few years, the high percentage of Francophone seniors in \nAlberta (primarily in Edmonton and the northeast and northwest of the province), the high percentage of \nnew immigrants who speak only French as their first official language and who have recently arrived in \nlarge centres such as Calgary and Edmonton, the difficulty finding bilingual mental health workers at this \ntime, the increased social awareness of mental health problems, and the lack of this type of training \nprogram in French in Alberta and Western Canada, Axion recommends that Campus Saint-Jean create a \nmental health worker program to be offered in French. \nMoreover, given that no training programs for mental health workers are currently offered in English in \nAlberta or in Western Canada, we recommend further reflection be undertaken with potential employers \n(school boards, clinics, etc.) to find the exact name of the French-language program. \nCollege program – Emergency medical personnel \nIn Alberta, on December 31, 2015, there were 3,843 EMRs, 4,259 EMTs, and 2,579 EMT-Ps, for a total of \n10,681 professionals. Between 2009 and 2013, growth in this profession varied widely based on the level of \nMARKET STUDY: UNIVERSITY AND COLLEGE PROGRAMS IN HEALTH  11  \nFINAL REPORT – MARCH 2016 \nqualification of personnel. Growth was very high for technicians and technologists, while it was rather low \nfor medical responders.  \nAccording to data from the Alberta Learning Information Service, obtained through the 2013 Alberta Wage \nand Salary Survey, 37% of employers seeking to hire emergency medical personnel and paramedics had \ndifficulty finding employees and 19% still had positions available after 4 months. According to the data, the \nvacancy rate for emergency medical personnel and paramedics was at 3% in 2013.  \nRespondents indicated that there will be a sharp or moderate expansion of the job market for graduates \nfrom an emergency medical personnel or paramedic staff program over the next five to seven years. The \ngreatest demand will be for EMR paramedic staff. The number of graduates is not sufficient to keep up the \ngrowth in this sector and to replace departures. Respondents indicated that the greatest demand for these \nprofessionals would be from the private sector, Alberta Health and the industrial sector. One respondent \nindicated that the region of Girouxville, McLennan and Clare had needs for Francophone or bilingual \nemergency medical personnel or paramedics. According to the respondents, there is a high demand for \nambulance transport for seniors who do not have a car, particularly in rural areas. \nIn Alberta, the profession of emergency medical personnel and paramedics is regulated by the Alberta \nCollege of Paramedics. To be recognized as an EMR, EMT or EMT-P, emergency medical personnel must \nhave successfully completed a training program that meets Alberta College of Paramedics standards and \nmust pass Alberta College of Paramedics written and practical exams within one year.  \nSeveral programs are offered in Alberta to train emergency medical personnel (EMR, EMT and EMT-P). \nEleven institutions offer the EMR program approved by the Alberta College of Paramedics, seven offer the \nEMT certificate program, and five offer the EMT-P diploma program. No French-language or bilingual \ninstitutions offer these programs in French in Western Canada.  \nGiven the promising forecasts regarding the number of positions for emergency medical personnel and \nparamedics that will be created in the province over the next few years, the high percentage of elderly \nFrancophones in Alberta (primarily in Edmonton and the northeast and northwest of the province), the \ndifficulty finding emergency medical personnel and paramedics at this time, and the lack of French-\nlanguage training programs that are offered in Alberta and Western Canada, Axion recommends that \nCampus Saint-Jean create a training program for emergency medical personnel and paramedics to be \noffered in French and approved by the Alberta College of Paramedics. \nThe programs that seem most interesting are the certificate programs (Emergency Medical Technician – \nEmergency medical personnel or EMT) and diploma programs (Emergency Medical Technologist - \nParamedic or EMT-P).  \nCollege program – Speech language assistant \nIn 2013, more than 2,900 people were working in the Other Technical Occupations in Therapy and \nAssessment category (NOC 3235). The vacancy rate for technical personnel in therapy and assessment was at 14% in \n2013. \nMost respondents believe that there will be a sharp or moderate expansion of the job market for graduates \nfrom a speech language assistant program over the next five to seven years. Speech language assistants can \nobtain voluntary accreditation from the Therapy Assistant Association of Alberta if they obtain their \ndiploma from a program recognized by the Association and if they have worked as a speech language \nassistant for at least 3,000 hours in three years.  \nAt this time in Alberta, only two colleges offer a speech language assistant program that is recognized by \nthe Therapy Assistant Association of Alberta. One is in Edmonton and the other in Medicine Hat. No \nFrench-language or bilingual institutions offer such a program in French in Western Canada.  \nGiven the promising forecasts regarding the number of positions in the category of Other Technical \nOccupations in Therapy and Assessment (NOC 3235) that will be created in the province over the next few \nyears, the high percentage of Francophone seniors in Alberta (primarily in Edmonton and the northeast \nand northwest of the province), the high percentage of new immigrants who speak only French as their \nfirst official language and who have recently arrived in large centres such as Calgary and Edmonton, the \ndifficulty finding Francophone speech language assistants at this time, and the lack of training programs in \nFrench in Alberta and Western Canada, Axion recommends that Campus Saint-Jean create an speech \nlanguage assistant program to be offered in French. \nGiven that the pool of jobs for Francophone speech language assistants is nonetheless limited in Alberta \nand Western Canada, it is recommended that the program include courses on language development in \nEnglish and on the phonology of both languages so graduates are able to work in French and in English.  \nUniversity program – Nurse practitioner \nAs of December 31, 2015, there were 491 nurse practitioners in Alberta. However, this group of \nprofessionals grew by 52.6% between 2009 and 2014.  \nAccording to the professional nurse practitioners associations, the lack of funding makes it difficult to hire \nnurse practitioners in primary care. Other barriers cited by the professional associations are federal and \nprovincial laws. \nMost respondents believe that there will be a sharp or moderate expansion of the job market for graduates \nfrom a nurse practitioner program over the next five to seven years. One respondent felt that there would \nonly be low expansion in this area because, in his opinion, the province does not support the use of this \ntype of professional. Moreover, according to that respondent, 75% of nurse practitioners in the province do \nnot do the work for which they were trained.  \nMARKET STUDY: UNIVERSITY AND COLLEGE PROGRAMS IN HEALTH  13  \nFINAL REPORT – MARCH 2016 \nRespondents indicated that most demand for nurse practitioners would be from hospitals and long-term \ncare centres. Four out of the seven respondents also indicated their interest in hiring a total of 11.5 \nfull-time nurse practitioners and 1 part-time nurse practitioner in the next three years.  \nThe occupation of nurse practitioner in Alberta requires certification as a registered nurse in Alberta or \nanother province or territory, successful completion of a program approved by the Nurse Practitioner \nAssociation of Alberta or a similar program recognized elsewhere in Canada, and a passing mark on the \nAlberta nurse practitioner accreditation exam. \nAt this time in Alberta, only three universities offer a nurse practitioner program in English. No \nFrench-language or bilingual institutions offer such a program in French in Western Canada.  \nIt is difficult to predict the growth in this sector due to the small number of nurse practitioners within the \nlarger category of registered nurses. As well, graduates have difficulty working in their field of expertise \nand there is a lack of provincial funding for hiring this type of professional. Moreover, the sampling of \nemployers interviewed is limited and Axion was unable to obtain information from hospital human \nresource departments. We must also consider the interest shown in this type of profession among the \npeople interviewed (possible creation of 11.5 nurse practitioner positions over three years) and the lack of \ntraining programs in French in Alberta and Western Canada. Therefore, Axion recommends that Campus \nSaint-Jean study the market further before making a decision to create a French or bilingual nurse \npractitioner program. \nUniversity program – Psychologist  \nAs of December 31, 2015, there were 2,628 psychologists in Alberta. This group of professionals grew by \n13.8% between 2009 and 2014. As of December 31, 2015, the average age of psychologists was 49, and \n54.6% were 50 or younger. Just over 45% of psychologists (1,192) will be of retirement age in the next five \nyears.  \nAccording to data from the Alberta Learning Information Service, obtained through the 2013 Alberta Wage \nand Salary Survey, 38% of employers seeking to hire psychologists had difficulty finding employees and \n9% still had positions available after 4 months. The vacancy rate for psychologist positions was at 3% in \n2013. \nAll respondents believe that there will be a sharp or moderate expansion of the job market for graduates \nfrom a psychology program over the next five to seven years. The respondents indicated that the greatest \ndemand for psychologists will be from private practices, schools, mental health centres, hospitals and crisis \nlines. Four out of the seven respondents also indicated their interest in hiring a total of nine full-time \npsychologists in the next three years. The representative from the Psychologists’ Association of Alberta \nindicated receiving frequent requests for Francophone psychologists.  \nThe occupation of psychologist in Alberta requires accreditation from the College of Alberta Psychologists \n(CAP). To obtain accreditation, the person must have a master’s degree in psychology that meets CAP \nacademic criteria, have worked at least 1,600 hours under the supervision of an accredited psychologist \nrecognized by CAP, and have passed an exam in clinical or theoretical psychology approved by CAP.  \nAlberta and Quebec are the only provinces where a psychologist can practice the profession with a \nmaster’s degree and not a doctorate. At this time, six universities offer a master’s program in psychology. \nMARKET STUDY: UNIVERSITY AND COLLEGE PROGRAMS IN HEALTH  14 \n FINAL REPORT – MARCH 2016 \nFour are Alberta universities, while the other two are foreign universities. No French-language or bilingual \ninstitutions offer such a program in French in Western Canada.  \nGiven the promising forecasts regarding the number of psychologist positions that will be created in the \nprovince over the next few years, the large number of psychologists who will be of retirement age in the \nnext five years, the high percentage of Francophone seniors in Alberta (primarily in Edmonton and the \nnortheast and northwest of the province), the high percentage of new immigrants who speak only French \nas their first official language and who have recently arrived in large centres such as Calgary and \nEdmonton, the difficulty finding Francophone psychologists at this time, and the lack of training programs \nin French in Alberta and Western Canada, Axion recommends that Campus Saint-Jean create a master \nof psychology program. \nUniversity program – Dietitian  \nAs of December 31, 2015, there were 1,238 dietitians in Alberta. This group of professionals grew by 23.8% \nbetween 2009 and 2014. As of December 31, 2015, the average age of dietitians was 39, making it a very \nyoung profession; 80.9% of dietitians were 50 or younger.  \nThe Alberta Learning Information Service has no statistics regarding the predicted growth rate for the \nprofession over the next few years. However, according to the health and social services industry profile \nprepared by the Government of Alberta in 2015, this sector is expected to continue growing at an average \nrate of 3% per year between 2015 and 2019. That rate will be greater than the average growth of the other \nsectors of the Alberta economy.  \nAlmost all respondents believe that there will be a sharp or moderate expansion of the job market for \ngraduates from a dietitian program over the next five to seven years.  \nRespondents indicated that the greatest demand for dietitians will be from Primary Care Networks, \nhospitals, retirement homes, mental health centres, long-term care facilities, home care programs, private \npractices, and community health centres.  \nTo work as a dietitian in Alberta, accreditation is required from the College of Dietitians of Alberta (CDA). \nTo obtain accreditation, the person must have a bachelor or master’s degree in food sciences or nutrition \napproved by the CDA, have completed an internship of 35 to 40 weeks in an accredited Canadian \ninternship program, under the supervision of an accredited dietitian, and have passed the Canadian \nDietetic Registration Examination. \nIn Alberta, only the University of Alberta offers a Nutrition and Food Sciences program accredited by the \nCDA. It is also the only university to offer an integrated internship program recognized by the CDA. No \ninstitutions offer such a program in French in the province or in Western Canada.  \nGiven the growth seen in this profession between 2009 and 2014 and the expansion anticipated by \nrespondents to the survey over the next few years, the increase in health problems due to obesity and the \nincreased use of dietitian services to prevent and treat health problems, the high percentage of \nFrancophone seniors in Alberta (primarily in Edmonton and the northeast and northwest of the province), \nthe high percentage of obese people in the North, the high percentage of new immigrants who speak only \nFrench as their first official language and who have recently arrived in large centres such as Calgary and \nEdmonton, the difficulty finding Francophone dietitians at this time, and the lack of training programs in \nMARKET STUDY: UNIVERSITY AND COLLEGE PROGRAMS IN HEALTH  15  \nFINAL REPORT – MARCH 2016 \nFrench in Alberta and Western Canada, Axion recommends that Campus Saint-Jean create a bachelor of \nnutrition program. \nPool of potential students \nA study of the pool of potential students shows that 20,889 young students are currently studying in \nFrench in the provinces and territories of Western Canada, in addition to the 302,849 attending immersion \nschools in the same area.  \nThere is a large market for Campus Saint-Jean. However, to attract them, the institution must adopt \nquality programs in accordance with industry standards. The programs must also be presented in an \ninnovative manner in order to meet the needs of Francophone students throughout Alberta and elsewhere \nin the West and North. Competition is fierce. Youth have a wide choice of programs and institutions. \nCampus Saint-Jean must position itself as a hub for health programs in French in Western and Northern \nCanada.  \nMARKET STUDY: UNIVERSITY AND COLLEGE PROGRAMS IN HEALTH  16 \n FINAL REPORT – MARCH 2016 \n1. PRESENTATION OF THE STUDY \n1.1 BACKGROUND  \nCampus Saint-Jean is studying the possibility of creating eight new college and university programs to \ntrain Francophone and bilingual professionals in the health sector.  \nAt this time, Campus Saint-Jean does not offer any health programs at the college level, but offers a \nbilingual Bachelor of Science in Nursing and a Postgraduate Certificate in Francophone Practice for Speech \nlanguage therapists. \nThe market study targets the health service needs of Francophones in relation to each proposed program. \nParticular attention is paid to the college Health care aide program.  \nBefore making a decision to launch new programs, it is essential that Campus Saint-Jean rely on solid data. \nThe educational institution wishes to: \n Identify the major health needs of Francophones; \n Determine the job market demand for new college and university programs to train health care \nprofessionals; \n Analyze and interpret the data regarding job market demand in order to make informed decisions \nregarding the creation of possible programs. \nIn particular, Campus Saint-Jean wishes to determine: \n The health needs of Francophones; \n The characteristics of the work; \n The size of the job market; \n The size of the target market; \n The nature of the competition; \n A description of similar programs in the Western provinces and elsewhere in Canada; \n Demographic data; \n Trends; \n Educational needs in this area; \n Job prospects; \n Areas of application and opportunities for future graduates; \n Prospects for clinical internships; \n Accreditation and certification requirements; \n Francophone prospects in a minority setting. \nThe project includes a full market analysis, based on the following approach: \n Analysis of the health needs of Francophones \n Analysis of the job market \n Study of the competition from other educational institutions across Canada \n Interpretation of the results of the survey of employers  \nMARKET STUDY: UNIVERSITY AND COLLEGE PROGRAMS IN HEALTH  17  \nFINAL REPORT – MARCH 2016 \nThe recommendations presented in the conclusion aim to help Campus Saint-Jean develop and implement \nprograms, as applicable. \n1.2 METHODOLOGY  \nThere are three distinct elements to the methodology used in carrying out this study, namely demographic \nresearch regarding Francophones in Alberta, consultation with the province’s Francophone communities, \nand a market study for eight distinct college and university programs. \nFirst, Axion consulted Francophone associations and organizations to obtain recent demographic data \nregarding Francophones in Alberta. The information needed was related, among other things, to: \n The characteristics of the Francophone population of Alberta according to the 2011 National \nHousehold Survey; \n A portrait of Francophone immigration in Alberta; \n The health problems of certain communities with a larger concentration of Francophones. \nThis information was analyzed in order to present a demographic profile of the Francophone population of \nthe province. \nThe second part of this study aimed to consult Francophone communities in the province in order to \nidentify their workforce needs in the health sector. A series of consultations were organized in eight \ncommunities in the south, centre, northeast and northwest of the province. The meetings aimed at \nobtaining more information regarding the health services that should be available as a priority in order to \nbetter serve the province's Francophone communities. \nWith the information obtained, Axion was able to target eight educational programs for which targeted \nmarket studies were conducted. \nEach of the market studies was conducted using the same methodology.  \nAxion conducted a survey of online research from: \n Several major stakeholders in the targeted sectors, such as Alberta Health and national and \nprovincial professional associations; \n Colleges in Alberta and Canada; \n Various levels of government. \nThe studies included four sections: \n A presentation of sectoral data; \n A presentation and analysis of data from interviews with key people; \n Conditions for access to the profession; \n The conclusion and recommendations. \nThe presentation of sectoral data, using documentation, is divided into four subsections. We consider the \nprofessional profiles, we examine the general characteristics of the field, we analyze labour market trends \nand current and future areas of application, and we study qualitative and quantitative data regarding the \nworkforce in Ontario. \nThe second component includes an analysis of the results of a survey of 24 individuals, employers and \nrepresentatives from professional associations in the eight chosen disciplines. The individuals in question \nMARKET STUDY: UNIVERSITY AND COLLEGE PROGRAMS IN HEALTH  18 \n FINAL REPORT – MARCH 2016 \nwere selected jointly by Axion and Campus Saint-Jean. This qualitative and non-scientific survey focused \non the potential for development in the field. Axion contacted about fifty people throughout Alberta by \ntelephone and by email. The questionnaire is in Appendix 1 and the list of people who responded to the \nsurvey is in Appendix 2. \nIn addition to the individuals previously identified, Axion tried several times to contact officials from the \nHuman Resource departments of various health institutions (hospitals, long-term care centres, etc.). \nThe third component deals with conditions for accessing the profession in Alberta and educational \nprograms offered at colleges and universities in Alberta and elsewhere in Canada.  \nEach market study ends with recommendations to Campus Saint-Jean regarding the creation of an \neducational program.  \n2. PRESENTATION OF THE DEMOGRAPHIC PROFILE  \n2.1 FRANCOPHONE POPULATION IN ALBERTA  \nAccording to the 2011 National Household Survey, Francophones represent 2.1% of the total population of \nAlberta, or 74,075 residents whose first official language spoken is French. 1 In Alberta, the portrait of the \nFrancophone community varies largely depending on the criterion used, namely that of mother tongue \n(MT) or first official language spoken (FOLS), primarily regarding immigrants. In fact, French is not the \nmother tongue of a lot of immigrants, for whom French is their FOLS and who speak French as a second or \nthird language.  \nThe FOLS criterion is increasingly used in other provinces, such as Ontario, to present a portrait of the \nminority Francophone community. \nAlthough data from 2011 must be treated with caution, it is nonetheless interesting to note that, between \n2006 and 2011, the population of Alberta for whom French was the FOLS increased by 10.6%, from 67,000 \nto 74,074 people. In 10 years (2001-2011), the population of Alberta with French as their FOLS increased by \n19.5%, or 12,070 people. 2 The other provinces, with the exception of Ontario and British Columbia, saw a \ndecrease in the number of people with French as their FOLS during the same period. 3  \nThe sharp increase in the number of people with French as their FOLS in Alberta is explained in large part \nby the arrival of recent immigrants whose FOLS was French. In fact, three of four new residents of Alberta \n1 Brynaert Brennan and Associates. Immigration francophone en Alberta, Portrait statistique. November 2014. p. 12. \n2 Consortium national de formation en santé. Qui sont les francophones? In \nfact, 27.8% of Francophones were 55 or older in 2011, compared to 21% of the population as a whole.  \nA second element that must be considered is that, according to the 2006 census by Statistics Canada, 80% \nof Francophone seniors aged 65 and over were in four census divisions. There were 42% in the Edmonton \nmetropolitan area, 22% in the greater Calgary area, 8% in the northeast, including Bonnyville and Saint-\nPaul, where one in four seniors was Francophone in 2006, and 8% in the northwest, in the villages of Falher \nand Girouxville, where four of five seniors were Francophones.8  \nThis situation could have an impact on the demand for health services over the short, medium and long \nterms. In effect, several Canadian and international studies show a sharp decline in the second language \nlearned (in this case English) among seniors after their retirement. Moreover, studies clearly show that, in \nstressful situations, seniors tend to turn to their mother tongue. Finally, seniors with a cognitive disability \nsuch as dementia gradually lose their ability to communicate in the second language learned.9  \nAnother characteristic of the Francophone population in Alberta is the sharp rise in the number of \nFrancophone immigrants who arrived in the province between 2001 and 2011. In fact, according to the \nNational Household Survey, 22% of Francophones (FOLS) in Alberta are immigrants, or 16,230 people. \nOne quarter of them (4,215) arrived between 2001 and 2006 and just under one third (5,040) chose to live \nin Alberta between 2006 and 2011. It must be noted that 41% of very recent immigrants indicated only \nFrench as their FOLS.10  \nIn general, immigrants who arrive in Canada are in relatively good health due to selection processes and \ndisease screening policies. However, several studies, including the one published by the Canadian Medical \nAssociation Journal in 2011, note that the health of immigrants tends to deteriorate. That deterioration \nmay be the result of several factors: lifestyle (stress, sedentariness), diet (difficulty finding food in the \ncountry of origin), culture and language (language barrier), or poor knowledge of the health care system \n(lack of a family doctor, or poor knowledge of methods for preventing illness, such as preventive tests, \nvaccines and screening).11  \n2.3 HEALTH PROFILE OF COMMUNITIES WITH A GREATER CONCENTRATION OF FRANCOPHONES  \nOver the last few years, the Government of Alberta has developed a series of community profiles that \nprovide demographic and socioeconomic data, as well as data on the health of communities in the \nprovince. The province was subdivided into five large health services zones (North, Central, South, Calgary \nand Edmonton). Those zones were then subdivided into smaller areas, referred to as Local Geographic \nAreas (LGA). The purpose of the profiles is to make it easier to plan health services in the 132 LGAs \nestablished by Alberta Health Services. One of the socioeconomic indicators is related to the language \nspoken at home, but unfortunately, it only refers to non-official languages. However, some of that data \ncan be used to provide a portrait of communities with the greatest concentration of Francophones. \n8 Freychet, Yannick. Portrait des 50 + franco-albertains. The difficult economic situation that currently prevails in \nNorthern Alberta could have an impact on the health of the population, in addition to causing an exodus of \nresidents.  \n2.3.1 NORTH ZONE  \nThe obesity rate in the North is much higher than the provincial average, according to data from 2013 \n(27.4% for the North vs. 19.3% for Alberta as a whole). Moreover, the proportion of sedentary people is \nslightly higher than the province as a whole (43.8% for the North vs. 42.6% for Alberta).12  \nNorthwest region \nIn the Northwest, the local geographic areas with a concentration of Francophones are Grand Prairie (3%), \nPeace River (5.7%) and Falher (46.5%). Alberta Health Services grouped Girouxville, with 52.6% \nFrancophones, and McLennan, with 14.2% Francophones, in the geographic region of Falher and \nSt-Isidore, along with the area of Peace River. \nIn the Falher region, which includes Girouxville and McLennan, the population decreased by 10% between \n1994 and 2014 and is older than the provincial population. In fact, 16.7% of the population in the Falher \nregion are over the age of 65, while that figure is 11.3% for the province.  \nMoreover, proportionally more families live below the after-tax low income cut-off (14.1% for the region \nvs. 10.7% for Alberta). Some facts worth mentioning regarding the health of the population in that \nregion:13  \n The birth rate among young girls is nearly double that of the province (35.8 vs. 17.4 births per 1,000 \nwomen). \n The death rate per 100,000 residents is higher in the region (596.2 vs. 452.2 for Alberta). \nCardiovascular diseases were the main cause of death reported between 2004 and 2013. \nThe geographic area of Peace River (which includes St-Isidore) saw a population growth of 13.2% between \n1994 and 2014, compared to an increase of 56% for the province as a whole. The proportion of youth under \nthe age of 17 (25.6%) in the area of Peace River is slightly higher than that of the province (22.2%). The \nregion has a greater proportion of Aboriginal residents (10.2% vs. 3.4% for Alberta). Some other \ninteresting facts worth mentioning:14  \n The birth rate is higher among women than for Alberta as a whole (31.2 vs. 26.8 births per 1,000 \nwomen). That rate is more than double the provincial rate among young girls (39.3 vs. 17.4 per \n1,000 young girls). \n The spread rate of sexually transmitted infections is higher in the region than in the province as a \nwhole for four of the five most common diseases. \n The mortality rate per 100,000 residents is higher in the region (564.1 vs. 452.2 for Alberta). \nCardiovascular diseases were the main cause of death reported between 2004 and 2013.  \n The number of visits to the emergency room for mental and behavioural disorders is higher in the \nregion than in the province as a whole (899.4 vs. 633.3 per 100,000 residents). \n12 Alberta Health, Community Profile. In the same vein, the proportion of sedentary people is very close to the \nprovincial percentage (42.8% vs. 42.6%).25 \n3. PRESENTATION AND ANALYSIS OF DATA FROM THE CONSULTATIONS \nIn order to identify the need for Francophone and bilingual labour to better serve Francophone \ncommunities in Alberta in terms of health care, consultations were organized in four regions (Calgary, \nEdmonton, Northeast and Northwest).  \nIn Calgary and Edmonton, Axion organized two sessions, one in the afternoon and the other in the \nevening, in order to reach seniors and the general population. In the Northeast and Northwest, we \norganized two videoconferences in the evening to reach several communities at the same time. The \nNorthwest region included participants in St-Isidore, Grande Prairie and Falher, while the consultation in \n23 Alberta Health. Community Profile. Participants indicated that \napproximately 300 health care professionals in Calgary speak French. The two groups consulted, however, \nindicated that access health care professionals who are able to provide services in French is a major issue \nfor Calgary residents. \nThe groups noted that some services are available in French in Calgary: doctors, nurse practitioners, \noccupational therapists, dietitians, physiotherapists and dentists. However, it is important to note that \nthose services are only available because there are bilingual professionals working in those fields.  \nAgain according to participants, no health care services are officially available in French in Calgary, with the \nexception of the newly established Francophone clinic. Within health care institutions, access to \nFrancophone professionals is random, as their number is quite limited. Translation services are also \navailable for those who have difficulty or who request such services.  \nWhen establishing priorities regarding health care professionals, Francophones in Calgary targeted certain \nprofessions, indicated in the table below.  \nMARKET STUDY: UNIVERSITY AND COLLEGE PROGRAMS IN HEALTH  30 \n FINAL REPORT – MARCH 2016 \nMARKET STUDY: UNIVERSITY AND COLLEGE PROGRAMS IN HEALTH  31  \nFINAL REPORT – MARCH 2016 \nTable 6. There are professionals who \nspeak French, but they are hard to find. Moreover, some do not dare publicize that they speak French for \nfear of increasing their workload. That said, participants indicated that a lot of progress has been made in \nthe last 20 years.  \nThe situation for new residents is very difficult, however, particularly those who do not understand English. \nThe problem of identifying Francophone professionals is the same in Edmonton and in Calgary. Over the \nlast few years, a health navigator service has been implemented to accompany new residents and offer \nthem interpretation services, as needed. \nWithin health care institutions, patients who are unable to communicate in English have difficulty \nexplaining their health condition, which could have repercussions on their safety. The situation is the same \nfor home care, which is governed by Alberta Health Services. Contracts are awarded to private companies \nthat do not see language as being important. \nAccording to participants, emphasis must be placed on primary care and front line personnel. PRESENTATION OF DATA FOR THE HEALTH CARE AIDE COLLEGE PROGRAM \n4.1 OCCUPATIONAL PROFILE OF THE TARGET SECTOR  \nAccording to Human Resources and Skills Development Canada, 27 health care aides are part of the “Health \nOccupations” skill type and the “Nurse aides, orderlies and patient services associates” unit group in the \nNational Occupational Classification (NOC 3413). That group is part of the larger “Assisting occupations in \nsupport of health services” group (NOC 341).  \nHealth care aides provide assistance to elderly, disabled, or chronically ill people who require short term \nassistance or ongoing support. 28  \nHealth care aides perform some or all of the following duties: 29  \n Answer calls, provide and empty bedpans, wash and clothe patients and do their toiletries.  \n Use food trays and feed patients or help them eat and choose their menu, and measure intake and \noutput as needed. \n Weigh, lift, turn or position patients and help them walk with a walker or crutches. \n Supervise series of exercises done by patients, distribute medication and reminders, care for \nsimple wounds, use respiratory equipment or provide urinary care. \n Implement therapeutic interventions for behaviour management. \n Administer suppositories and fleet enemas and assist with toileting. \n Ensure the safety of their clients and themselves. \n Communicate with clients, and observe and report on client condition.  \n4.2 GENERAL CHARACTERISTICS OF THE TARGET SECTOR  \n4.2.1 TYPE OF EMPLOYMENT  \nAccording to the data provided by Alberta Health, 90% of health care aides work in continuing care, while \n10% work in acute care. Health care aides can work at long-term care institutions or in the patient’s home. \nThe hours of work vary greatly, as some agencies offer 24-hour and weekend service. It therefore often \ninvolves shift work and weekend work. \nGiven the nature of the work (lifting, washing and dressing patients), aides may be required to lift heavy \nloads and remain standing for long periods. They must therefore be in good health and be familiar with \ntechniques for lifting patients without injuring them. \n27 Human Resources and Skills Development Canada, Nurse aides, orderlies and patient service associates unit group, \nhttp://www5.hrsdc.gc.ca/NOC/English/NOC/2011/Profile.aspx?val1=3413, site accessed in February 2016. \n28 Alberta Government, OCCInfo, Occupations and Educational Programs, http://occinfo.alis.alberta.ca/occinfopreview/info/browse-\noccupations/occupation-profile.html?id=71003293, site accessed in February 2016. \n29 Ibid. \nhttp://www5.hrsdc.gc.ca/NOC/English/NOC/2011/Profile.aspx?val1=3413\nhttp://occinfo.alis.alberta.ca/occinfopreview/info/browse-occupations/occupation-profile.html?id=71003293\nhttp://occinfo.alis.alberta.ca/occinfopreview/info/browse-occupations/occupation-profile.html?id=71003293\nMARKET STUDY: UNIVERSITY AND COLLEGE PROGRAMS IN HEALTH  36 \n FINAL REPORT – MARCH 2016 \n4.2.2 SALARY  \nAccording to the Alberta Learning Information Service site, the average salary in 2013 at the start of a \nhealth care aide’s career was $17.94 per hour and could be as high as $21.65 per hour. The average salary \nfor health care aides was $20 per hour or $31,005 per year. The average salary in the health and social \nservices industry was $30,843 per year in 2013. The average work week for health care aids was 30 hours. 30  \nChart 1. Average hourly salary31 \nThe salaries of health care aides can vary considerably depending on the employer and the employee’s \nresponsibilities and experience.  \n4.3 LABOUR MARKET TRENDS   \nAlberta \nIn Alberta, 31,083 people were working as nurse aides, orderlies and patient service associates as of \nDecember 31, 2014, making that category of profession the second largest in the number of employees in \nthe province’s health sector. 32   \nAccording to information obtained from job sites in Alberta from 2013 to 2017, job prospects for health \ncare aides are above average and this group is expected to see 3.1% growth per year until 2017. 33  It is \nforecasted that 248 health care aide positions will be created each year, in addition to job openings created \nby employment turnover, promotions or retirements. \n30 Ibid. \n31 Alberta Government, Industry Profiles, Health Care and Social Assistance Industry 2015, p. 6. \n32 Alberta Health, Health Human Resources Planning and Strategy Branch, Health Care Aides, 2016. \n33 Alberta Government, OCCinfo: Occupations and Educational Programs, http://occinfo.alis.alberta.ca/occinfopreview/info/browse-\noccupations/occupation-profile.html?id=71003293, site accessed in February 2016. \n0\n5\n10\n15\n20\n25\n30\n35\nHealth care aides Health and welfare sector All industries\nhttp://occinfo.alis.alberta.ca/occinfopreview/info/browse-occupations/occupation-profile.html?id=71003293\nhttp://occinfo.alis.alberta.ca/occinfopreview/info/browse-occupations/occupation-profile.html?id=71003293\nMARKET STUDY: UNIVERSITY AND COLLEGE PROGRAMS IN HEALTH  37  \nFINAL REPORT – MARCH 2016 \nWhen examining the forecasts, it is nonetheless important to consider the economic conditions that affect \nthe entire Alberta job market following the drop in the price of a barrel of oil. It is also important to note \nthat, in some cases, growth may be concentrated outside the large centres.  \nGiven the average age of 44 for nurse aides, orderlies and patient service associates (NOC 3413), \nretirements are expected to increase considerably over the next few years. 34  \nAccording to data from the Alberta Learning Information Service, obtained through the 2013 Alberta Wage \nand Salary Survey, 40% of employers seeking to hire health care aides had difficulty finding employees and \n12% still had positions available after 4 months. According to the data, the vacancy rate for health care \naides was at 4% in 2013.  \nAccording to the health and social services industry profile prepared by the Government of Alberta in 2015, \nthis sector is expected to continue growing at an average rate of 3% per year between 2015 and 2019. That \nrate will be greater than the average growth of the other sectors of the Alberta economy. 35  \nCanada \nDuring the period from 2013 to 2022, projections for the larger Assisting occupations in support of health \nservices group (NOC 341) that includes the Nurse aides, orderlies and patient services associates group \n(NOC 3413) show excess demand. It is expected that, across Canada, there will be 149,262 job \nopportunities resulting from expansion demand and replacement demand, while there will be 143,936 job \nseekers. \nChart 2. Projection of demand – Assisting occupations in support of health services (NOC 341). \nJob openings are projected to arise from both employment growth and retirements. The retirement rate in \nthis occupational group is expected to be similar to the national average. As with most occupational groups \nin the health sector, the aging population and the increasing need for long-term care is projected to \nincrease the demand for health services assistants. This is particularly true since these workers provide \nfront-line services to people who are hospitalized and those living in long-term care facilities. Nevertheless, \nthe number of jobs created is expected to remain lower than recorded over the previous decade (2003-\n34 Ibid. \n35 Alberta Government, Industry Profiles, Health Care and Social Assistance Industry 2015, p. 2. \n120,000\n125,000\n130,000\n135,000\n140,000\n145,000\n150,000\n2013-2022\nJob seekers\nJob opportunities\nMARKET STUDY: UNIVERSITY AND COLLEGE PROGRAMS IN HEALTH  38 \n FINAL REPORT – MARCH 2016 \n2012), mainly because of the commitment made by governments to reduce deficits, thereby requiring \nhealth care providers to develop labour-saving strategies. With regard to labour supply, there will be a \nsignificant increase in the number of school leavers in response to the high demand for workers in this \noccupation. 36  \n4.4 QUALITATIVE AND QUANTITATIVE LABOUR DATA   \nAccording to data from Alberta Health for 2014, 20% of workers in the Health care aide category work full-\ntime, while 44% work part-time and 36% are casual employees. This data is very different in Alberta \ncompared to the rest of Canada, as 71% of people in this profession were working full-time in Canada in \n2013.  \nAgain according to that data, in Alberta, women held 91% of nurse aide, orderly and patient service \nassociate positions in 2014, while that percentage was 86% for the same category across Canada.  The \nresults are shown in Graph 3. 37  \nGraph 3. Percentage of women in the profession in Alberta and in Canada \nThe profession is open to new immigrants who meet the access conditions. In 2011, 20.5% of nurse aides, \norderlies and patient service associates were immigrants, a percentage that is higher than for all \nprofessions (13.7%).38  \n4.4.1 ESSENTIAL QUALIFICATIONS FOR A FROM INTERVIEWS WITH KEY PEOPLE  \nAxion gathered qualitative data by consulting with key people working with health care aides. We spoke \nwith five people who were able to provide us with information regarding this sector people working for the \nfollowing types of employers: health centres, seniors residences, Francophone employment centres, union \nrepresentatives, and doctors. The interviews took place between February 9 and 29, 2016. The survey was \nnot scientific, due to the number of respondents. It was more qualitative and focused on the potential for \ndevelopment of a French-language or bilingual training program for health care aides.  \nDespite numerous efforts, we were unable to talk to Human Resources staff in the province’s hospitals to \nidentify their labour needs. \nAlthough some of the respondents manage Francophone or bilingual institutions, none employ health care \naides as such, as they are employees of Alberta Health Services or Covenant Health.  \n4.5.1 TRAINING OF HEALTH CARE AIDES  \nAt this time, no training programs for health care aides are offered in French in Alberta. It is therefore very \ndifficult to find Francophone personnel to serve clients of Francophone or bilingual establishments, such as \nthe Manoir Saint-Joachim or the Saint-Thomas Community Health Centre.  \nThe establishments mentioned by the respondents as offering a good training program for health care \naides were Bow Valley College and CDI College of Business, Technology and Health. \nAccording to the respondents, Francophone health care aides often come from other provinces, such as \nQuebec. Unfortunately, their English is often poor, which makes it hard for them to find work.  \n39 Alberta Government, OCCinfo: Occupations and Educational Programs, http://occinfo.alis.alberta.The \nabca/occinfopreview/info/browse-occupations/occupation-profile.html?id=71003293, site accessed in February 2016. \n40 Canadian Research Network for Care in the Community, Ontario Personal Support Workers in Home and Community Care: \nCRNCC/PSNO Survey Results, http://www.ryerson.ca/content/dam/crncc/knowledge/infocus/factsheets/InFocus-\nOntario%20PSWs%20in%20Home%20and%20Community%20Care.pdf, site accessed in February 2016. \nhttp://www.ryerson.ca/content/dam/crncc/knowledge/infocus/factsheets/InFocus-Ontario%20PSWs%20in%20Home%20and%20Community%20Care.pdf\nhttp://www.ryerson.ca/content/dam/crncc/knowledge/infocus/factsheets/InFocus-Ontario%20PSWs%20in%20Home%20and%20Community%20Care.pdf\nMARKET STUDY: UNIVERSITY AND COLLEGE PROGRAMS IN HEALTH  40 \n FINAL REPORT – MARCH 2016 \n 4.5.2 FUTURE DEMAND  \nAll respondents believe that there will be a moderate expansion of the job market for graduates from a \nhealth care aide program over the next five to seven years. Although health agencies that hire these \nprofessionals are not seeking bilingual personnel, the demand for health care aides will continue to \nincrease, as health professionals are ageing and retiring and the population is continuing to age.  \nBilingual health care aides are an asset for employers, even though the employers are not necessarily \naware of that. As well, it is very difficult to find Francophone or bilingual health care aides. According to \none doctor questioned, bilingual health care aides must be trained so they can be available to serve the \ngrowing Francophone community. \nAccording to one respondent, there is interest in a French-language or bilingual health care aide program \namong new Francophone residents looking for work.  \nRespondents indicated that the greatest demand for health care aides would be from Alberta Health \nServices, retirement homes, or community organizations. The seniors sector is certainly the one in which \nthere will be the most demand for these professionals. One respondent indicated that Covenant Health \noften posts openings for bilingual health care aides in order to fill positions in long-term care institutions.  \n 4.5.3 PREFERRED TYPE OF TRAINING  \nAxion wanted to know the type of training that respondents would prefer for a health care aides program. \nRespondents did not seem to prefer one option over another. Some mentioned that a full-time program \nwould better meet the needs of students and their needs, while one respondent indicated a preference for \npart time, while ensuring that the program is paired with English courses.  \nConcerning the type of training (distance or on campus) that would best meet the needs and students and \nemployers, respondents indicated that a combination of distance training and on-campus training would \nmake the program more interesting for Francophones across the province.  \nThe respondents believe that it is important for such a program to offer cooperative internship \nopportunities that would thus allow students to obtain experience with local employers.  \nMARKET STUDY: UNIVERSITY AND COLLEGE PROGRAMS IN HEALTH  41  \nFINAL REPORT – MARCH 2016 \n 4.5.4 ADDITIONAL COMMENTS FROM RESPONDENTS  \nA few respondents made comments and suggestions for a potential program to best meet the needs of \nstudents and of the job market. The following points summarize those observations: \n It is essential to be able to count on Francophone or bilingual health care aides. \n Priority emphasis must be placed on health care close to patients. \n The importance of knowledge of English cannot be neglected. \n Internship experience in Alberta is very important for new residents. \n Marketing is needed in French-language high schools to promote careers in health care in French. \n There is little government desire to offer French-language services. \n4.6 CONDITIONS FOR ACCESS TO THE PROFESSION  \n 4.6.1 REGULATOR BODY  \nThe profession of health care aide is not regulated by a professional order in Alberta, and there is little data \nfor planning human resource needs. Alberta Health has nonetheless developed a competency profile for \nhealth care aides in order to standardize the teaching of this program in the various educational \ninstitutions.  \nAlberta Health is also working to develop a Health Care Aide Directory. That directory will provide \ninformation on the profession and on attaining the core competencies needed to train a qualified and \ncompetent workforce. The site was unveiled in Fall 2015 and should be functional by Spring 2016. 41  \nThis profession is also not regulated elsewhere in Canada and no certificate is issued by a professional \norder, unlike many other health care professions. Educational institutions issue a certificate to attest the \nstudent’s success, but there is no final program examination.  \n4.6.2 TRAINING AND COMPETITION  \nIn Alberta, the training needed to become a health care aide varies greatly from one employer to another. \nSeveral private and public institutions offer a postsecondary program for health care aides. Some \nemployers offer an on-the-job training program in partnership with postsecondary institutions, thus \nallowing employees to obtain their diploma while working. \nSome employers also require that their staff complete a first aid program and food security training. \nIn 2001, Alberta Health established a competency profile for health care aides. An updated competency \nprofile should be released in 2016. Alberta Health is also responsible for the health care aide provincial \ncurriculum. That curriculum was developed in 2005 and revised in 2010.  \nAt this time, the program is offered by a large number of private institutions, community colleges and \nemployers certified by the province. Some programs are offered full-time or part-time, while others are \noffered as distance education or offered partly in the regions. The full-time programs are offered over a \nperiod of 15 to 27 weeks. \n41 Alberta Health Care Aide Directory, About the Alberta Health Care Aide Directory, http://albertahcadirectory.com/, site access in \nFebruary 2016.  \nhttp://albertahcadirectory.com/\nMARKET STUDY: UNIVERSITY AND COLLEGE PROGRAMS IN HEALTH  42 \n FINAL REPORT – MARCH 2016 \nThe following table presents the programs offered in English in Alberta in this field, as well as those offered \nin French in Western Canada.  \nTable 12.
