In 2011, 13.8% of registered \nnurses were immigrants, the same percentage as for all professions (13.7).121   \n9.4.1 ESSENTIAL QUALIFICATIONS FOR A NURSE PRACTITIONER   \nThe following qualifications and skills are needed to become a nurse practitioner:122  \n Excellent verbal and written communication \n Excellent problem-solving and critical thinking skills \n Excellent organizational skills \n Good judgment and the ability to make decisions quickly in difficult situations \n Patience, understanding and a caring attitude \n Flexibility and enthusiasm \n The ability to lead and work co-operatively with others \n Good health \n9.5 PRESENTATION OF DATA FROM INTERVIEWS WITH KEY PEOPLE  \nAxion gathered qualitative data by consulting with key people working with nurse practitioners. We spoke \nwith seven people who were able to provide us with information regarding this sector – people working for \nthe following types of employers: retirement homes, primary care networks, Francophone clinics, early \nchildhood organizations, bilingual health services organizations, Francophone employment centres and \nthe Nurse Practitioner Association of Alberta. The interviews took place between February 9 and 29, 2016. \nThe survey was not scientific, due to the number of respondents. It was more qualitative and focused on \nthe potential for development of a French-language or bilingual training program for nurse practitioners.  \nDespite numerous efforts, we were unable to talk to human resources staff in the province’s hospitals to \nidentify their labour needs. \n9.5.1 TRAINING OF NURSE PRACTITIONERS  \nAt this time, no training programs for nurse practitioners are offered in French in Alberta. It is therefore \nvery hard to find Francophone personnel to serve Francophone clients. Two respondents hired a total of 11 \npart-time nurse practitioners in the last five years. All professionals hired had taken a nurse practitioner \nprogram in English in Alberta. The institutions mentioned most often were Athabasca University, the \nUniversity of Lethbridge, the University of Alberta, the University of Calgary, and Grande Prairie College.  \n 9.5.2 FUTURE DEMAND  \n120 Ibid. \n121 Ibid. \n122 Alberta Government. OCCinfo: Occupations and Educational Programs. \nhttp://occinfo.alis.alberta.ca/occinfopreview/info/browse-occupations/occupation-profile.html?id=71017912. Site accessed in \nMarch 2016. \nhttp://occinfo.alis.alberta.ca/occinfopreview/info/browse-occupations/occupation-profile.html?id=71017912\nMARKET STUDY: UNIVERSITY AND COLLEGE PROGRAMS IN HEALTH  92 \n FINAL REPORT – MARCH 2016 \nMost respondents believe that there will be a sharp or moderate expansion of the job market for graduates \nfrom a nurse practitioner program over the next five to seven years. One respondent felt that there would \nonly be low expansion in this area because, in his opinion, the province does not support the use of this \ntype of professional. Moreover, according to that respondent, 75% of nurse practitioners in the province do \nnot do the work for which they were trained. According to one respondent, there is currently no demand \nfor bilingual nurse practitioners and no job offers mention bilingualism as a hiring criterion. \nRespondents indicated that most demand for nurse practitioners would be from hospitals and long-term \ncare centres.  \nFour of the seven respondents also indicated their interest in hiring a total of 11.5 full-time nurse \npractitioners and 1 part-time nurse practitioner in the next three years.  \n 9.5.3 PREFERRED TYPE OF TRAINING  \nAxion wanted to know the type of training that respondents would prefer for a nurse practitioner program. \nRespondents did not seem to prefer one option over another. A few respondents believe that the program \nshould be offered full-time and part-time.  \nConcerning the type of training (distance or on campus) that would best meet the needs and students and \nemployers, almost all respondents indicated that a combination of distance training and on-campus \ntraining would make the program more interesting for Francophones across the province. That type of \nprogram would be more financially accessible for many potential students. It would also encourage \ngraduates to remain in their community.  \nAll respondents believe that it is important for such a program to offer workplace internship opportunities. \nStudents would thus better understand their work and would develop a good network of contacts, which \ncould help them find their first job. Four respondents stated that their organization could take on an intern \nfrom a nurse practitioner program at any time of the year.  \n 9.5.4 ADDITIONAL COMMENTS FROM RESPONDENTS  \nA few respondents made comments and suggestions for a potential program to best meet the needs of \nstudents and of the job market. The following points summarize those observations: \n It would be important to offer internships in Francophone or bilingual workplaces. \n Marketing is needed in French-language high schools to promote careers in health care in French. \n At this time, there is no need to increase internal capacity in French for nurse practitioners, as \nthere is little demand from clients.  \n There is little government desire to offer French-language services. Bilingualism is not a criterion at \nthis time when hiring health care professionals. \n9.6 CONDITIONS FOR ACCESS TO THE PROFESSION  \n 9.6.1 REGULATOR BODY  \nMARKET STUDY: UNIVERSITY AND COLLEGE PROGRAMS IN HEALTH  93  \nFINAL REPORT – MARCH 2016 \nThe following is required in order to work as a nurse practitioner in Alberta: 123  \n Hold accreditation as a registered nurse from Alberta or from another province or territory \n Have successfully completed a program approved by the Nurse Practitioner Association of Alberta \nor a similar program recognized elsewhere in Canada \n Have passed the Alberta nurse practitioner accreditation exam in one of the three selected areas \n(family/all ages; adult; or pediatric, with neonatal specialty) \nNurse practitioners who obtained their diploma in another province can be authorized to work in Alberta in \ntheir field of expertise. That recognition does not apply to nurse practitioners from Quebec, who must be \nevaluated individually. \n 9.6.2 TRAINING AND COMPETITION  \nAt this time in Alberta, only three universities offer a nurse practitioner program in English.  \nThe following table presents the programs offered in Alberta in this field.  \nTable 18. Colleges offering training programs for nurse practitioners  \nINSTITUTION TRAINING DURATION TYPE OF PROGRAM \nPrograms offered in English \nAthabasca University \n(Athabasca) \nMaster of Nursing – Nurse \nPractitioner \n(Family All Ages) \n2 years Master’s \nUniversity of Alberta \n(Edmonton) \nMaster of Nursing – Nurse \nPractitioner \n(Adult or Family All Ages) \n2 years Master’s \nUniversity of Calgary \n(Calgary) \nMaster of Nursing – Nurse \nPractitioner \n(Adult Health Acute Care) \n2 years Master’s \nNo French-language or bilingual institutions offer such a program in French in Western Canada.  \n9.7 CONCLUSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS  \nAs of December 31, 2015, there were 491 nurse practitioners in Alberta. However, this occupational group \ngrew by 52.6% between 2009 and 2014.  \n123 Alberta Health, Nurse Practitioners, 2016. \nMARKET STUDY: UNIVERSITY AND COLLEGE PROGRAMS IN HEALTH  94 \n FINAL REPORT – MARCH 2016 \nAccording to the professional nurse practitioners associations, the lack of funding makes it difficult to hire \nnurse practitioners in primary care. Other barriers cited by the professional associations are federal and \nprovincial laws. \nAs mentioned previously, the Francophone population of Alberta is older than the province’s population. In \nfact, 27.8% of Francophones were 55 or older in 2011, compared to 21% of the population as a whole. \nSeveral Canadian and international studies show a sharp decline in the second language learned (in this \ncase English) among seniors after their retirement. As well, seniors with a cognitive disability such as \ndementia gradually lose their ability to communicate in the second language learned. \nMost respondents believe that there will be a sharp or moderate expansion of the job market for graduates \nfrom a nurse practitioner program over the next five to seven years. One respondent felt that there would \nonly be low expansion in this area because, in his opinion, the province does not support the use of this \ntype of professional. Moreover, according to that respondent, 75% of nurse practitioners in the province do \nnot do the work for which they were trained.  \nRespondents indicated that most demand for nurse practitioners would be from hospitals and long-term \ncare centres. Four of the seven respondents also indicated their interest in hiring a total of 11.5 full-time \nnurse practitioners and 1 part-time nurse practitioner in the next three years.  \nThe occupation of nurse practitioner in Alberta requires certification as a registered nurse in Alberta or \nanother province or territory, successful completion of a program approved by the Nurse Practitioner \nAssociation of Alberta or a similar program recognized elsewhere in Canada and a passing mark on the \nAlberta nurse practitioner accreditation exam. \nAt this time in Alberta, only three universities offer a nurse practitioner program in English. No \nFrench-language or bilingual institutions offer such a program in French in Western Canada.  \nIt is difficult to predict the growth in this sector due to the small number of nurse practitioners within the \nlarger category of registered nurses. As well, graduates have difficulty working in their field of expertise \nand there is a lack of provincial funding for hiring this type of professional. Moreover, the sampling of \nemployers interviewed is limited and Axion was unable to obtain information from hospital human \nresource departments. We must also consider the interest shown in this type of profession among the \npeople interviewed (possible creation of 11.5 nurse practitioner positions over three years) and the lack of \ntraining programs in French in Alberta and Western Canada. Therefore, Axion recommends that Campus \nSaint-Jean study the market further before making a decision to create a French or bilingual nurse \npractitioner program. \nMARKET STUDY: UNIVERSITY AND COLLEGE PROGRAMS IN HEALTH  95  \nFINAL REPORT – MARCH 2016 \n10.  PRESENTATION OF DATA FOR THE PSYCHOLOGIST UNIVERSITY PROGRAM \n10.1 OCCUPATIONAL PROFILE OF THE TARGET SECTOR  \nAccording to Human Resources and Skills Development Canada,124 psychologists are part of the major \ngroup “Occupations in Social Science, Education, Government Service and Religion” in the National \nOccupational Classification (NOC 41) and the “Psychologists” unit group (NOC 4151). That group is part of \nthe larger “Psychologists, Social Workers, Counsellors, Clergy and Probation Officers” group (NOC 415).  \nPsychologists assess and diagnose behavioural, emotional and cognitive disorders, counsel clients, provide \ntherapy and research and apply theory relating to behaviour and mental processes. Psychologists help \nclients work toward the maintenance and enhancement of physical, intellectual, emotional, social and \ninterpersonal functioning.125   \nPsychologists carry out some or all of the following duties: 126  \n Examine and assess behaviour, diagnose behavioural, emotional and cognitive disorders, counsel \nclients and provide therapy. \n Help clients deal with an illness or physical disorder. \n Provide counsel to individuals and groups to help them develop and better adapt personally, \nsocially and professionally, and offer mediation services. \n Administer standard psychological tests for assessment purposes. \n Develop and assess intervention programs. \n Apply psychological theories and principles regarding behaviour and mental processes, such as \nlearning, memory, perception, and language development. \n Develop hypotheses and experimental plans, review documentation, conduct studies and publish \nresearch reports, educational texts and articles. \n Give at conferences, workshops and symposiums. \n Provide consulting services to government agencies. \nPsychologists specialize in applied psychology or experimental research. Sub-specialties include \nbehavioural psychology, child psychology, cognitive, educational, school or industrial psychology, social \npsychology, and sports psychology.127  \n124 Service Canada. Psychologists. http://www.servicecanada.gc.ca/eng/qc/job_futures/statistics/4151.shtml. They work in mental health clinics, correctional institutions, hospitals, \nprivate practices, rehabilitation centres, schools, post-secondary institutions, and businesses. \nClinical psychologists work in various locations and are required to travel to visit their clients (e.g. in a \nschool environment). Their work schedules can vary based on their clients’ needs. They can work regular \nhours or work evenings and weekends. \nAccording to the 2011 National Household Survey, in Canada, 69.5% of psychologists work full-time, \ncompared to an average of 81% for all professions.128   \n10.2.2 SALARY  \nAccording to the Alberta Learning Information Service site, the average salary in 2013 at the start of a \npsychologist’s career was $44.48 per hour and could be as high as $54.95 per hour. The average salary for \npsychologists was $54.03 per hour, or $90,799 per year. The average salary in the health and social services \nindustry was $30,843 per year in 2013. Over the past few years, the number of psychologists \nhas risen sharply. This rise is primarily attributable to the public's much greater sensitivity to social needs: \naging population, mental health, stress, personal growth, home support, dropping out of school, \nbehavioural problems, pathological gambling, homelessness, violence, suicide, exclusion, etc. As this trend \nof sharp increase should be mitigated because of government cuts, it is expected that the number of \npsychologists will increase over the coming years, but at a slower pace than it did in the past.134 \nOpenings will arise mainly from the need to replace psychologists who will be retiring, but also from \nemployment increase. The proportion of psychologists aged 55 and over in 2011 was clearly higher than \nthat of all occupations (29% compared with 18%, according to National Household Survey data). Turnover \nis low. Combined with the appropriate training, experience in this occupation can lead to promotions to \nmanagement positions and can open the way to positions in many occupations: family mediator, teacher, \nguidance counsellor, employment counsellor, management consultant, specialist in human resources, etc. \n10.4 QUALITATIVE AND QUANTITATIVE LABOUR DATA   \nAccording to data from Alberta Health for 2015, women held 74.2% of psychologist positions in 2015, while \nthat figure was 44.8% for all professions in Alberta.135  The percentage of women in this profession is \npractically the same as in Canada as a whole (74.5% according to the 2011 National Household Survey).136  \nChart 21. We spoke with \nseven people who were able to provide us with information regarding this sector – people working for the \nfollowing types of employers: school boards, school associations, community health centres, Francophone \nclinics, early childhood organizations, provincial unions and the provincial association of psychologists. The \ninterviews took place between February 9 and 29, 2016. The survey was not scientific, due to the number \nof respondents. It was more qualitative and focused on the potential for development of a French-\nlanguage or bilingual training program for psychologists.  \nDespite numerous efforts, we were unable to talk to human resources staff in the province’s hospitals to \nidentify their labour needs. \n10.5.1 TRAINING FOR PSYCHOLOGISTS  \nAt this time, no training programs for psychologists are offered in French in Alberta. It is therefore very \nhard to find Francophone personnel to serve Francophone clients. Four respondents hired a total of 12 full-\ntime psychologists and 1 part-time psychologist in the last 5 years. Four of the 12 psychologists were \nbilingual. All of the bilingual professionals hired were from Quebec or Francophone countries. The Alberta \ninstitutions most often mentioned for training for psychologists were the University of Alberta, Athabasca \nUniversity and the University of Calgary.  \nAccording to a few respondents, it is very hard to find Francophone psychologists and even harder to hire \nFrancophone psychologists who also understand and speak English.  \n 10.5.2 FUTURE DEMAND  \n137 Ibid. \n138 Ibid. \n139 Alberta Government. OCCinfo: Occupations and Educational Programs. \nhttp://occinfo.alis.alberta.ca/occinfopreview/info/browse-occupations/occupation-profile.html?id=71002221. Site accessed in \nMarch 2016. \nhttp://occinfo.alis.alberta.ca/occinfopreview/info/browse-occupations/occupation-profile.html?id=71002221\nMARKET STUDY: UNIVERSITY AND COLLEGE PROGRAMS IN HEALTH  100 \n FINAL REPORT – MARCH 2016 \nEveryone who answered this question believes that there will be a sharp or moderate expansion of the job \nmarket for graduates from a psychology program over the next five to seven years. According to the \nrepresentative from the Psychologists’ Association of Alberta, the province has the largest number of \npsychologists per capita in North America. Despite that, the profession is still expanding in the province, as \npsychologists work in several fields.  \nIn the educational sector, the demand for psychologists could increase with the coming application a new \ninclusive education framework. There is also increased social awareness of mental health needs. Finally, \ngiven the difficult economic situation in Alberta, respondents see even more need to rely on psychologists, \nwho can listen to and help families who are experiencing difficulties.  \nThe respondents indicated that the greatest demand for psychologists will be from private practices, \nschools, mental health centres, hospitals and crisis lines.  \nFour of the seven respondents also indicated their interest in hiring a total of nine full-time psychologists in \nthe next three years. A few respondents indicated that there is an urgent need for bilingual psychologists \nto work in French-language schools. The representative from the Psychologists’ Association of Alberta \nindicated receiving frequent requests for Francophone psychologists.  \n 10.5.3 PREFERRED TYPE OF TRAINING  \nAxion wanted to know the type of training that respondents would prefer for a psychology program. \nRespondents did not seem to prefer one option over another. A few respondents believe that the program \nshould be offered full-time and part-time to meet the needs of a greater number of students. According to \nthe representative from the provincial association, a part-time program would better meet the needs of \nstudents who work full-time.  \nConcerning the type of training (distance or on campus) that would best meet the needs and students and \nemployers, almost all respondents indicated that a combination of distance training and on-campus \ntraining would make the program more interesting for Francophones across the province. It would also \nencourage graduates to remain in their community. \nAll of the respondents believe that it is important for such a program to offer workplace internship \nopportunities. However, it can be difficult to find internships in Francophone environments. Four \nrespondents indicated that their organization could take on an intern form a psychology program at any \ntime of the year.  \n 10.5.4 ADDITIONAL COMMENTS FROM RESPONDENTS  \nA few respondents made comments and suggestions for a potential program to best meet the needs of \nstudents and of the job market. The following points summarize those observations: \n It would be important to offer internships in Francophone or bilingual workplaces. \n Marketing is needed in French-language high schools to promote careers in health care in French. \n Graduates must be fully bilingual. \n Students must be available to travel in their work. \n Graduates must understand the reality of social inclusion in order to meet the needs of youth. \nMARKET STUDY: UNIVERSITY AND COLLEGE PROGRAMS IN HEALTH  101  \nFINAL REPORT – MARCH 2016 \n The importance of working as a team and collaborating must be taught. \n Knowledge is needed of the reality of life in a minority language situation. \n Justification for a French-language psychology program could encourage the government to agree \nto create a university program in that field. \n There is little government desire to offer French-language services. Bilingualism is not a criterion at \nthis time when hiring health care professionals. \n10.6 CONDITIONS FOR ACCESS TO THE PROFESSION  \n 10.6.1 REGULATOR BODY  \nThe occupation of psychologist in Alberta requires accreditation from the College of Alberta Psychologists \n(CAP). The following is required for such accreditation: 140  \n A master’s degree in psychology that meets CAP academic criteria. \n At least 1,600 hours of work under the supervision of an accredited psychologist recognized by the \nCAP. \n Passing a clinical or theoretical psychology examination approved by the CAP. \nPsychologists who received their diploma in another province can be authorized to work in Alberta if the \nskills required and the responsibilities are similar in both jurisdictions. \n 10.6.2 TRAINING AND COMPETITION  \nAlberta and Quebec are the only provinces where a psychologist can practice the profession with a \nmaster’s degree and not a doctorate. According to the representative from the Psychologists’ Association \nof Alberta, Quebec and Alberta offer a practical program that focusses less on research. At this time, six \nuniversities offer a master’s program in psychology. Four are Alberta universities, while the other two are \nforeign universities that offer a master’s program in the province.  \nThe following table presents the programs offered in Alberta in this field.  \nTable 19. This group of professionals grew by \n13.8% between 2009 and 2014. As of December 31, 2015, the average age of psychologists was 49, and \n54.6% were 50 or younger. Just over 45% of psychologists (1,192) will be of retirement age in the next five \nyears.  \nAccording to data from the Alberta Learning Information Service, obtained through the 2013 Alberta Wage \nand Salary Survey, 38% of employers seeking to hire psychologists had difficulty finding employees and \n9% still had positions available after 4 months. The vacancy rate for psychologist positions was at 3% in \n2013. \nAccording to the health and social services industry profile prepared by the Government of Alberta in 2015, \nthis sector is expected to continue growing at an average rate of 3% per year between 2015 and 2019. That \nrate will be greater than the average growth of the other sectors of the Alberta economy.  \nMARKET STUDY: UNIVERSITY AND COLLEGE PROGRAMS IN HEALTH  103  \nFINAL REPORT – MARCH 2016 \nAs mentioned previously, the Francophone population of Alberta is older than the province’s population. In \nfact, 27.8% of Francophones were 55 or older in 2011, compared to 21% of the population as a whole. \nSeveral Canadian and international studies show a sharp decline in the second language learned (in this \ncase English) among seniors after their retirement. As well, seniors with a cognitive disability such as \ndementia gradually lose their ability to communicate in the second language learned. \nAnother characteristic of the Francophone population in Alberta is the sharp rise in the number of \nFrancophone immigrants who arrived in the province between 2001 and 2011. It must be noted that 41% of \nvery recent immigrants have indicated only French as their FOLS.  \nAll respondents believe that there will be a sharp or moderate expansion of the job market for graduates \nfrom a psychology program over the next five to seven years. The respondents indicated that the greatest \ndemand for psychologists will be from private practices, schools, mental health centres, hospitals and crisis \nlines. Four of the seven respondents also indicated their interest in hiring a total of nine full-time \npsychologists in the next three years. The representative from the Psychologists’ Association of Alberta \nindicated receiving frequent requests for Francophone psychologists.  \nThe occupation of psychologist in Alberta requires accreditation from the College of Alberta Psychologists \n(CAP). To obtain accreditation, the person must have a master’s degree in psychology that meets CAP \nacademic criteria, have worked at least 1,600 hours under the supervision of an accredited psychologist \nrecognized by CAP, and have passed an exam in clinical or theoretical psychology approved by CAP.  \nAlberta and Quebec are the only provinces where a psychologist can practice the profession with a \nmaster’s degree and not a doctorate. At this time, six universities offer a master’s program in psychology. \nFour are Alberta universities, while the other two are foreign universities. No French-language or bilingual \ninstitutions offer such a program in French in Western Canada.  \nGiven the promising forecasts regarding the number of psychologist positions that will be created in the \nprovince over the next few years, the large number of psychologists who will be of retirement age in the \nnext five years, the high percentage of Francophone seniors in Alberta (primarily in Edmonton and the \nnortheast and northwest of the province), the high percentage of new immigrants who speak only French \nas their first official language and who have recently arrived in large centres such as Calgary and \nEdmonton, the difficulty finding Francophone psychologists at this time, and the lack of training programs \nin French in Alberta and Western Canada, Axion recommends that Campus Saint-Jean create a master \nof psychology program. \nOnce again, the people consulted indicated their interest in the offer of a hybrid program (distance and on-\ncampus) in order to allow Francophones from across Alberta to take the training.  \nMARKET STUDY: UNIVERSITY AND COLLEGE PROGRAMS IN HEALTH  104 \n FINAL REPORT – MARCH 2016 \n11.  PRESENTATION OF DATA FOR THE DIETITIAN UNIVERSITY PROGRAM \n11.1 OCCUPATIONAL PROFILE OF THE TARGET SECTOR  \nAccording to Human Resources and Skills Development Canada,141 dietitians are part of the “Health \noccupations” competency group and the “Dietitians and nutritionists” unit group in the National \nOccupational Classification (NOC 3132). That group is part of the larger “Pharmacists, dietitians and \nnutritionists” group (NOC 313).  \nDietitians and nutritionists plan, implement and oversee nutrition and food service programs. They plan and \nassess the nutritional content of means, manage food service systems, promote food security, and conduct \nresearch.142  \nDietitians carry out some or all of the following duties:143 \n Develop, administer and supervise nutrition and food preparation and service programs in \nhospitals, nursing homes, schools company cafeterias or similar settings.  \n Evaluate the nutritional status of individuals and communities and provide nutrition guidance and \nconsulting to health professionals, individuals, dietetic interns, community groups, government \nand the media.   \n Aid in the prevention and/or treatment of inadequate nutrition.  \n Plan, evaluate and conduct nutrition education programs and develop educational materials for \nvarious audiences.  \n Analyze current scientific nutritional studies, conduct research and evaluate program effectiveness \nto improve the nutritional value, taste, appearance and preparation of food. \nDietitians and nutritionists may specialize in areas such as administrative dietetics, clinical dietetics, \ncommunity dietetics, public health nutrition or research dietetics.144  \n11.2 GENERAL CHARACTERISTICS OF THE TARGET SECTOR  \n11.2.1 TYPE OF EMPLOYMENT  \n141 Statistics Canada. Workplace of dietitians145 \nWorking conditions for dietitians can vary considerably depending on the type of position they hold. In \ngeneral, they work during the week, but may also be required to work evenings or weekends.  \nAccording to Alberta Health, 54% of dietitians in Alberta work full-time.146   \n11.2.2 SALARY  \nAccording to the Alberta Learning Information Service site, the average salary in 2013 at the start of a \ndietitian’s career was $35.39 per hour and could be as high as $46.60 per hour. The average salary for \ndietitians was $43.19 per hour or $73,064 per year. The average salary in the health and social services \nindustry was $30,843 per year in 2013. The average work week for dietitians was 32.5 hours.147  \nChart 23. Average hourly salary148 \n145 Alberta Health. This group of professionals grew by 23.8% \nbetween 2009 and 2014. As of December 31, 2015, the average age of dietitians was 39, making it a very \nyoung profession; 80.9% of dietitians were 50 or younger. Demographic breakdown of dietitians in Alberta149 \nThe Alberta Learning Information Service has no statistics regarding the predicted growth rate for the \nprofession over the next few years.  \nAccording to the health and social services industry profile prepared by the Government of Alberta in 2015, \nthis sector is expected to continue growing at an average rate of 3% per year between 2015 and 2019. That \nrate will be greater than the average growth of the other sectors of the Alberta economy.150  \n149 Alberta Health, Health Human Resources Planning and Strategy Branch, Psychologists, 2016. \n43.19 \n29.51 28.12 \n0.00\n10.00\n20.00\n30.00\n40.00\n50.00\nDietitians Health and welfare\nsector\nAll industries\n348 \n380 \n239 \n169 \n55 \n9 \n12 \n15 \n8 \n8 \n0 \n0 \nAge 30 and under\nAge 31 to 40\nAge 41 to 50\nAge 51 to 60\nAge 61 to 65\nAge 65 and over\nWomen Men\nMARKET STUDY: UNIVERSITY AND COLLEGE PROGRAMS IN HEALTH  107  \nFINAL REPORT – MARCH 2016 \nCanada \nIn Canada, job prospects in this profession are good. In recent years, the number of dietitians and \nnutritionists has increased sharply. This increase may be explained by the aging of the population, growing \npublic concern about healthy eating, increased government spending in the health sector, the \nexacerbation of health problems due to obesity and the growing use of the services of dieticians to prevent \nand treat health problems. Given that most of these trends should continue, the number of dietitians and \nnutritionists should increase sharply over the next few years. Government budget cuts could, however, \nmitigate the extent of the increase.151  \nOpenings will arise mainly from employment increase and, to a lesser degree, from positions left vacant by \ndietitians and nutritionists who are retiring or leaving the occupation to become managers. \nThese openings will be accessible mainly to university graduates. In fact, few experienced unemployed \ndietitians and nutritionists will apply for these vacant positions, since the unemployment rate is very low in \nthis occupation. \n11.4 QUALITATIVE AND QUANTITATIVE LABOUR DATA   \nAccording to data from Alberta Health for 2015, women held 96.9% of dietitian positions, while that figure \nwas 44.8% for all professions in Alberta.152  \nThe percentage of women in this profession is practically the same as in Canada as a whole (95.6% \naccording to the 2011 National Household Survey).153  \nChart 25. In 2011, 10.5% of dietitians \nwere immigrants, a percentage that is slightly lower than for all professions (13.7%).155  \n154 Ibid. \n155 Ibid. \nMARKET STUDY: UNIVERSITY AND COLLEGE PROGRAMS IN HEALTH  109  \nFINAL REPORT – MARCH 2016 \n11.4.1 ESSENTIAL QUALIFICATIONS FOR A PSYCHOLOGIST   \nThe following qualifications and skills are needed to become a dietitian:156  \n An interest in science and foods \n Good verbal communication \n Innovation and flexibility \n Honesty, ethics and caring \n Good critical thinking \n Self-assurance, sincerity and diplomacy \n Practicality and focus on results \nDietitians must also enjoy coordinating and developing educational programs, analyzing information, \nsolving problems and working with people. \n11.5 PRESENTATION OF DATA FROM INTERVIEWS WITH KEY PEOPLE  \nAxion gathered qualitative data by consulting with key people working with dietitians. We spoke with six \npeople who were able to provide us with information regarding this sector – people working for the \nfollowing types of employers: retirement homes, Francophone clinic, primary care network, mental health \norganizations, provincial unions and the provincial association of dietitians. The interviews took place \nbetween February 9 and 29, 2016. The survey was not scientific, due to the number of respondents. It was \nmore qualitative and focused on the potential for development of a French-language training program for \ndietitians.  \nDespite numerous efforts, we were unable to talk to human resources staff in the province’s hospitals to \nidentify their labour needs. \n11.5.1 TRAINING FOR DIETITIANS  \nAt this time, no training programs for dietitians are offered in French in Alberta. It is therefore very hard to \nfind Francophone personnel to serve Francophone clients. Two respondents hired a total of 11 full-time \ndietitians in the last five years. The primary care network alone hired 10 of the 11 dietitians, one of whom \nwas bilingual. Almost all of the dietitians were trained at the University of Alberta, where the program is in \nEnglish. The Alberta institutions most often mentioned for training for dietitians were the University of \nAlberta and Athabasca University.  \nAccording to one respondent, it is difficult to find Francophone dietitians, as the pool of Francophone \ngraduates is smaller.  \n 11.5.2 FUTURE DEMAND  \nAlmost all respondents believe that there will be a sharp or moderate expansion of the job market for \ngraduates from a dietitian program over the next five to seven years.  \n156 Alberta Government. OCCinfo: Occupations and Educational Programs. \nhttp://occinfo.alis.alberta.ca/occinfopreview/info/browse-occupations/occupation-profile.html?id=71002221. Site accessed in \nMarch 2016. \nhttp://occinfo.alis.alberta.ca/occinfopreview/info/browse-occupations/occupation-profile.html?id=71002221\nMARKET STUDY: UNIVERSITY AND COLLEGE PROGRAMS IN HEALTH  110 \n FINAL REPORT – MARCH 2016 \nThe representative from the provincial association of dietitians estimates that there will be strong \nexpansion in demand for dietitians due to the ageing population and the increase in chronic illnesses \nassociated with ageing. Only one respondent believes that demand will remain stable over the next five to \nseven years due to economic conditions and slowing population growth.  \nRespondents indicated that the greatest demand for dietitians will be from primary care networks, \nhospitals, retirement homes, mental health centres, long-term care facilities, home care programs, private \npractices, and community health centres.  \n 11.5.3 PREFERRED TYPE OF TRAINING  \nAxion wanted to know the type of training that respondents would prefer for a dietitian program. \nRespondents did not seem to prefer one option over another. A few respondents believe that the program \nshould be offered full-time and part-time to meet the needs of a greater number of students. According to \nthe representative from the provincial association, a part-time program would better meet the needs of \nstudents who work full-time, particularly new residents, for whom it is important to be able to study while \nworking.  \nConcerning the type of training (distance or on campus) that would best meet the needs and students and \nemployers, almost all respondents indicated that a combination of distance training and on-campus \ntraining would make the program more interesting for Francophones across the province. It would also \nencourage graduates to remain in their community.  \nAccording to the representative from the provincial association, the program cannot be offered completely \nthrough distance learning, as students must also have practical training on-campus.  \nAll respondents believe that it is important for such a program to offer workplace internship opportunities. \nHowever, it can be difficult to find internships in a Francophone environment. One respondent indicated \nthat his organization could take on an intern dietitian at any time of the year.  \n 11.5.4 ADDITIONAL COMMENTS FROM RESPONDENTS  \nA few respondents made comments and suggestions for a potential program to best meet the needs of \nstudents and of the job market. The following points summarize those observations: \n It would be important to offer internships in Francophone or bilingual workplaces. \n Marketing is needed in French-language high schools to promote careers in health care in French. \n Graduates must be fully bilingual. \n Future graduates must be available to travel in their work. \n There is no part-time dietitian program outside Edmonton, and that is a problem at this time. \n At this time, there is no need to increase internal capacity in French for dietitians, as there is very \nlittle demand from clients.  \n There is little government desire to offer French-language services. Bilingualism is not a criterion at \nthis time when hiring health care professionals. \nMARKET STUDY: UNIVERSITY AND COLLEGE PROGRAMS IN HEALTH  111  \nFINAL REPORT – MARCH 2016 \n11.6 CONDITIONS FOR ACCESS TO THE PROFESSION  \n 11.6.1 REGULATOR BODY  \nTo work as a dietitian in Alberta, accreditation is required from the College of Dietitians of Alberta (CDA). \nThe following is required for such accreditation:157  \n A bachelor’s or master’s degree in food science or nutrition approved by the CDA  \n Have completed an internship of 35 to 40 weeks in an accredited Canadian internship program, \nunder the supervision of an accredited dietitian \n Have passed the Canadian Dietetic Registration Examination \nDietitians who received their diploma in another province can be authorized to work in Alberta if the skills \nrequired and the responsibilities are similar in both jurisdictions. \n 11.6.2 TRAINING AND COMPETITION  \nIn Alberta, only the University of Alberta offers a Nutrition and Food Sciences program accredited by the \nCDA. It is also the only university to offer an integrated internship program recognized by the CDA. \nStudents access that program through a competitive process in their third year of university studies. The \ninternship is a prerequisite to work as an accredited dietitian in the province.  \nThe following table presents the program offered in Alberta in this field.  \nTable 20. University offering a training program for dietitians  \nINSTITUTION TRAINING \nNUMBER OF \nYEARS  TYPE OF PROGRAM \nProgram offered in English \nUniversity of Alberta \n(Edmonton) \nScience in Nutrition and \nFood Sciences \n4 year Bachelor \nNo French-language or bilingual institutions offer such a program in French in Western Canada.  \n11.7 CONCLUSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS  \nAs of December 31, 2015, there were 1,238 dietitians in Alberta. This group of professionals grew by 23.8% \nbetween 2009 and 2014. As of December 31, 2015, the average age of dietitians was 39, making it a very \nyoung profession; 80.9% of dietitians were 50 or younger.  \nThe Alberta Learning Information Service has no statistics regarding the predicted growth rate for the \nprofession over the next few years. However, according to the health and social services industry profile \nprepared by the Government of Alberta in 2015, this sector is expected to continue growing at an average \n157 College of Dietitians of Alberta. Registration Requirements. \nhttp://www.collegeofdietitians.ab.ca/BecomeanRD/RegistrationRequirements.aspx. Site accessed in March 2016. \nhttp://www.collegeofdietitians.ab.ca/BecomeanRD/RegistrationRequirements.aspx\nMARKET STUDY: UNIVERSITY AND COLLEGE PROGRAMS IN HEALTH  112 \n FINAL REPORT – MARCH 2016 \nrate of 3% per year between 2015 and 2019. That rate will be greater than the average growth of the other \nsectors of the Alberta economy.  \nAs mentioned previously, the Francophone population of Alberta is older than the province’s population. In \nfact, 27.8% of Francophones were 55 or older in 2011, compared to 21% of the population as a whole. As \nwell, a greater proportion of seniors live in Edmonton and the Northeast and Northwest parts of the \nprovince.  \nThe percentage of obese people in the North is much higher than the provincial average, according to data \nfrom 2013 (27.4% for the North vs. 19.3% for Alberta as a whole).  \nSeveral Canadian and international studies show a sharp decline in the second language learned (in this \ncase English) among seniors after their retirement. As well, seniors with a cognitive disability such as \ndementia gradually lose their ability to communicate in the second language learned. \nAnother characteristic of the Francophone population in Alberta is the sharp rise in the number of \nFrancophone immigrants who arrived in the province between 2001 and 2011. It must be noted that 41% of \nvery recent immigrants have indicated only French as their FOLS.  \nAlmost all respondents believe that there will be a sharp or moderate expansion of the job market for \ngraduates from a dietitian program over the next five to seven years.  \nRespondents indicated that the greatest demand for dietitians will be from primary care networks, \nhospitals, retirement homes, mental health centres, long-term care facilities, home care programs, private \npractices, and community health centres.  \nTo work as a dietitian in Alberta, accreditation is required from the College of Dietitians of Alberta (CDA). \nTo obtain accreditation, the person must have a bachelor or master’s degree in food sciences or nutrition \napproved by the CDA, have completed an internship of 35 to 40 weeks in an accredited Canadian \ninternship program, under the supervision of an accredited dietitian, and have passed the Canadian \nDietetic Registration Examination. \nIn Alberta, only the University of Alberta offers a Nutrition and Food Sciences program accredited by the \nCDA. It is also the only university to offer an integrated internship program recognized by the CDA. No \ninstitutions offer such a program in French in the province or in Western Canada.  \nGiven the growth seen in this profession between 2009 and 2014 and the expansion anticipated by \nrespondents to the survey over the next few years, the increase in health problems due to obesity and the \nincreased use of dietitian services to prevent and treat health problems, the high percentage of \nFrancophone seniors in Alberta (primarily in Edmonton and the northeast and northwest of the province), \nthe high percentage of obese people in the North, the high percentage of new immigrants who speak only \nFrench as their first official language and who have recently arrived in large centres such as Calgary and \nEdmonton, the difficulty finding Francophone dietitians at this time, and the lack of training programs in \nFrench in Alberta and Western Canada, Axion recommends that Campus Saint-Jean create a bachelor of \nnutrition program. \nOnce again, the people consulted indicated their interest in the offer of a hybrid program (distance and on-\ncampus) in order to allow Francophones from across Alberta to take the training.   \nMARKET STUDY: UNIVERSITY AND COLLEGE PROGRAMS IN HEALTH  113  \nFINAL REPORT – MARCH 2016 \n13. The following table shows that nearly 21,000 students study there in French. Ove the last year, \nthere has been a 4% increase in the number of students attending such schools.  \nTable 21. Youth have a wide choice of \nprograms and institutions. Campus Saint-Jean must therefore position itself as a hub for health programs \nin French in Western and Northern Canada.  \nCONCLUSION AND GENERAL RECOMMENDATIONS \nFollowing the preparation of a demographic profile of Francophones in Alberta and the consultations \nconducted with the various Francophone communities around the province, it is clear that there is \ncurrently a lack of health professionals who are able to serve the province’s Francophone community. \nThis first finding helped identify eight areas in which Francophone or bilingual health professionals could \nhelp improve the health services offered to the province’s Francophone population.  \nAxion recommends the creation of the following programs based on the eight separate market studies \nconducted. \nTable 23.
