 DR A FT \nEXECUTIVE SUMMARY \nThe University of Alberta aims to serve “For the Public Good.” This guiding ethos, affirmed through \nthe recent completion of a university-wide strategic planning process, expresses the U of A’s \ncommitment to “inspire the human spirit through outstanding achievements in learning, discovery, \nand citizenship in a creative community, building one of the world’s great universities for the public \ngood.” \nRanked within the top 100 universities in the world, the University of Alberta is the province’s leading \neducator, generator of new ideas, and engine of social, cultural and economic prosperity. The \nuniversity’s new strategic plan, For the Public Good, articulates the central goal to provide education \nand research that is truly transformative to the respective groups it serves: students, the community, \nour province, country and the world.  \nThis driving mandate is in alignment with key Government of Alberta priorities to diversify the \nprovincial economy, lead on humanity’s grand challenges—such as climate change,  educate and \ntrain citizens to compete in the global economy, build safe and inclusive communities, and create \nhealth and social wellbeing. \nWe are a diverse and inclusive community with a far-reaching impact. \nThe multi-campus University of Alberta plays a pivotal role in Alberta’s post-secondary system, \neducating the citizens that serve in our social, industrial and corporate sectors. With over 29,000 \nundergraduate and 7,000 graduate students, over 600 post-doctoral fellows, over 2,000 full-time \nfaculty members in 18 faculties, and 15,000-plus employees, the U of A is Alberta’s fourth-largest \nemployer and has an economic impact of $12.3 billion per year in Alberta.  \nThe university prizes excellence in teaching and is first among Canadian universities with 41 3M \nTeaching Fellowship winners, Canada’s highest honour for undergraduate teaching. The U of A \nembraces community engagement. Last year, over 1,500 students participated in Community \nService-Learning with over 180 community partners.  \nWe are a leading research enterprise. \nWith over $4.6 billion in sponsored research over the last decade, U of A researchers have \nproduced numerous game-changing discoveries such as the world-famous Edmonton Protocol \ntreatment for Type 1 Diabetes; received $75 million to launch Future Energy Systems to build on the \nuniversity’s broad energy and environmental expertise to innovate towards a zero-carbon future; \ndiscovered the first life-saving antiviral therapy for hepatitis B; and attracted the largest joint \nresearch gift in U of A history—$54 million—for the Women’s and Children’s Health Research \nInstitute, which mobilizes ground-breaking research into changes in practice that improve the health \nof women and children the world over.  \nWe solve problems. \nAcross a wide range of issues, the university takes a multi-faceted approach to solving relevant \nproblems. A $14 million facility in Edmonton is building on research based in the Faculty of \nAgricultural, Life & Environmental Sciences to deploy innovative technologies that use crops to \nproduce nutraceuticals, pharmaceuticals, and cosmetics, increasing economic diversity and \ninvestment in the agricultural sector. The Alberta Cell Therapy Manufacturing facility, the only facility \nof its kind in western Canada, will produce clinical grade human cell and tissue products—offering \nhope for a host of regenerative treatments for diseases ranging from cancer to cardiovascular \nCOMPREHENSIVE INSTITUTIONAL PLAN 2017-20                                                              5 \nC OMPR E HE N S I VE  I NS T I TU T ION A L  PL A N  -  DR A FT \ndisease to neurological disorders. A breakthrough technology using feed stock—animal fat, beef \ntallow, restaurant grease—has been pioneered, allowing this waste to be converted into the next \ngeneration of renewable fuels and creating value-added products for Alberta’s economy. The U of \nA’s Protective Clothing and Equipment Research Facility tests and develops fabrics for extreme \nenvironments, such as forest fire-fighting, and the Western Partnership for Wildland Fire Science \nstudies the fires themselves—each aspect supporting the work of the other as well as the work of \nAlbertans handling real crises in the province. \nFundamentally, the university’s far-ranging work is conducted within the mandate of its role in the \nsix-sector system and embraces the Province of Alberta’s Adult Learning System Principles.  \nThe 2017 Comprehensive Institutional Plan presents the university’s priorities for the next three \nyears according to each of the principles. Highlights of these include:  \nAccessibility   \nThe U of A provides access to a full range of foundational programs, specialized professional \nprograms, and graduate programs that drive research and innovation. By ensuring the availability of \na wide array of programs within the province, we support a diversity of career aspirations and wider \neconomic objectives. It is a priority to ensure that the U of A is accessible and that the wellness of \nour community is enhanced, recognizing that all students, and particularly under-represented \ncohorts, are served by improved supports, communication, facilities, and infrastructure. The \nuniversity directly supports accessibility through a robust system of financial supports, administering \napproximately $90 million in scholarship and bursary funding.  \nA key accessibility goal is an action-oriented response to the final report of the Truth and \nReconciliation Commission of Canada, one that achieves meaningful change for Indigenous \nstudents, staff, and faculty. Through dedicated hiring, community outreach, student supports, and \npartnerships with Indigenous post-secondary institutions, we are committed to closing the long-\nstanding educational attainment gap between Indigenous and non-Indigenous people.  \nWe will also address equity through improved recruitment and hiring practices that identify and \naddress systematic biases that discourage diversity. We will increase the proportion of women, \nvisible minorities, and persons with disabilities among our faculty, staff, and students. We will launch \na university-wide wellness strategy to enhance wellbeing, safety, and security, and will continue \nimplementation of recommendations from a 2015 review of sexual assault support services.  \nAffordability   \nThe University of Alberta supports affordability through direct financial support to students—over $89 \nmillion in annual student bursaries and awards—and by ensuring the efficiency and sustainability of \nour programs, services, and infrastructure. Efficient administration and financial stewardship enable \nus to devote scarce resources to academic programming and to student services and supports, \nminimizing organizational costs that must be supported through our tuition base. With economic \ngrowth, cost pressures must be managed to align resources and needs with natural and pervasive \ninflationary effects to produce the stable development of fee structures.  \nIn the next three years, our priorities relating to affordability include: securing funding to support key \nstrategic goals; upgrading IT infrastructure to increase service reliability to the U of A community and \nto extend reach to partner institutions and isolated communities; enhancing transparency and \nconsultation within the university to promote efficiencies and evidence-based decision-making \nacross the institution; and renewing key physical infrastructure through our Capital Plan, which is \nfocused on asset renewal to drive efficient space utilization and meet program needs sustainably. \nCOMPREHENSIVE INSTITUTIONAL PLAN 2017-20                                                              6 \nC OMPR E HE N S I VE  I NS T I TU T ION A L  PL A N  -  DR A FT \nQuality   \nThe University of Alberta is committed to quality across our teaching, research, and administrative \nfunctions. Our global ranking reflects our reputation for excellence, and we remain committed to \nattracting world-class faculty and research to support the best and brightest students from Alberta, \nCanada, and the world. Our research enterprise in particular is a major driver of Alberta’s innovation \nand economic diversification for the future.  \nCentral to our quality agenda is a process to identify and support signature areas of research and \nteaching excellence. These are areas of local, national, and global relevance in which the U of A is, \nor is emerging as, a world leader with a critical mass of scholars and students. Identifying signature \nareas will enable us to focus resources strategically to maximize our impact and our success \npursuing major funding opportunities. To ensure the long-term sustainability of our professoriate, we \nare committed to developing a faculty renewal program, with an initial focus on increasing our \nproportion of Assistant Professors and achieving equity and diversity in the academy. The university \nis driving quality and innovation in teaching through initiatives such as an enhanced focus on \nexperiential learning, ensuring that our graduates are exposed to many environments and develop \nworld-ready skills.  \nCoordination   \nAs the province’s largest post-secondary institution, the U of A embraces its leadership role within a \ndifferentiated Campus Alberta system. Examples of our collaborations with Campus Alberta partners \ninclude: hosting of the Olds College PeopleSoft financial system; provision of the moodle Learning \nManagement System to four institutions; support for the Norquest College capital expansion project \nthrough provision of project management, design and operational review, business case \ndevelopment, construction delivery advisement and procurement assistance; and, through TEC \nEdmonton, collaboration with NAIT to share infrastructure in supporting commercialization. \nIn the next three years, specific goals include strengthening relationships with Indigenous post-\nsecondaries in Alberta, with rural and remote learners through IT supports, and with Alberta Health \nServices to enhance health-related teaching, learning, and continuous education opportunities within \nboth organizations.  \nAs a community of over 50,000 individuals, we also promote and embrace coordination across our \nfive campuses, 18 faculties, and multiple administrative units. This is a key driver for our \nadministrative and information technology goals over the next three years.  \nAccountability   \nThe University of Alberta is responsible and accountable to the Government of Alberta and other \ninvestors for our funding. As a top 100 global university, the University of Alberta is also accountable \nto researchers, scholars, teachers, industries and other organizations to fulfill its mandate to \ncontinuously generate new knowledge and new scientific discoveries. From dinosaurs to islet \ntransplants to ease the burden of Type 1 diabetes; to the University Hospital’s strength as a centre \nfor organ transplants and cardiac care; from bitumen extraction from the oilsands to the finest live \ntheatre acting program in Canada; communities and organizations look to the university for \ninnovation, discoveries, and for talented graduates who will carry those organizations forward.  \nIn the next three years, the U of A will focus on both fiscal and social accountability. To drive long-\nterm stewardship, we are undertaking to revise our budget model and to continue to diversify our \nfunding sources and donor base. Socially, we are committed to changing the university environment \nto be more supportive of Indigenous people and new Canadians, and to contribute to the necessary \ndevelopment of economic and social diversity in Edmonton and in Alberta.  \nCOMPREHENSIVE INSTITUTIONAL PLAN 2017-20                                                              7 \nC OMPR E HE N S I VE  I NS T I TU T ION A L  PL A N  -  DR A FT \nACCOUNTABILITY STATEMENT \nThis Comprehensive Institutional Plan was prepared under the Board’s direction in accordance with \nlegislation and associated ministerial guidelines, and in consideration of all policy decisions and \nmaterial, economic, or fiscal implications of which the Board is aware. \nMichael Phair \nChair, Board of Governors \nCOMPREHENSIVE INSTITUTIONAL PLAN 2017-20                                                              8 \nC OMPR E HE N S I VE  I NS T I TU T ION A L  PL A N  -  DR A FT \nINSTITUTIONAL CONTEXT \nUniversity of Alberta Mandate \nCreated by the University Act, 1906, of the Legislative Assembly of the Province of Alberta, the \nUniversity of Alberta is a board-governed, publicly-funded, multi-campus university that operates as \na Comprehensive Academic and Research Institution under the authority of Alberta’s Post-\nSecondary Learning Act. Its fundamental mandate is to offer a broad range of outstanding learning \nand research programs to prepare citizens and leaders who will make a difference. The university \nplays a leading role in Campus Alberta through collaboration with other Alberta institutions, \nresponding to vital community relationships at every level and giving a national and international \nvoice to Alberta innovation. Its activities enhance the range and quality of student opportunities, and \nbuild Alberta’s capacity for long-term, knowledge-driven, sustainable development at the global \nforefront. \nThe university provides instructional excellence through both on-campus and distance delivery in a \nvibrant and supportive learning and research environment. Its residential, multi-campus setting \nincludes many research and field facilities. The university community discovers, disseminates, and \napplies new knowledge through interrelated core activities. \nIn a dynamic and integrated learning and research environment, the University of Alberta offers \ngraduate and undergraduate students the opportunity to earn internationally respected credentials, \nincluding bachelor’s, master’s and doctoral degrees, and university certificates and diplomas. It also \noffers French-language programs leading to university degrees, certificates, and diplomas, as well \nas college certificates and diplomas. A number of its programs are unique within Alberta and \nwestern Canada. Post-doctoral fellows come to the university to refine their teaching, mentoring, and \nresearch skills. \nThe University of Alberta is a balanced multi-campus academy, with strong arts and sciences \nprograms featuring the Faculties of Agricultural, Life and Environmental Sciences, Arts, Augustana, \nExtension, Native Studies, Physical Education and Recreation, Science, and Campus Saint-Jean. \nThese faculties are foundational to and interlinked with the university’s network of strong \nprofessional faculties, including Business, Education, Engineering, Graduate Studies and Research, \nLaw, Medicine and Dentistry, Nursing, Pharmacy and Pharmaceutical Sciences, Public Health, and \nRehabilitation Medicine. In addition, all of our faculties are involved in professional development and \ncontinuing education. \nThe university establishes and maintains an environment of inquiry-based learning anchored in \nstrong academic programming and an array of co-curricular student life opportunities. Academic \nsupport, social and community enrichment, health and wellness, and career and life development \nare cornerstones of the University of Alberta student experience. The intellectual and creative \ndiversity of the campus, including its international and multi-cultural population and exchange \nprograms, makes for an engaging student experience. Fine arts displays, stage performances, \nmuseum collections, athletics, and recreational opportunities combine with residence life to present \nmulti-dimensional possibilities. Experiential learning opportunities based in the community augment \non-campus activities with real-life applications. \nTransfer and collaborative degree completion agreements with partner institutions broaden student \nopportunities and provide rural, northern, and Indigenous communities with access to University of \nAlberta programs. Similar innovative arrangements centred at the university deliver information and \nCOMPREHENSIVE INSTITUTIONAL PLAN 2017-20                                                              9 \nC OMPR E HE N S I VE  I NS T I TU T ION A L  PL A N  -  DR A FT \nknowledge resources to post-secondary and government communities through our Libraries and \nhighly-trained librarians using inter-library loans and online access. \nThe university’s research and creative activities produce a dual impact through the preparation of \nhighly- qualified graduates and a continuous flow of innovation. The university attracts scholars of \ninternational reputation: undergraduate and graduate students, post-doctoral fellows, staff, and \nfaculty. Collectively, they foster, conduct, and disseminate research and creative activity, both pure \nand applied, within and across all the major program areas at an internationally recognized level of \nexcellence.  \nUniversity faculties, centres, and institutes combine resources and talents for collaborative \nadvantage through research partnerships with other academic institutions, business, governments, \nand public agencies. The Centre for Entrepreneurship and Family Enterprise is dedicated to \nunderstanding family businesses—often the seeds economic development. The university actively \ntransfers new knowledge and creative works to Alberta, Canada, and the world for community \nbenefit, including commercial development of intellectual property when appropriate and feasible. \nResearch and innovation are core contributors to Alberta’s economic growth and sustainability. \nIn every aspect of its mandate, the University of Alberta partners with the province of Alberta in \nsocial, cultural, and economic development, fostering and establishing the provincial, national, and \ninternational connections and understanding that support leading global enterprise and citizenship \nfor Albertans. University administrators, faculty, staff, and students contribute regularly to public \ndebate and to government and corporate examination of issues. Start-up companies and new \ntechnologies licensed to existing companies lead Alberta in new directions and employ graduates. \nThe university continually moves out into its communities through its graduates, its creative and \nresearch advances, and its ongoing opportunities for experiential and lifelong learning. \nAs approved by the Minister of Advanced Education and Technology, July 2009 \nUniversity of Alberta Mission Statement  \nWithin a vibrant and supportive learning environment, the University of Alberta discovers, \ndisseminates, and applies new knowledge for the benefit of society through teaching and learning, \nresearch and creative activity, community involvement, and partnerships. The University of Alberta \ngives a national and international voice to innovation in our province, taking a lead role in placing \nCanada at the global forefront.  \nFor the Public Good, approved June 2016 \nCOMPREHENSIVE INSTITUTIONAL PLAN 2017-20                                                              10 \nC OMPR E HE N S I VE  I NS T I TU T ION A L  PL A N  -  DR A FT \n CONSULTATION \nThis Comprehensive Institutional Plan (CIP) builds upon, and derives its goals from, the recently-\nadopted Institutional Strategic Plan, For the Public Good, which was unanimously approved by the \nGeneral Faculties Council and the Board of Governors in June of 2016. For the Public Good was \ncommunity-developed, drawing on the experiences, perspectives, and leadership of our students, \nfaculty and staff, and on the many communities and institutions with which the university fosters \nproductive relationships. \nThe consultation process on For the Public Good was extensive, and included the following steps:  \n● The formation of an Institutional Strategic Plan Advisory Committee with broad \nrepresentation from across the university. \n● Pre-planning consultations with all faculty councils and with senior administration that \nresulted in the development of a discussion paper that supported institutional discussions \nabout our approach to the new plan. \n● The discussion paper supported the next round of consultations, including campus forums, \nroundtable meetings, meetings with student associations, meetings with faculty councils, \nconsultation with university committees, the Senate, the Alumni Council and the Board of \nGovernors, and the collection of online and email submissions. Based on these \nconsultations, a first draft was developed. \n● Consultation on the draft plan included a new round of campus forums, roundtables, \nmeetings with student associations, meetings with faculty councils, consultation with \nuniversity committees, the Senate, the Alumni Council and the Board of Governors.  \n● Regular updates to the university community, including summaries of the consultation, were \nprovided through the Institutional Strategic Plan website.  \n● In the end, more than 65 meetings with stakeholders and members of the university \ncommunity were held, engaging a combined total of more than 1,800 people. More than 125 \nonline and email responses were received. \n● A separate consultation process with administrative and governance committees took place \nin fall 2016 on institutional indicators for measuring our progress on For the Public Good. \nThese performance measures were approved in early 2017. \nThe goals and priority initiatives described in this CIP reflect the input received over many months of \nconsultation on the Institutional Strategic Plan in 2015-16. These goals and initiatives directly \nsupport Alberta’s Adult Learning System Principles: ensuring the accessibility of a wide range of \nprograms and experiences to Albertans and top students from Canada and the world; maintaining \nthe affordability of the U of A experience and the sustainability of our programming and \ninfrastructure; delivering research and teaching that has global quality, relevance, and impact; \npromoting coordination within and beyond our walls, with partners in education, community, \ngovernment, and industry; and embracing our accountability to government, to our community, and \nto a wide array of stakeholders within and beyond the academy. \nThe measures described in the CIP align with those developed as a part of the accountability \nprocess in For the Public Good, and will align with the university’s annual reporting process to the \nGovernment of Alberta. Where quantitative measures cannot be identified, a narrative update on \nprogress on the priority initiatives listed in the CIP will be provided in the Annual Report.  \nThe University of Alberta Comprehensive Institutional Plan is developed by a working group with \nrepresentation from all administrative units across the institution and is approved through a robust \ngovernance process.   \nCOMPREHENSIVE INSTITUTIONAL PLAN 2017-20                                                              11 \nC OMPR E HE N S I VE  I NS T I TU T ION A L  PL A N  -  DR A FT \nAs the university now turns its attention to the implementation of the goals and objectives articulated \nin For the Public Good, and reflected in the Comprehensive Institutional Plan, the university \ncontinues to consult with those stakeholders affected by specific strategies to ensure that the plan’s \nrealization continues to be a reflection of our diverse community. \nIn addition to the consultation process described above, the University of Alberta is in regular \ncommunication with the other Comprehensive Academic and Research Institutions (CARI) \ninstitutions, and other institutions in Alberta, to share information about our approach to the CIP. The \nuniversity has also been in regular contact with officials from the Ministry of Advanced Education to \nensure full understanding of the CIP Guidelines and to ensure that the CIP includes the information \nexpected from the ministry.  \nCOMPREHENSIVE INSTITUTIONAL PLAN 2017-20                                                              12 \nC OMPR E HE N S I VE  I NS T I TU T ION A L  PL A N  -  DR A FT \nGOALS, PRIORITY INITIATIVES, AND \nEXPECTED OUTCOMES  \nOverview \nFor the Public Good affirms our vision to inspire the human spirit through outstanding achievements \nin learning, discovery, and citizenship, motivated by the University of Alberta’s 109-year history of \nleadership and public service.  \nThe goals described in For the Public Good directly support Alberta’s Adult Learning System \nPrinciples, and position us to lead on the accessibility, affordability, quality, coordination, and \naccountability of the post-secondary and research system as whole. \nAlberta’s Adult Learning System Principles \nAccessibility \nThe University of Alberta is committed to accessibility for Albertans, Canadians, and citizens of the \nworld. As a Comprehensive Academic and Research Institution (CARI), the U of A plays a critical \nrole in Alberta’s post-secondary system by providing access to a full range of foundational programs, \nspecialized programs in the professions (e.g., Law, Medicine, and Engineering), and graduate \nprograms that support Alberta’s research and innovation needs across faculties. Our multi-campus \nenvironment provides access to diverse experiences, ranging from our downtown Enterprise Square \nto rurally-based Augustana to the francophone Campus Saint-Jean, unique in western Canada. \nThrough its breadth of offerings, the U of A provides students at all stages with access to a variety of \nacademic and career pathways, and our internationally renowned quality positions our graduates to \nmake an impact for the benefit of all Albertans. Accessibility is supported by almost $90 million in \nscholarship and bursary funding per year. \nOver the next three years, the U of A will prioritize increasing the diversity of the student body by \nreaching out to under-represented communities of learners—including Indigenous, rural, and \nfrancophone students—and diversifying our international student population without decreasing \naccess for Albertans. It is also critical to ensure that our academic and non-academic workforce \nbegins to reflect the makeup of the population we serve and benefits from a rich diversity of cultures, \nexperiences, and ideas. Accordingly, we aim to increase the proportion of women professors, \nIndigenous professors, visible minorities, Indigenous peoples, and persons with disabilities among \nour faculty, staff, and students by reviewing recruitment and hiring practices to eliminate barriers to \ndiversity and encourage under-represented groups, such as Indigenous young men. \nThe university is committed to responding to the Calls to Action of the Truth and Reconciliation \nCommission of Canada with concrete initiatives that contribute to closing the educational gap \nbetween Indigenous and non-Indigenous people and improving relationships with Indigenous \npeoples and communities. The development of the Maskwa House of Learning as a place for \nunderstanding and dialogue between Indigenous and non-Indigenous members of our community is \ncentral to this commitment.  \nOur accessibility goals are supported by a commitment to the health and wellness of our students, \nfaculty, and staff. The key to great programs is great people offering and participating in them. The \nCOMPREHENSIVE INSTITUTIONAL PLAN 2017-20                                                              13 \nC OMPR E HE N S I VE  I NS T I TU T ION A L  PL A N  -  DR A FT \nuniversity is launching an institution-wide Healthy University Strategy, and continues to improve \nservices for victims of sexual assault in response to a comprehensive review conducted in 2015.  \nAffordability \nIn addition to its robust systems for student financial aid, the university supports affordability by \nensuring the efficiency and sustainability of our programs, administrative systems, and \ninfrastructure. By ensuring efficient and optimal performance across all areas, we are able to \ncontinue to offer programs and educational experiences of international quality while preserving \naffordability and accessibility. Sustainability should ensure that programs, including specialty \nprograms, remain in reach of the students desiring them, and that cost drivers and market conditions \nare constantly monitored to ensure that programs are appropriately placed to attract and support \nparticipation across economic circumstances. \nThe specialty, professional, and research-intensive programs available at the University of Alberta \nenhance accessibility and affordability to Albertans who might otherwise have to travel further and \nlive in different economic climates to pursue careers in areas such as Law, Public Health, Business, \nEconomics, Medicine, Animal Science, and Engineering, to name a few. Note: initiatives related to \nstudent financial supports are included under Accessibility, as they directly support the university’s \ngoals in this area. \nOver the next three years, the university will particularly prioritize seeking secure funding to support \nstrategic goals, upgrading its IT infrastructure to increase the reliability of services to the university \ncommunity and to extend reach to partner institutions and isolated communities and to enhance \ncommunication clarity and effectiveness within the university and to its partners and stakeholder \ncommunities. On an ongoing basis, the university monitors the quality, relevance, and appeal of its \nprograms through regular program quality reviews and continual tracking of application pressure and \nprogram completion. \nQuality \nQuality is paramount for the University of Alberta. The university is committed to a minimum \nacceptable standard of quality in all its activities. The province must have world-class institutions of \nhigher learning to attract the best and brightest students, faculty, and staff who all equally participate \nas citizens. A high quality intellectual environment will continue to attract diverse and creative \nthinkers to the province, where they will invigorate established industries and develop economic and \nsocial diversity. Only with world-class faculty members and students in a secure, inviting, diverse, \nand innovative environment can world-class outcomes of teaching, learning, research, and \ninnovation be achieved.  \nAt the centre of our quality agenda is a process to identify and support signature areas of research \nand teaching excellence. These are areas of local, national, and global relevance in which the U of A \nis, or can become, a world leader with a critical mass of scholars and students. Identifying signature \nareas will enable us to focus resources strategically to maximize our impact and our success in \npursuing major funding opportunities. To ensure the long-term sustainability of our professoriate, we \nare also committed to developing a faculty renewal program, with an initial focus on increasing the \nproportion of Assistant Professors and supporting equity and diversity in the academy. The \nuniversity is driving quality and innovation in teaching through initiatives such as an enhanced focus \non experiential learning, ensuring that our graduates are exposed to a range of environments and \ndevelop world-ready skills outside the classroom. In the immediate future, we are developing an \ninventory of existing opportunities and online resources to make current and new opportunities and \nsupports more accessible to students.  \nCOMPREHENSIVE INSTITUTIONAL PLAN 2017-20                                                              14 \nC OMPR E HE N S I VE  I NS T I TU T ION A L  PL A N  -  DR A FT \nCoordination \nThe university’s approach to coordination is reflected in its ongoing partnerships with Campus \nAlberta institutions across the post-secondary system and its numerous and long-standing \ncollaborations with community organizations—for the public good. Our deep commitment to \nengagement, outreach, and partnership ensures that we make efficient use of our resources, \nmaximize the impact and relevance of our research, and deliver value for the communities we serve. \nAs a community of over 50,000 people, we also promote and embrace coordination across our five \ncampuses, 18 faculties, and multiple administrative units. \nOver the next three years, the U of A will continue to build capacity to connect with diverse \ncommunities across our campuses, including seeking funding and support for shared-use, \ncommunity-access infrastructure in downtown Edmonton and at South Campus. We are developing \nstrategies to ensure that we are taking full advantage of our multi-campus environment to offer \ndiverse experiences and engage local and regional stakeholders on an ongoing basis.  \nWe will continue to build relationships with Indigenous post-secondary providers in Alberta, to \nsupport rural and remote learners through IT supports, and work with Alberta Health Services to \nenhance health-related teaching, learning, and continuous education opportunities within both \norganizations.  \nAccountability \nAs an institution dedicated to the public good, the university is accountable to government and other \nfunders, as well as to the local community, our students and faculty, and to the global community of \nscholars and teachers. These accountabilities inform our commitments to the responsible \nstewardship of our resources and the transparent communication of our impact. Core priorities for \nthe 2017-20 period include revising our budget model, developing a suite of measures and tools for \nreporting on institutional performance, and continuing to build our advancement function to diversify \nour funding sources. We are also prioritizing the development of an institutional communications \nstrategy to better tell the university’s story and demonstrate its impact on communities locally, \nnationally, and abroad—this is critical to our accountability to community, and directly supports a \nbroad range of community engagement activities described above. Our commitment to \naccountability is also reflected in our active response to the Truth and Reconciliation Commission of \nCanada, as outlined in our Accessibility initiatives. \nFinancial and Budget Information \nTo support all these activities, the university must respond to existing budget pressures and \ncommitments, and manage its budget and finances frugally. For any uncommitted funds, we have \nprioritized renewing the professoriate, which entails making career-long commitments to the best \nand brightest faculty and staff who will be role models of diversity and will contribute to our research \nand scholarly missions. The budget remains subject to systemic risks that are largely outside the \ncontrol of the university, most notably the Campus Alberta Grant—the university’s largest source of \nunrestricted revenue—and revenue from tuition and fees.  \nEnrolment and Programming \nThe university is rolling out new national and international recruitment plans to ensure that we \nremain competitive for top students without compromising access for Albertans. Overall, program \nenrolment is expected to be stable over the next three years, with some small shifts between \nprograms within faculties. Several faculties have the capacity to enrol beyond current targets if \nfunded. In contrast, numerous other programs experience significant application pressure they \nCOMPREHENSIVE INSTITUTIONAL PLAN 2017-20                                                              15 \nC OMPR E HE N S I VE  I NS T I TU T ION A L  PL A N  -  DR A FT \ncannot accommodate within their current funding envelopes—these include Computer Science, \nKinesiology, Augustana B.Sc. programs, the Collaborative Nursing program, all programs in \nPharmacy (being one of only two PharmD programs in Canada), the BCom in Accounting and \nFinance and the MBA in Business, all Engineering, the Education collaborative program with Red \nDeer College, and the Aboriginal Teacher Education Program. With additional dedicated funding, \ncapacity in these programs could be increased to address unmet demand from qualified applicants.  \nResearch \nA unique feature of the CARI sector is the mandate to produce cutting-edge knowledge, \ntechnologies, and products through the research enterprise. Across a range of partnerships and \ninitiatives, the university is a major driver of Alberta’s research outcomes: a strong economy and \neffective resource management; environmental stewardship; and supporting the health of Albertans \nin all of our communities.  \nThe university is also mindful of the need to meet the Grand Challenges facing our world, and we \nwill continue to enthusiastically pursue all funding options to support world-class research and \nscholarship, including partnerships with industries, organizations, and communities to facilitate \nknowledge translation. \nCommunity outreach and underrepresented learners \nCommunity engagement is a core priority of For the Public Good. The university boasts many strong \npartnerships with community, such as the Saville Community Sports Centre on South Campus. \nProjects across the academic, research, and capital plans are heavily-focused on strengthening \nengagements with community stakeholders. Prominent examples are the South Campus Community \nArenas project, the Maskwa House of Learning, and Future Energy Systems, which will bring \ntogether researchers across scientific disciplines to examine energy and other resource use. A \nspecific plan has been developed to recruit and retain Indigenous students, thus we expect to see \nincreased participation and increased rates of degree completion and overall satisfaction with their \nuniversity experience. \nInternationalization \nThe province benefits from graduates who are aware of the world-wide best practices and \napproaches to performance, professions, technology, social and environmental management. \nStudents benefit from understanding different perspectives and the vibrancy created by diversity in \ntheir social and work groups. The university will increase its relevance to the world by its continuous \npresence on the global stage in all its activities.  \nAn International Diversity Plan has been developed for international student recruitment. \nInternationalization is created by ensuring diversity among students (maintaining a 15 per cent \noverall proportion) and offering excellent learning opportunities to international students that they \ncannot access elsewhere. These efforts reap dividends for the province with more than 80 per cent \nof international students staying in Alberta, contributing to our economy and society. \nInternationalization also entails ensuring that the professoriate includes diverse, global viewpoints, \nthat University of Alberta people visit others around the world, and that visitors come to us from far \nand wide to share and germinate ideas.  \nCapital Plan \nWe must continue to ensure infrastructure of a level and quality commensurate with our current \nglobal position and ambitious goals. Our Capital Plan emphasizes renewal and repurposing, \nCOMPREHENSIVE INSTITUTIONAL PLAN 2017-20                                                              16 \nC OMPR E HE N S I VE  I NS T I TU T ION A L  PL A N  -  DR A FT \nensuring that previous investments are respected and preventing expensive and unnecessary new \nbuilds. Renewal and repurposing keeps pace with the changing world and needs of our students, \nfaculty, and staff, while increasing sustainability and reducing our carbon footprint. Our highest \npriorities are campus-specific.  \nAt Augustana and Campus Saint-Jean, science labs and classrooms need to be renewed to ensure \nthat all students and instructors have access to the high quality facilities and learning spaces. At \nNorth Campus, the biggest campus, the top priority is the Maskwa House of Learning. Second is the \nrenewal of several key buildings including the historically significant Dentistry/Pharmacy Building. \nFinally, various upgrades are necessary to support new teaching and learning technologies not \nimagined 50 to 100 years ago when these buildings were constructed. At South Campus, a \ncontinued focus on multi-use facilities that serve the university and engage the surrounding \ncommunities are priorities, including a High Performance Training and Research Centre that will \nbring together the best athletes and the best scientists, and the South Campus Community Arenas \nproject that will contribute to the general facilities context of the province.  \nInformation Technology \nFrom an infrastructure standpoint, it is abundantly clear that Information Technology is the backbone \nof communications and knowledge translation effectiveness and capacity. In order to stay \ncompetitive and serve our mandate, the university needs to keep pace with a lightning-fast \ntechnological world, use those technologies to their maximum potential, and to constantly educate \nourselves and the university stakeholder communities about engaging with those technologies, and \nlearning and engaging with cyber security measures in this fast-paced domain. \nKey outcomes \nThe University of Alberta’s new strategic plan speaks to our commitment to ensuring that everything \nwe do—in teaching, research, outreach, and administration—offers value to the communities we \nserve.  \nThe University of Alberta will deliver key outcomes that help realize that commitment. It will deliver a \nrenewed faculty that positions us to strengthen world-class teaching excellence; drive research and \ninnovation for Alberta; ensure an academy that is more diverse and inclusive, and with a healthier \nbalance of junior and senior rank academics. It will deliver a more diverse student body that reflects \nour commitment to accessibility and inclusiveness, with a higher proportion of Indigenous students \nand a more varied international student body. It will deliver a visible commitment to reconciliation, \nreflected in student and faculty recruitment, campus services, course and program offerings, and our \ncampus environments.  \nBy the end of this CIP time frame, we will have clearly defined signature areas of research and \nteaching excellence—areas in which we make world-class contributions for the benefit of Alberta, \nCanada, and the world. We will offer expanded access to experiential learning for our students, and \ndeeper connections with community and post-secondary partners, including Indigenous post-\nsecondaries. We will be accountable stewards, reflected in our internal budget model as well as our \nstrategies for communicating our value and impact to Albertans—including through a new U of A-\nlinked news site. \nThrough the goals and initiatives outlined below, the U of A will remain the province’s leading \neducator, generator of new ideas, and a leading engine of social, cultural, and economic \nprosperity—for the public good.  \nCOMPREHENSIVE INSTITUTIONAL PLAN 2017-20                                                              17 \nC OMPR E HE N S I VE  I NS T I TU T ION A L  PL A N  - Operational efficiency is reported under Affordability (PM2) \n2018 Sustain Obj 21 \nC OMPR E HE N S I VE  I NS T I TU T ION A L  PL A N  -  DR A FT \nCOMPREHENSIVE INSTITUTIONAL PLAN 2017-20  \nAPPENDIX A: FINANCIAL AND BUDGET \nINFORMATION \n2017-18 Consolidated Budget \nThe purpose of the University of Alberta’s consolidated budget is to lay out a financial plan for \nthe coming fiscal year and to project forward for three years, the allocation of the university’s \nresources in support of its strategic plan, For the Public Good. Through the implementation of its \nstrategic plan, the university is contributing to the achievement of the ministry’s priorities of \naccessibility, affordability, quality, coordination and accountability. As a major economic driver in \nthe province, with government’s commitment of stable and predictable funding, the university will \nplay a critical role in buffering the Alberta economy from other sectors currently facing \nchallenges. \nThe U of A’s 2017-18 consolidated budget (see Table 1) reflects the entire enterprise, including \nunrestricted and restricted funds. Funding for general operations is fully unrestricted within the \nconsolidated budget. Funding for ancillary operations remains within those entities while the \nmajority of research revenues, philanthropic sources of revenue, and capital project funding are \nfully restricted. In an effort to provide full transparency of the university’s budgets, Table 2, on \npage 11, provides a breakdown of the consolidated budget by fund. \nFor 2016-17, the university is forecasting, at year-end, a consolidated excess of revenue over \nexpense of $35 million: a variance of $2.6 million below budget. Consolidated revenue is \nestimated to be $20.2 million lower than budgeted, while consolidated expense is estimated to \nbe $17.6 million lower than budgeted. The majority of the excess funds are restricted and \ntherefore can only be used for the purpose for which they have been granted. \nFor 2017-18, the consolidated budget reflects an excess of revenue over expense of $16.4 \nmillion on budgeted revenue of $1,919.1 million and budgeted expense of $1,902.7 million. The \n$16.4 million excess of revenue over expense is equal to less than one per cent of the \nuniversity’s budgeted consolidated revenue and is driven almost exclusively by excess of \nrevenue over expense within the restricted funds. For example, endowment funds and the \ncapitalized interest applicable to the endowment funds must remain intact in perpetuity and are \nunavailable for general operational spending. \nThe Statement of Operations and Expense by Function (consolidated budget) prepared under \nthe Public Sector Accounting Standards and the Statement of Cash Flows are presented in \nTables 5 and 6 found on pages 17 and 18. \nConsolidated revenue \nBudgeted revenue for 2017-18 is $1,919.1 million. As illustrated in Figure 1, 51.1 per cent or \n$980.9 million comes from the Government of Alberta, mostly through the Campus Alberta \nGrant, sponsored research funding, and capital funding. Of the $980.9 million, $628 million \nrepresents the Campus Alberta Grant, the primary source of unrestricted funding for the \nC OMPR E HE N S I VE  I NS T I TU T ION A L  PL A N  -  DR A FT \n28 \nD\nR\nA\nF\nuniversity’s day-to-day operating activity and as reflected in the operating fund (see Table 2). \nThe 2017-18 budget has been prepared based on an assumption of a two per cent increase to \nthe Campus Alberta Grant or the equivalent of $12.3 million.  \nGiven the current fiscal challenges facing the province, forecasting a two per cent increase to \nthe Campus Alberta Grant is not without its risks. Given government’s tremendous commitment \nto support higher education and the university’s importance to diversifying the provincial \neconomy, the university has factored in this increase. However, the university has also \ndeveloped budget scenarios with no change to the grant and a two per cent decrease to the \ngrant.  \nThe federal and other government revenue of $216.8 million largely reflects the funding received \nby the university in support of its research mandate, and is its third largest source of revenue. \nThis revenue is budgeted to be $33.2 million higher than the 2016-17 budget. This increase in \nresearch funding is driven primarily by the federal government’s investment in higher education \nthrough the Post-secondary Strategic Initiatives Fund (SIF) program. The balance of the \nbudgeted research funding is directly linked to the university’s overall success in securing Tri-\nCouncil research grants and grants from other organizations and entities.  \nTuition and related fees are budgeted at $329.9 million and, at 17.2 per cent, represent the \nsecond-largest source of consolidated revenue and is budgeted to be slightly higher than 2016-\n2017 budgeted revenue. Tuition and related fees include all instructional fees, market modifiers, \nprogram fee differentials, international differential fees and non-instructional fees. In October \n2016, the provincial government announced a continuation of the tuition freeze to 2017-18 for \nregulated tuition fees and Mandatory Non-Instructional Fees (MNIFs). This budget has been \nprepared assuming no backfill funding to offset the tuition freeze. In December 2016, the Board \nof Governors approved a 3.02 per cent increase to international student tuition fees as well as \nan additional increase of $4,000 to all full-time graduate programs not including cost recovery \nprograms and not including graduate based Physical Therapy, Occupational Therapy, and \nSpeech Therapy programs. This $4,000 increase will be offset by an equal amount of financial \nsupport provided to international graduate students. The Board also approved an increase in \ninternational differential fees in the Integrated Petroleum Geosciences program.  \nThrough extensive consultation with students, the university developed an agreed to Academic \nPrice Index (API). The API is a prospective calculation used in determining the increasing costs \nof the university’s operations. The API is a weighted calculation based on the major operating \nfund expenditure categories of salaries, benefits, supplies, utilities and maintenance. The \nuniversity’s API for 2017-18 was calculated at 3.02 per cent. Originally developed with students \nto determine the annual increases in MNIFs, this formula has been further applied to set \ninternational tuition increases. Unlike the Alberta Consumer Price Index (CPI), the API basket of \nservices is tied directly to the university’s primary cost drivers. The university believes that the \ndevelopment of a province-wide API could be of value as government undertakes its current \nreview of the post-secondary sector funding model and tuition fee regulation. The university has \nused the API calculation methodology to forecast future international tuition fee increases. \nHowever, as always, the university continues to closely monitor overall program costs, market \ndemand and tuition levels for international students at competing institutions to determine \nwhether additional adjustments in international tuition should be made.  \nThe fourth-largest source of revenue is sales of services and products at $206.1 million. The \nmajority of sales of services and products are generated through the university’s ancillary \noperations such as parking and the university’s housing and food service operations. Increased \nrevenue through ancillary operations is driven by forecast growth such as increased residence \ncapacity as well as rate increases for residence services and parking as approved by the Board \nC OMPR E HE N S I VE  I NS T I TU T ION A L  PL A N  -  DR A FT \n29 \nD\nR\nA\nF\nin December, 2016. However, the demand for residence spaces is starting to soften in response \nto rental market conditions, which could impact revenues from ancillary operations. \nThe other sources of consolidated revenue for 2017-18 include grants and donations of $117 \nmillion and investment income of $68.2 million. Changes in donations are directly tied to the \nuniversity’s philanthropic activities and can be influenced significantly by large single donations \nand economic conditions. It is important to note that the majority of donations are restricted and \ncan only be used for the purposes designated. Investment income is driven by the overall value \nof the university’s restricted endowments, its short-term investments as well as changes in \ninterest rates and the performance of the university’s investment portfolios. \nFigure 1. Consolidated Revenue Budget 2017-18 by source  \nConsolidated expense \nFor 2017-18, consolidated expense is budgeted at $1,902.7 million.  \nAs Figure 2 illustrates, investments in salaries and benefits to maintain teaching, research, and \nother critical activities account for over $1.1 billion or 60.0 per cent of total expense. This \nexpense factors in negotiated collective agreement salary increases to faculty and staff. In \naddition, the university must also budget for the regulated and inflationary increases to statutory \nand non-statutory benefits such as supplementary health plans. Adjustments for increased \npension plan contributions in response to unfunded pension plan liabilities have also been \nfactored in.  \nThe university’s next largest expense is materials, supplies, and services. Budgeted at $311.3 \nmillion, these expenses provide essential support across the campuses, including Information \nServices and Technology, research expenditures, library resources, and central expenditures \nsuch as insurance premiums. This expense line also includes the costs of goods sold and \nrecovered, largely within ancillary operations. In budgeting for these expense items, the \nuniversity continues to face the challenge whereby inflation within higher education as reflected \nby the Academic Price Index (API), significantly exceeds general inflationary increases as \nC OMPR E HE N S I VE  I NS T I TU T ION A L  PL A N  -  DR A FT \n30 \nD\nR\nA\nF\nrepresented by the Alberta Consumer Price Index (CPI). Reasons why the API is higher than the \nCPI include library collections, which are not only impacted by general inflationary increases but \nalso the value of the Canadian dollar relative to the US dollar, in which most library purchases \nare made. For 2017-18 the library estimates serial and monograph inflation of between 2.5 and \n6.0 per cent (on a $25 million collections budget) and an additional currency impact of $1.7 \nmillion. Information Technology is forecasting inflation increases of 6.2 per cent on contracts and \nFacilities and Operations is forecasting average inflation increases of 5.0 per cent including the \nimpact of the increased minimum wage. The university has estimated that for the 15-month \nperiod of January 1, 2017 to March 31, 2018, the carbon levy will increase the university’s utility \ncosts by a minimum of $2.5 million. When fully implemented the carbon levy will increase the \nuniversity’s utility costs by an estimated $6 million or the equivalent of a one per cent grant \nadjustment to the university’s 2017-18 Campus Alberta Grant.  \nA further significant expense in the consolidated budget is $178.5 million for the amortization of \ncapital assets. Amortization is an annual expense that is calculated based on the estimated \nuseful life of the asset. These assets include buildings, scientific and computing equipment, \nsoftware, and learning resources. \nFor 2017-18, scholarships and bursaries expenses are budgeted at $89.2 million. This \nrepresents a $2.1 million, or 2.4 per cent increase, over the 2016-17 budget and the university’s \ncontinuing commitment to invest in its students—particularly those who require financial \nassistance to attend the university. \nC OMPR E HE N S I VE  I NS T I TU T ION A L  PL A N  -  DR A FT \n31 \nD\nR\nA\nF\nFigure 2. Consolidated Expense Budget 2017-18 by type \nOperating fund investment priorities \nThe operating fund (see Table 2) is used to support the primary teaching and learning activities \nof the university. It is also within the operating fund that strategic investments are made to \nadvance For the Public Good and its five strategic goals of: Build, Experience, Excel, Engage \nand Sustain. \nThe operating fund is prepared under Public Sector Accounting Standards and must reflect \nlosses that are incurred or recoveries that are made including re-evaluations as a result of \nchanges in market conditions. The $9.3 million operating excess that is shown in the operating \nfund is the result of re-measurement of the university's unfunded benefits liability, which is driven \nprimarily by pension plans. The improving financial markets contributed to this $9.3 million re-\nevaluation gain. There remains a significant unfunded liability within the pension plans, therefore \nthe $9.3 million does not reflect cash on hand and cannot be used to invest in new expenditures \nor offset any increases in existing expenditures. \nThe consolidated budget has been prepared based on two fundamental assumptions. One, the \nuniversity will receive a two per cent increase to the Campus Alberta Grant or approximately \n$12.3 million in the operating fund. Secondly, that faculties and units will continue to be \nresponsible for funding their portion of collective agreement increases which represents \napproximately $12.0 million. Many of the faculties and units will need to reduce other \nexpenditure lines, eliminate vacancies, lay off staff or draw down their positive carry forwards in \norder to fund the costs associated with their staff. The net result is that faculties and units will \nneed to re-allocate expenditure lines within their budgets to fund salaries. \nAlthough the provincial government has committed to investing in higher education and \nproviding predicable funding, given the government’s current fiscal situation, the two per cent \ngrant increase assumption has some risk. Within the operating fund, priority areas identified for \nsupport include sustaining ongoing commitments to faculty recruitment and retention, partial \nC OMPR E HE N S I VE  I NS T I TU T ION A L  PL A N  -  DR A FT \n32 \nD\nR\nA\nF\nfunding of inflationary pressures associated with the university’s library collections, and base \nfundraising support to sustain current operations and resulting donation levels. Final allocation \ndecisions will not be made until after the provincial budget is tabled and the ministry confirms the \nadjustment to the university’s Campus Alberta Grant. \nIn the event that the university gets less than a two per cent grant increase or its grant is \nreduced, the university will need to apply budget cuts across the institution in addition to the \nimpact of the faculties and units absorbing across the board (ATB) salary settlements and merit. \nBased on a scenario where there is no change to the grant, a budget cut up to 2.2 per cent \nwould be required. This would be in addition to the average impact of 1.7 per cent across the \noperating fund as the faculties and units absorb ATB and merit, for a combined impact up to 3.9 \nper cent. A two per cent cut to the Campus Alberta Grant would have a combined impact of up \nto a 5.5 per cent cut. Every one per cent cut to the operating fund is equal to $7.5 million which \nequates to 48 faculty positions, or 106 support positions or the elimination of over 1,000 class \nsections.  \nPublic sector institutions like the University of Alberta play a critical role in the economy from \nmany different perspectives. First, the university attracts hundreds of millions of dollars from \noutside Alberta largely for research, creating high paying professional employment opportunities \nand subsequent spin-off benefits to the Alberta economy. Secondly, the university attracts \nthousands of students from around the world, further bolstering the economy. As a world-leading \nresearch institution, the university supports diversification of the economy. Finally, it is educating \ntens of thousands of students who will create further economic benefits and assist in economic \ndiversification. For these reasons and others, providing the necessary increases to the Campus \nAlberta Grant is a sound investment as the University of Alberta functions as a critical buffer to \nthe economy while other industries and sectors continue to adjust to the current economic \nenvironment.  \nConsolidated budget highlights and sensitivities \nThe university prepares its fiscal estimates and final budgets using a comprehensive integrated \nplanning and budget process, involving key stakeholders from across the institution. Key budget \nassumptions and sensitivities are cornerstones of the university’s multi-year budgeting process. \nThe goal is to achieve improved accuracy in forecasting elements of the budget and provide \ncommon assumptions for budget planners across the university. \n2017-18 budget highlights \nKey revenue highlights include: \n● A two per cent increase to the Campus Alberta Grant.  \n● Maintaining general tuition increases and MNIF at 2014-15 levels (due to government \ntuition freeze) while increasing international student tuition fees by 3.02 per cent for \n2017-18. \n● A $4,000 per full-time student increase to international graduate tuition offset by $4,000 \nin financial support (with some program exclusions). \n● Tuition increase to the Integrated Petroleum Geosciences graduate program. \n● Continuation of a low interest rate environment with endowment returns budgeted at 6.97 \nper cent. \n● Modest growth in research funding. \n● Modest growth in investment income. \n● Slight decline in capital funding. \nKey expenditure highlights include: \nC OMPR E HE N S I VE  I NS T I TU T ION A L  PL A N  -  DR A FT \n33 \nD\nR\nA\nF\n● Academic staff negotiated ATB salary increase of 1.50 per cent, with an average merit of \n1.10 per cent. \n● Support staff negotiated ATB salary increase of 0 per cent, with an average merit of 1.04 \nper cent (Note: Support staff ATB salary adjustment to be negotiated if government \nunions negotiate an increase). \n● Benefit cost increases of 3.1 per cent (excluding Fund 290). \n● All faculties and administrative units absorb negotiated salary ATB and merit. \n● General inflation on contractual obligations of 4.0 per cent. \n● Impact of carbon levy of $2.2 million ($6 million impact when fully rolled in year three). \n● Impact of minimum wage increase in contractual obligations of $1.0 million. \n2017-18 budget sensitivities \nRevenue Approximate Value \n● One per cent on Campus Alberta Grant: $6.2 million. \n● One per cent on credit tuition: $2.7 million. \n● A 0.25 per cent on short-term interest rate: $1.5 million. \nExpense Approximate Value \n● One per cent change in salary settlements (ASSUA and NASA): $6.3 million. \n● One per cent increase in benefits: approximately $1.8 million. \n● A $1/gigajoule increase in natural gas: $2.7 million. \n● One per cent operating budget reduction: $7.5 million. \nForecast budget assumptions 2018-19 to 2020-21 \nThe university has used the following forecast budget assumptions. \nRevenue assumptions: \n● The grant will increase at two per cent per year for each of 2018-19 to 2020-21. [Note: \nthe review of post-secondary funding is currently underway by government]. \n● Regulated tuition will increase by the Alberta Consumer Price Index and all other fees \nwill increases by a minimum of the Academic Price Index (approximately 3.3 per cent per \nyear). [Note: The review of the tuition fee regulation is currently underway by \ngovernment]. \n● All mandatory non-instructional fees will increase by the Academic Price Index \n(approximately 3.3 per cent). \n● Modest growth in research funding. \n● Interest income will remain at historically low levels with some modest recovery with \nendowment returns ranging from 7.0 to 7.25 per cent. \n● Stable capital funding. \nExpenditure assumptions: \n● ATB salary adjustments and merit and benefit costs will be subject to collective \nagreement negotiations (AASUA agreement expires June, 2018 and NASA agreement \nexpires March, 2019).  \n● Benefit cost increases forecast at 6.1 per cent per year predominantly related to pension \ncontributions. \n● All faculties and administrative units will continue to have responsibility for the funding of \nsalary ATB adjustments and merit. \n● All other expenditures are forecast to increase in the range of two to ten per cent. \nC OMPR E HE N S I VE  I NS T I TU T ION A L  PL A N  -  DR A FT \n34 \nD\nR\nA\nF\nTable 1. 2017-18 Consolidated Budget and Projections ($,000) \nC OMPR E HE N S I VE  I NS T I TU T ION A L  PL A N  -  DR A FT \n35 \nD\nR\nA\nF\nTable 2. 2017-18 Consolidated Budget By Fund ($,000)  \nInstitutional budget risks  \nThe current environment in which the university’s 2017-18 budget was prepared is \nunprecedented in terms of the major government policy reviews underway including the post-\nsecondary funding and tuition regulation reviews, the uncertainty around various revenue \ngeneration initiatives currently before government and the provincial government’s fiscal \nsituation. As a result, the university has identified many of its revenue and expenditure drivers at \na high risk level. The degree of uncertainty associated with core elements of the university’s \nbudget would indicate that a high level of caution should be exercised regarding current and \nlonger term budget assumptions.  \nSpecific factors affecting the university’s budget risks have been grouped in terms of systemic \nas well as specific revenue and expenditure risks for 2017-18. Systemic risks are those largely \noutside the control of the university. \nC OMPR E HE N S I VE  I NS T I TU T ION A L  PL A N  -  DR A FT \n36 \nD\nR\nA\nF\nSystemic Risks \nRevenue \n● Campus Alberta Grant. The grant continues to be the university's primary source of \nunrestricted revenue. The provincial government’s current fiscal situation, along with the \nongoing funding review, place any increase to the grant and the longer-term value of the \ngrant at a high risk.  \n● Tuition revenue. Tuition revenue is a factor of quantity, mix and price. The university \nrequires the ability to adjust enrolment numbers and mix to align with demand as well as \nadjust tuition levels in response to cost increases as well as market demand and \nconditions. This is particularly true regarding graduate and international student tuition \nfee levels. As well, if there are constraints on the Campus Alberta Grant, the university \nmust have the flexibility to generate tuition revenue to fulfill its academic mission and \nsustain the quality of the learning experience. As the tuition regulation is currently under \nreview by government, this revenue source may be at a high risk. \n● Student enrolment quantity and mix. Total tuition revenue is driven by tuition levels \nand a complex mix of student type and degree program. A broad range of external \nenvironmental or geopolitical issues can impact the university’s quantity and mix of \nstudents.  \n● Interest Rates. Interest income is an important source of revenue on the margin. With a \ncontinuation of low oil prices and the sluggish Canadian economy, there continues to be \ndownward pressure on the Bank of Canada rate. Low interest rates continue to impact \nthe ability of university to grow, on the margin, unrestricted revenue. \n● Alternative Revenue. The university must increase its capacity to generate alternative \nsources of revenue to offset constraints to grant funding, tuition revenue limitations, and \nlow investment income returns. However, the government has yet to approve the transfer \nof land to the land trust and several academic initiatives that could generate new sources \nof revenue. \n● Exchange Rates. The Canadian dollar continues to hover around $0.75 to the US dollar. \nAlthough a lower Canadian dollar benefits the provincial government in terms of oil and \ngas revenue, and reduces some international students’ cost of tuition, a declining \nCanadian dollar also increases the university's costs for many of the goods and services \nproduced in the US or purchased in US dollars. \nExpenditures \n● Compensation. The single largest expenditure for the university is employee \ncompensation. The greatest challenge faced by the university is the fact that total \ncompensation continues to increase at a faster rate than the university’s Campus Alberta \nGrant and tuition, its primary sources of operating funding.  \n● Unfunded Pension Plan Liabilities. The university is carrying a large unfunded liability \nin its pension plans. The high contribution rates and the overall level of the unfunded \nliability present significant risk to the university. \n● Academic Price Index The costs within the university (Academic Price Index) are rising \nat a faster rate than general inflation (Consumer Price Index) and the overall rate of \nincrease in the university’s operating revenues. The university’s API is also being \nnegatively impacted by the carbon levy as well as the increases to minimum wage. The \nC OMPR E HE N S I VE  I NS T I TU T ION A L  PL A N  -  DR A FT \n37 \nD\nR\nA\nF\noverall rate of increase in the API continues to put significant pressure on ability of the \nuniversity to balance its operating fund. \n● Deferred Maintenance. As the largest and oldest post-secondary institution in the \nprovince, the university’s deferred maintenance continues to increase, reaching $938 \nmillion. This level of deferred maintenance could result in a significant building system \nfailure, negatively impacting the university’s operations.  \n2017-18 Budget Risks \n● Campus Alberta Grant: High. Given the current fiscal position of the provincial \ngovernment receiving a two per cent increase the grant is considered high risk. \n● NASA Collective Agreement: Medium. The NASA collective agreement is subject to a \nwage re-opener if the Alberta government and its employees negotiate a general wage \nincrease. This could result in an increase to the university’s salary expenditures. \n● Impact of ATB and Merit: Medium to High. Each faculty and unit is responsible to fund \ntheir cost of ATB and merit. Each unit has a different capacity to meet these \ncommitments. Depending on the unit, this may require a re-alignment of budgets \nincluding position eliminations and staff layoffs.  \n● Salary equity Issues: Medium. The university is continuing its review of data to assess \nwhether salary equity issues are present within the academic workforce. If salary equity \nissues are identified they will need to be addressed by the university.  \nCapital and ancillary budgets \nCapital  \nThe university can only achieve its vision if it has access to well-maintained and financially \nsupported facilities. Through a focus on well-planned functional renewal and re-purposing of \nexisting fiscal assets, essential teaching and research capacity that meets the needs of \ntomorrow’s students, teachers and researchers, can be delivered. The university’s ability to \nmeet its own and the province’s post-secondary access objectives, focusing on energy and \ncarbon footprint reductions and supporting the move to renewal energy (supporting the Climate \nLeadership Plan), depends on increased and stable investment in deferred maintenance, and \nstrategic investment in the renewal, upgrading and repurposing of older facilities, minimizing the \nneed for new construction. \nTable 3 lists the approved capital projects for 2017-18. New and continuing capital construction \nis budgeted at $128.7 million of the $156.7 million in capital projects. The capital budget also \nincludes $28 million in capital program spending under the infrastructure maintenance and \nenergy management programs. Table 3 also identifies proposed capital projects which are \nsubject to available funding. Detailed information on the university’s Capital Plan can be found in \nAppendix F. \nC OMPR E HE N S I VE  I NS T I TU T ION A L  PL A N  -  DR A FT \n38 \nD\nR\nA\nF\nTable 3. 2017-18 Capital Budget ($,000) \nC OMPR E HE N S I VE  I NS T I TU T ION A L  PL A N  -  DR A FT \n39 \nD\nR\nA\nF\nAncillaries \nThe university runs several large ancillary operations: Ancillary Services (Residence Services, \nHospitality Services, Parking, Real Estate and Commercial Property Management Services, \nONEcard) the Bookstore, Enterprise Square, the University Health Centre, and Utilities.  \nAll of the university’s ancillaries must generate the required revenues to support their operating \nand ongoing capital requirements as well as maintain both operating and capital reserves. \nAncillary Services \nDespite short-term deficiencies in annual net operations, Ancillary Services maintains a \nsubstantial positive reserve balance. Significant challenges include: Michener Park housing \ncomplex deferred maintenance; increasing residence vacancy rate; increasing costs associated \nwith unionization; increasing minimum wage and the carbon levy; and residence and parking \nrates at, or near, market rates. These factors will need to be monitored carefully in terms of the \nlong-term impact on our financial position. \nBookstore \nThe bookstore continues to be challenged with e-delivery of course materials and increasing \ncompetition. With a strong focus on cost control, the bookstore is returning to a positive net \nposition. \nEnterprise Square \nEnterprise square remains in a positive financial position with all of its space fully occupied. \nUniversity Health Centre \nThe University Health Centre remains in a positive financial position. The area of greatest \nconcern is the continuation of targeted mental health funding from government to support the \nprovision of additional mental health support services. \nUtilities \nUtilities net operations remain in a balanced position while providing highly-dependable and \ncost-effective utilities to North Campus and surrounding entities. The greatest uncertainty is the \nlong-term impact of the government’s carbon levy policies and the need to upgrade its power \ngrid infrastructure to align with enhancements being implemented by EPCOR.  \nC OMPR E HE N S I VE  I NS T I TU T ION A L  PL A N  -  DR A FT \n40 \nD\nR\nA\nF\nTable 4. 2017-18 Ancillaries Budget and Projections ($,000)  \nC OMPR E HE N S I VE  I NS T I TU T ION A L  PL A N  -  DR A FT \n41 \nD\nR\nA\nF\nConclusion \nThe university faces significant budget uncertainty in 2017-18 however is determined to move \nforward with the implementation of its goals, objectives and strategies as outlined in For the \nPublic Good. As a major economic driver in the province and a critical contributor to the \ndiversification of the Alberta economy, the university looks to the province for stable and \npredictable funding. Working in partnership with the government, the university is confident that \nit can fulfill its mandate, provide a dynamic and integrated learning and research environment \nand meet the social, cultural and economic needs of this province.  \nStatement of Operations and Cash Flow \nStatement of Operations Budget for the Years ending March 31, 2018 \nto 2021 ($,000)  \nTable 5.
