The U of A remains committed to attracting outstanding \nundergraduate and graduate students, post-doctoral fellows, and professors from Alberta, \nacross Canada, and abroad. Our academy represents and contributes to Canada’s and \nAlberta’s cultural diversity and is an inclusive community that values the perspectives of all \ngenders, Indigenous people, under-represented groups, and people from rural and northern \ncommunities. Along with an exceptional professoriate and highly-skilled technical and \nprofessional staff, these individuals create an integrated environment of teaching, learning, \nresearch, and creative activities. The U of A aims to provide enriched and transformative student \nexperiences, resulting in graduates who are engaged global citizens, prepared to contribute to \nthe social and economic wellbeing of the province, the nation, and the world. \nAccess to programs at the U of A evolves in response to student demand, workforce needs, and \nthe availability of resources. The University of Alberta values its relationships with other Campus \nAlberta institutions, and continues to engage in productive inter-institutional collaborations \nsupporting transfer and learner mobility.  \nPrograms listed in the table below are drawn from the Provider and Program Registry System \n(PaPRS). It is important to note that there is not perfect alignment between the programs listed \nin PaPRS and those listed in the University Calendar. There are a number of historical and \nsystems-related reasons for this incongruity, but university staff and staff from Advanced \nEducation are collaborating to create better alignment. \nGraduate Full Load Equivalent (FLE) numbers are artificially-inflated by the methodology for \ncalculating FLEs prescribed by PaPRS and the Learning Enrolment Reporting System (LERS). \nGraduate FLEs are calculated based on a fixed per-credit allocation which may not correspond \nto the actual credit load of a full-time U of A graduate student. For example, PaPRS assigns \nthesis-based graduate students a full load of 18 credits, while a full-time U of A graduate student \nregistered in a thesis-based program has a normal full load of 30 credits (1.7 FLE). Normal full \nloads for course-based programs at the U of A vary by program according to structure and \nrequirements. At the U of A, headcount is a more accurate reflection of graduate enrolment. \nUniversity of Alberta staff are engaged with Advanced Education about the challenges related to \nthese data.  \nWhile the table below provides enrolment figures and projections for all programs, as per the \ngovernment’s CIP guidelines, enrolment targets are provided at a faculty level. In most cases, \nthe university does not manage enrolment to a specific target or quota by program. Rather, \nenrolment is managed to an overall faculty-level target. This allows individual programs to \nrespond dynamically to changes in demand, supporting the growth and sustainability of \nemerging areas of study. This approach also allows students greater flexibility to change \nprograms within the same faculty as they refine their interests and educational goals. Finally, it \nallows faculties to manage the evolution of their programs, starting new ones and phasing out \nold ones without affecting overall enrolment numbers at the faculty level.  \nC OMPR E HE N S I VE  I NS T I TU T ION A L  PL A N  -  DR A FT \n44 \nD\nR\nA\nF\nInternational enrolment, reported here as per the CIP guidelines, varies widely across programs, \nespecially between undergraduate and graduate programs. Overall, the university’s proportion \nof international students (graduate and undergraduate) is approximately 17 per cent, with the \nproportion for the undergraduate student body at 14 per cent. International enrolment within \nindividual programs is driven by a variety of factors, including, in many cases, specific \narrangements with governments in foreign countries. Graduate programs tend to have a higher \nproportion of international enrolment than undergraduate programs. This is consistent with other \nmajor research universities, and reflects the university’s contribution to attracting outstanding \nfuture researchers, innovators, and professionals to Alberta. Graduate students pursue \nadvanced degrees based on where they can work with key scholars, or in internationally known \nprograms. A strong majority of international students will remain in Alberta post-graduation. \nAccording to the 2012 Graduate Outcomes Survey, 84 per cent of Alberta students who lived \noutside Canada at the time they applied to school remained in Alberta after graduation. At the U \nof A in particular, more than 70 per cent of international PhD students who entered the U of A \nbetween 2004 and 2006 had become Permanent Residents or citizens of Canada by the time \nthey graduated.  \nProposed programming changes \nQuality Degree Programs \nThe U of A offers foundational and relevant programs with the goal to educate every student as \nan engaged and informed citizen and lifelong learner. We provide a sophisticated layering of \ncredentials through programming designed to meet the needs of all learners seeking \nbaccalaureate credentials and higher, through to the highest professional and scientific \ndesignations.  \nAlongside traditional degree programs, there is growing interest in, and need for, combined \ndegrees, interdisciplinary programs, and cross-disciplinary embedded certificates. One of the \nfastest-growing credential types is the Course-Based Master’s degree, reflecting the need in \nindustries and communities for workers prepared with advanced degrees, Learning is a lifelong \nprocess, and the complexity of today’s economy means that learners return to the university for \nnew credentials throughout their careers. Diversity is captured in new programs and content and \nin approaches to studying problems and implementing solutions. New approaches to higher \neducation include regular evaluation of programs—allowing for refreshing and phasing out of \nprograms not sufficiently responsive to current educational, economic, and scientific needs \nalongside the development of new ones—and the development of traditional credentials (e.g., \nprofessional programs) to meet evolving learner needs.  \nThe university anticipates The University of Alberta \nhas prioritized increasing the opportunities for, and participation rates in, experiential learning for \nboth undergraduate and graduate students, through engagement with community, industry, \nprofessional organizations, and government locally, nationally, and internationally. The \nUniversity has a rich history of providing experiential learning opportunities that enhance the \nlearning experience and provide platforms through which our students will be better equipped to \nenter or re-enter the workforce post-graduation. Robust professional development, including internships, events, \nworkshops and online resources, enhances their job market competitiveness. It also better \nprepares students to use their advanced degrees and other credentials beyond the academy. \nThis movement is critical for the future career paths of the students as well as for the \ndevelopment of the industries, organizations, and disciplines in which they will work. At the U of \nA, this priority is facilitated by a new requirement that each graduate student create a plan for \ntheir own development and undertake at least eight hours of professional development activity \nduring the course of their graduate degree. Professional development plans require students to \nresearch potential career paths, assess their own skillsets and fill any gaps, and establish a plan \nfor moving from graduate degree to career. \nENROLMENT TABLE APPENDED SEPARATELY  \nC OMPR E HE N S I VE  I NS T I TU T ION A L  PL A N  -  DR A FT \nAPPENDIX C \nRESEARCH, APPLIED RESEARCH, AND \nSCHOLARLY ACTIVITIES \nAs one of Canada’s top research-intensive institutions, the U of A leads the province in world-\nclass research outcomes and student experiences that drive innovation and enhance social, \ncultural, and economic development. The university offers research and doctoral programs \nacross seven thematic areas: humanities and fine arts, social structure and systems, science \nand technology, energy, environment, food and bio-resources, and health and wellness. This full \nspectrum of learning and inquiry positions the U of A’s academy and its graduates to make the \ncomprehensive contributions towards scientific, social, and cultural innovations needed to \nsupport Alberta’s post-secondary system principles and its identified provincial outcomes: \neffective resource and environmental management, a broadened economic base, and resilient \nand healthy individuals and communities. \nThe University of Alberta’s ground-breaking research focuses on redefining the social, cultural, \nenvironmental, and technological contexts that we will need in the future. Teacher education, for \nexample, must evolve to address new demands in schools where increased population diversity, \nas well as shifts in employment and industry, are influencing what and how we teach our \nchildren. Similar demographic changes are influencing the delivery of health care and health \ninformation. Increased use and fast-paced changes in communications technology are \ninfluencing how people access, consume, and critically examine information—all skills that \nuniversity graduates need. \nAt the forefront of training future researchers and innovators, the University of Alberta produces \nthe highest quality graduates, researchers, educators, highly-trained professionals and skilled \npersonnel for potential employers, ranging from social agencies, to schools, to other research-\nintensive universities, to industries, and to public and private corporations. Research must also \nbe recognized as an important career path in itself that supports the aspirations of individual \nAlbertans and the province as a whole. \nAccessibility \nAccessibility and diversity  \nAccessibility and diversity initiatives enable equitable access to positions of influence that shape \nand set research agendas and priorities, and to a full range of personal growth opportunities and \neventual employment paths. The U of A must bridge a critical gender gap in the professoriate, \nparticularly in the most senior ranks, and must strive to increase the representation of \nIndigenous scholars in the professoriate. The U of A must also strive to increase numbers of \nwomen and Indigenous people in STEM (Science, Technology, Engineering, and Math) \ndisciplines. Despite long-term efforts to achieve gender balance in the academy, male faculty \nmembers continue to significantly outnumber female faculty. Indigenous people currently \ncomprise only one per cent of the professoriate. There is a dearth of Indigenous people with \nPhDs in Canada, and the university plays a key role in providing opportunities for Indigenous \npeople to reach this credential. Indigenous people achieve higher education at the rate of about \n12 per cent, only about half the rate among the general population (about 23 per cent). The U of \nC OMPR E HE N S I VE  I NS T I TU T ION A L  PL A N  -  DR A FT \n48 \nD\nR\nA\nF\nA, in partnership with other post-secondaries, must address this education gap at the \nundergraduate level by recruiting and offering appropriate supports to Indigenous students—one \nof the Calls to Action in the Truth and Reconciliation Commission report.  \nBalance of professors, post-doctoral fellows, graduate students and \nundergraduate students \nIn 2015-16, the U of A was home to over 36,000 students (almost 30,000 undergraduate and \nover 7,000 graduate). We are home to 2,071 full-time faculty members, approximately 600 post-\ndoctoral fellows, and 926 contract academic staff. Maintaining the right balance among \nprofessors, post-doctoral fellows, graduate, and undergraduate students is critical to the \nuniversity’s ability to offer access to a world-class education and an environment that nurtures \nand supports the full educational and career lifecycle of all U of A members. \nGraduate education and post-doctoral fellows \nOver 200 graduate programs, attended by over 7,000 graduate students and nearly 600 post-\ndoctoral trainees, are intricately entwined with the U of A’s learning, teaching, and discovery \nmandate. Individuals educated to the PhD level enable large-scale, visionary, and ambitious \nresearch agendas that have the potential for big impact results. They drive innovation and \nresearch within and beyond the university, including in industrial, community, and social \nsettings. They are key to shifting Alberta to a learning-based society that welcomes intellectual \nand economic diversity.  \nThe Faculty of Graduate Studies and Research Professional Development program advances \nresearch and develops professional skills and qualities sought by employers, making the U of \nA’s graduate students competitive in the global market for traditional educational positions (e.g., \nprofessors), industry positions (e.g., industrial research and development) and within \ncommunities (e.g., in the public service or non-profit sector).  \nThe post-doctoral complement is critical to Alberta’s research capacity and impact, yet receives \nno provincial support. Post-doctoral fellows are essential to our role as a global partner of choice \nfor international research consortia and training programs with top-tier collaborators. As highly \neducated and mobile researchers, post-doctoral fellows ensure the continuing vitality of the \nacademic community and increase the speed of sharing of knowledge and ideas. An expanded \nand diverse community of post-doctoral fellows will contribute to the U of A’s capacity to \ndiscover novel solutions to complex social, cultural, and economic challenges. Cohort targets \nwill be determined in response to the U of A strategic plan. \nQuality \nIn 2016, the U of A began to identify and support established and emerging areas of global \nresearch distinction and leadership by building a portfolio of signature areas that distinguish us \nfrom our peer institutions in Campus Alberta and beyond, and which exemplify the University of \nAlberta’s capacity to engage in big questions and global challenges. The first signature areas \nwill be announced in 2017. \nResearch initiatives at the U of A build on our prominent international collaborations to make \nsignificant contributions to areas of fundamental importance to Albertans (including energy and \nthe environment, health, and economic diversification), and to recruit and train the innovative \nleaders of tomorrow. We leverage provincial funding to attract greater external investment from \nthe public and private sector, including participation in prominent federal funding programs such \nas the Canada First Research Excellence Fund (CFREF), and to foster long-term cycles of \nsignificant re-investment in Alberta.  \nC OMPR E HE N S I VE  I NS T I TU T ION A L  PL A N  -  DR A FT \n49 \nD\nR\nA\nF\nOur strategic research priorities include projects focusing on environmental stewardship, \neffective resource management, supporting healthy Albertans in all of our communities, and a \nstrong economy. Selected strategic research projects include: \nA Strong Economy and Effective Resource Management \nFuture Energy Systems \nFuture Energy Systems brings together researchers across disciplines to improve energy \nsystems related to unconventional hydrocarbon resources—tailings ponds, greenhouse \nemissions, water use, land reclamation, and safe, efficient energy transportation. Future Energy \nSystems is multi-disciplinary and will examine energy demands, sources and processes through \na broad range of perspectives including social, economic, legal-scientific and technological. \nBuilding on the U of A’s strengths in advanced materials, smart electrical grids and \nbioprocessing, Future Energy Systems will help move Canada to a low-carbon energy economy. \nThe $75-million federal investment is part of the Canada First Research Excellence Fund to \nstrategically invest in areas where post-secondary research institutions have a competitive \nadvantage and can become global leaders. \nSCENEREI  \nTwo world-class research universities in energy and the environment, Tsinghua University in \nBeijing, China and the University of Alberta, have established a substantial collaborative \nresearch program—the Sino-Canadian Energy and Environment Research and Education \nInitiative (SCENEREI) – to address the world’s growing need for sustainable energy solutions \nthat offer reduced water use and minimized environmental impact. Stage 1 and Stage 2 of this \ncollaboration, totaling 34 research projects with more than $1.5 million in research investment, \ninclude: clean energy (hydrocarbon recovery, lignite upgrading, geoscience), energy policy \n(regulation, supply chains, socioeconomics), transportation (infrastructure), and the environment \n(water, monitoring, minimizing impact). \nThe partnership works on carbon capture and storage, geothermal energy, land reclamation and \nwater management, opening up new opportunities for technology transfer and collaboration with \nbusiness and industry. \nHelmholtz (HAI) \nHAI-E&E (Energy and Environment), a collaboration involving over 50 U of A researchers and \nfour Helmholtz research institutes in Germany, is involved in exploring cleaner energy \nproduction and solutions to pressing environmental issues facing Alberta’s oilsands and \nGermany’s coal production. \nEnvironmental Stewardship \nTracking Change, funded through SSHRC’s Partnership Grant, supports local and traditional \nknowledge research activities in the Mackenzie River basin and sister projects in the Lower \nAmazon and Lower Mekong River Basins. The project is led by the U of A alongside the \nTraditional Knowledge Steering Committee of the Mackenzie River Basin Board, the \nGovernment of Northwest Territories, and many other valued organizations. Its long-term goal is \nto strengthen the voices of subsistence fishers and Indigenous communities in the governance \nof major fresh-water ecosystems. \nSupporting Healthy Albertans in all of our Communities \nAddictions and mental health \nThe Addictions and Mental Health Research Lab in the School of Public Health is a collaborative \nand multi-disciplinary initiative that works closely with policy makers and health practitioners to \nmobilize knowledge. Current priorities include the Edmonton Drug Use and Health Survey, part \nC OMPR E HE N S I VE  I NS T I TU T ION A L  PL A N  -  DR A FT \n50 \nD\nR\nA\nF\nof a project funded by the Canadian Institutes of Health Research on evidence-based harm \nreduction policy. \nHealth and life sciences \nHelmholtz (HAI) partnerships strengthen health and life sciences research, such as infectious \ndisease, neuro-degenerative disease research and most recently diabetes research. An \nemerging partnership within HAI is the collaboration in diabetes research between the Alberta \nDiabetes Institute (ADI) and Helmholtz Zentrum Mϋnchen (HZM). Possible fields of joint \nstrategic research also identified include: metabolism and obesity, drug targets, gene and \nhormone therapy and cell therapies and Immunology. \nCoordination \nThe U of A embraces coordination with partners within and beyond Alberta to advance a \ndiversity of research priorities and agendas and to ensure the efficient and effective use of \nresources through collaborations that leverage our research strengths and those of partner \ninstitutions. In keeping with this approach, the U of A continues to build and support \ncollaborative research capacity to produce leading-edge research on critical issues that confront \nhumanity. Priority collaborations supporting Alberta’s social and economic outcomes are \nidentified above, and the university remains open to and encouraging of a wide range of \nresearch partnerships and collaborations.  \nThe university is committed to working with other partners in Alberta’s post-secondary sector to \nensure that Albertans have access to a range of programs to satisfy their post-secondary goals \nand employment needs. Graduate programs in particular are closely linked to the university’s \nresearch faculty and activities, and a robust and varied research enterprise directly supports the \navailability of a diversity of programming across the learning continuum.  \nThe U of A also remains committed to community-level collaboration, whether through the \ninvolvement of community partners in research or through ensuring that graduate students \ndevelop the skills to excel across all sectors of society. Initiatives to support collaboration in \nresearch are directly supportive of the university’s quality and accessibility objectives and are \ndescribed in Appendix D. \nC OMPR E HE N S I VE  I NS T I TU T ION A L  PL A N  -  DR A FT \n51 \nD\nR\nA\nF\nAPPENDIX D \nCOMMUNITY OUTREACH AND UNDER-\nREPRESENTED LEARNERS \nThe University of Alberta engages with a diverse set of stakeholder communities, defined by \ngeography, by cultural, linguistic, or ethnic affiliation, or by interest or practice. The U of A is \ncommitted to celebrating and supporting a diversity of cultures, languages, economies, and, \nespecially, ideas. The title of the new Institutional Strategic Plan, For the Public Good, reflects \nthis commitment. Engagement occurs in many forms and contexts: formally and informally; \ninternally and externally; locally, nationally, and globally. Community engagement is integral to \nthe U of A’s approach to achieving excellence and relevance in teaching and research, to \norganizational effectiveness, to infrastructure development, and to serving its diverse \nstakeholder communities. Much of the U of A’s engagement work is long-term in nature and \nreflects ongoing, mutually beneficial relationships between the university and the communities to \nwhich it is accountable. The sections below outline institutional priorities for 2017-19. Priorities \nand partnerships related to research and to capital planning are addressed in appendices C and \nF, respectively. \nAs part of its commitment to foster academic excellence through diversity, the U of A continues \nto prioritize recruiting and supporting under-represented and Indigenous learners. In concert, the \nuniversity is committed to actively responding to the Truth and Reconciliation Commission of \nCanada. \nCommunity Outreach and Engagement \nShowcasing the value and impact of the U of A, its scholars, and its alumni \nThe university prioritizes opportunities to connect researchers with communities and to enhance \nthe impact and profile of their work. For example, the Community-University Partnership for the \nStudy of Children, Youth, and Families brings together U of A researchers with community \nagencies from across Alberta. Through projects like the Centre for Public Involvement, a \npartnership between the U of A and City of Edmonton, the university participates in creating \n‘living laboratories’ to improve the practice of community engagement and outreach. And \nthrough programs like USchool, which partners with schools across Alberta to expose K-12 \nstudents to future possibilities in post-secondary education, the U of A builds bridges between \nits researchers and socially vulnerable communities. \nThe U of A connects its researchers to the general public through programs like the U of A \nSpeakers’ Bureau, partnered Speakers’ Series with the Edmonton Public Library, and many \nothers. The U of A also profiles its next generation of researchers through participation at the \nannual international Falling Walls Conference before a judging panel of academic, government, \nmedia and industry leaders. In addition, the university highlights exceptional efforts at \nengagement through its Community Connections Awards, which honour those whose work \nembodies the U of A’s promise of “uplifting the whole people” and has a positive impact on \ncommunities near and far. \nThe University of Alberta’s alumni showcase the positive impacts of its graduates on \ncommunities locally and abroad. The university will build on past successes as it expands \noutreach initiatives with graduates at all stages of their post-university life, with particular \nC OMPR E HE N S I VE  I NS T I TU T ION A L  PL A N  -  DR A FT \n52 \nD\nR\nA\nF\nemphasis on encouraging graduates from the last decade to re-engage with the university to \nhelp connect with their communities and participate in outreach and volunteer initiatives. Alumni \nengagement in 2017 will include conducting an alumni needs assessment to ensure that \nprograms, services, and benefits align with the interests of the alumni community. \nRecognizing that the university has a significant national profile, Canada’s sesquicentennial year \noffers an opportunity to raise awareness and pride around the university's distinctive and \npowerful contributions to Canadian history—to bring to light stories of achievement, \naccomplishment, and individual and shared histories that contribute to Canada's national fabric \nand its diverse sense of place and self. Engagement activities will be undertaken throughout the \nyear on the university's multiple campuses and through the digital space. \nInstitutional marketing and communications strategy \nFor 2017-18, the U of A has prioritized the development of a new institutional marketing and \ncommunications strategy that will include the building of a brand platform and launch of a news \nsite to tell the institution’s story in broader, more accessible ways. Working with internal \npartners, the marketing and communications strategy will use multiple delivery platforms to \nshowcase the U of A's economic, social, and other impacts, locally and globally. This strategy is \nintended to raise the U of A’s profile while increasing Albertans’ and Canadians’ awareness of \nthe value of the university. The news site, separate from, but unabashedly connected to the \nUniversity of Alberta, will carry a wide range of stories produced across the institution, and look \nand feel like a news website. Although completion of the marketing and communications \nstrategy is targeted for 2018-19, work to communicate the university's contributions is ongoing. \nSupporting community through campuses and infrastructure \nUniversity infrastructure across all campuses serves firstly to ensure adequate teaching, \nresearch, and experiential spaces for students, faculty, and staff, and secondly to provide \nshared spaces where citizens, scholars, research discoveries, sports, culture, language, and \nperforming arts can intersect. The U of A works continuously to maintain relationships with \nsurrounding communities and to develop new relationships with communities, near and far, of \nlearners, researchers, professionals as well as neighborhoods, municipalities, and countries. \nLocally, the institution will leverage its five campuses and infrastructure assets to establish \ngathering places that connect the university and communities. Rurally based Augustana \nCampus and bilingually focused Campus Saint-Jean boast unique opportunities to engage with \ndistinct communities. In 2017-18, the university will commence an amendment to the Long \nRange Development Plan for the Augustana Campus. The university places a high level of \nimportance on linguistic duality and multi-culturalism and will develop strategies that position the \ninstitution and Campus Saint-Jean as the leader in attracting local and international students \nwishing to further their post-secondary education in French. New infrastructure priorities for the \nnext three years include the proposed community arena complex on South Campus that, in \npartnership with the City of Edmonton, will provide enhanced sport infrastructure for the \nuniversity and city as a whole. Capital priorities are addressed in Appendix F. \nExperiential and community-engaged learning \nThrough a strengthened emphasis on experiential learning and professional development, the U \nof A also aims to enhance connections between students and their community (including \nemployers and not-for-profit organizations). At the undergraduate level, the university supports \nover 1,500 community service-learning (CSL) placements per year, working with nearly 200 \ncommunity organizations since 2003. Leveraging this success, the university will complement its \nstrong support for co-operative education and CSL with a focus on increasing the number and \naccessibility of experiential learning opportunities.  \nC OMPR E HE N S I VE  I NS T I TU T ION A L  PL A N  -  DR A FT \n53 \nD\nR\nA\nF\nAt the graduate level, the U of A’s emphasis on professional development is reflected in the \nmulti-year initiative of the Faculty of Graduate Studies and Research to place students in \nprofessional internships, providing mutually beneficial opportunities with community-based \norganizations and industry. The U of A is also engaged with global efforts to ‘redefine the PhD’ \nto ensure that industry benefits from more highly-prepared graduates and that PhD graduates \ncan consider robust non-academic career paths. These initiatives will not only enhance current \ncommunity relationships, but also better position our students to make an impact after \ngraduation.  \nUnder-represented and Indigenous learners \nThe U of A is pursuing a range of strategies to enhance the diversity of its student body and \nincrease participation among under-represented and Indigenous students. The active \nrecruitment of Indigenous students is complemented by student supports that aim to improve \nstudent success, and is supported by a broader institutional approach to Indigenization and \nreconciliation (outlined below).  \nThe U of A’s multi-campus environment, in particular Augustana and CSJ, plays a key role in \nsupporting access to the U of A among rural and francophone students. Augustana offers a \nleading liberal arts education in a smaller institutional setting, while the scope of CSJ’s French-\nlanguage programming is unique in western Canada. \nThrough a new national recruitment strategy, the institution is seeking to diversify the \nuniversity’s Canadian student body, while continuing to prioritize access for Albertans. Diversity \nenriches the academic environment and student experience, while ensuring that the university \nmore closely reflects the demographic composition of Alberta and Canada.  \nThe digital environment is an important component of the U of A’s efforts to expand access to \nlearning. The U of A continues to be a leader in the delivery of Massive Open Online Courses, \nor MOOCs, and to date, nearly a quarter of a million registrants have taken a MOOC at the \nUniversity of Alberta. With a wide variety of subject matter, classes are offered to the public for \ngeneral interest as well as for-credit for U of A students and students at other institutions. The \ncourses, which have included Dino 101, led by world-renowned paleontologist Dr. Phil Currie \nand Understanding Video Games and More, an area of emerging strength in Alberta, encourage \nlifelong learning, serve as a bridge to draw in non-traditional students and bring meaningful, \nuniversity-level content to participants. Two new MOOCs, Mountains 101 and Indigenous \nCanada, launched in 2017.  \nIndigenization and reconciliation \nThrough sharing, listening, and supporting as well as enhanced curriculum and programming for \nin-class or in the digital realm, the U of A will continue to take steps to value, recognize, and \nreconcile with Indigenous communities and traditional ways of knowing. Advancing the \nconstruction of the new Maskwa House of Learning, a gathering and academic space for the \nIndigenous students on North Campus, remains a high priority. The institution continues to \ndevelop strategies to increase enrolment of Indigenous students in all faculties, with the goal of \nenrolling Indigenous students at least proportionately to their representation in the Alberta \npopulation. Aboriginal Students Support Services continues to provide Indigenous students \nassistance so that they can be successful throughout their post-secondary experience at the U \nof A. An Indigenous Cultural Support Office has been initiated to support culturally sensitive \ndevelopment of initiatives, curriculum, and individual faculty and staff. Deliberate efforts will \ncontinue within faculties to encourage participation in enduring reconciliation. The university \nprovides multiple avenues to learn about historical relationships between the Indigenous and \nC OMPR E HE N S I VE  I NS T I TU T ION A L  PL A N  -  DR A FT \n54 \nD\nR\nA\nF\nnon-Indigenous communities through traditional classroom offerings across several faculties in \naddition to the new Indigenous Canada MOOC.  \nC OMPR E HE N S I VE  I NS T I TU T ION A L  PL A N  -  DR A FT \n55 \nD\nR\nA\nF\nAPPENDIX E \nINTERNATIONALIZATION \nThe University of Alberta’s international collaborations create exceptional learning, discovery, \ncitizenship, and innovation opportunities. Such collaborations position the U of A among the \nworld’s top public institutions, and advance the university’s international reputation for \nexcellence in teaching and learning. The U of A actively cultivates a diverse academy that \nwelcomes international students and scholars and that offers Albertans a world-class, globally \nminded education.  \nIn conjunction with the implementation of the university’s strategic plan, For the Public Good, in \n2017-18, the U of A will establish an internal leadership structure to guide strategic engagement \non international initiatives. The President’s Committee on International Strategy will ensure that \nthe university’s approach to global engagement continues to reflect a forward-looking, system-\nwide perspective.  \nSpecific priorities for 2017-20 include exploring strategic options to further diversify the \nuniversity’s population of international students while maintaining the overall proportion of \ninternational students among the student body. A large majority of Alberta’s international \nstudents will remain in Alberta post-graduation (84 per cent, according to the 2012 Graduate \nOutcomes Survey), and international students educated at the U of A provide the province with \nadditional highly-skilled, innovative, and entrepreneurial professionals. They also bring cultural \ndiversity together with global connections, insights, and perspectives on shared issues and \nchallenges.  \nThe university also remains focused on delivering international education by providing study \nabroad and global engagement opportunities to our students, offering experiential, internship, \nand co-operative education experiences in over 60 countries, as well as multiple joint and dual \ndegree programs with partner institutions from around the world. The Alberta School of Business \nalso offers a Master of Financial Management program in China in partnership with Xi’an Jiao \nTong University (89 full-time registrants in 2015-16). A complete list of the university’s \ninternational shared credential (joint/dual degree) programs is provided in the university’s 2016 \nAnnual Report, available at ualberta.ca/reporting.  \nAs a research-intensive institution, the U of A pursues active collaborations with partners all over \nthe world. Global research engagement has many benefits to the U of A and the province, \nincluding leveraging funding resources and expertise from other parts of the globe to address \nchallenges that affect everyone, including Albertans. Key research partnerships are addressed \nin Appendix C. \nC OMPR E HE N S I VE  I NS T I TU T ION A L  PL A N  -  DR A FT \n56 \nD\nR\nA\nF\nAPPENDIX F \nCAPITAL PLAN \nCapital planning considerations: strategic investment \nand long-range planning \nBackground \nAs Alberta’s largest and oldest post-secondary institution, the University of Alberta manages \nmore than 1.8 million square metres of complex facility inventory, ranging in age from more than \n100 years old to new. With the completion of the Institutional Strategic Plan, For the Public \nGood, and aligned with the Government’s Adult Learning System’s Principles of accessibility, \naffordability, quality, coordination and accountability, the university has refined its focus to \naddress deferred maintenance and functional renewal and repurposing of existing physical \nassets, as well as reviewing how underused space can provide cost-effective opportunities for \nconsolidation and repurposing to enhance space in order to build essential teaching and \nresearch capacity. With this key work completed, critical expansions would be required only to \nmeet the needs of tomorrow’s students, teachers and researchers, and to maintain the \ninstitution’s competitive global position. \nWith five well-established major campuses to support, the U of A has carefully evaluated its \nacademic requirements across all areas to ensure that all capital investments form part of the \nlong-term vision in support of For the Public Good. These priority investments would capitalize \non the utilization of existing, but old and functionally inadequate, infrastructure. Through \nrenovation and repurposing, the university would initially reduce its current level of deferred \nmaintenance ($938 million) by approximately $100-150 million, depending on the level of capital \ninvestment available. \nBy reclaiming classroom space for faculties that are badly space-constrained, university \npriorities improve program outcomes across a number of key faculties and, in some instances, \nwill negate the requirement for a new build. Reclaimed space reduces reliance on leased space, \nsets up the conditions for projects to support multiple faculties, and results in a ‘domino’ effect to \nsupport other faculties on North Campus through the provision of purpose-designed and built \nspace and the repurposing and renovation of vacated space in multiple buildings. Additionally, \ninvestment in the Augustana and Campus Saint-Jean campuses is key to maintaining and \ndeveloping programming for rural liberal arts and sciences as well as the only fully bilingual \npostsecondary education campus in western Canada. \nDeferred maintenance \nRecent investment in new facilities has created an opportunity, through backfill of vacated \nspace, to creatively address physical and functional renewal of our buildings. Many older \nfacilities were not designed to accommodate the functional and operational requirements of \ncurrent and future faculty and students (Faculty Renewal and Student Experience), nor are they \nparticularly energy efficient. Combining functional and physical renewal projects through backfill \nprojects provides a best-value model for capital projects, minimizing the need for new buildings \nwhile facilitating the needs of tomorrow’s learners and researchers, all at a significantly reduced \ncapital cost. \nC OMPR E HE N S I VE  I NS T I TU T ION A L  PL A N  -  DR A FT \n57 \nD\nR\nA\nF\nObjectives:  \n● Maximize opportunities to identify and proactively address deferred maintenance and \nfunctional renewal, through joint renewal and repurposing projects for existing building \ninventory that would also focus on energy and carbon footprint reductions and support \nthe move to renewable energy on an opportunity basis. \n● Ensure stable, long-term funding for deferred maintenance, and work with government to \ndevelop funding strategies that could support increased and sustained funding in the \norder of $25 million to $40 million annually. \n● Continue to maintain and improve the condition and functionality of the university’s \nphysical assets, which plays a critical role in our ability to attract, support, and retain the \nbest students, faculty, and staff. \n● Reduce the risk of building system failures that could affect life safety or result in building \nclosures. \n● Maintain the reliability of the university’s utility plant through focused investment in the \nfunctional renewal that deals with both deferred maintenance and increased efficiency \nwith new technology. \n● Support the academic and research goals of the institution and maximize use and life of \nexisting infrastructure, by ensuring that space is functional for current and future learning \nand research. \nPlanning \nIn order for high quality and detailed pre-design and planning services (including functional \nprogramming to lock scope) to be conducted, a multi-year allocation of planning funds is \nrequired. This will allow the university to focus specialist resources in support of program and \nbudget accuracy in order to be in position to develop innovative solutions that maximize the \nutilization of existing space while minimizing our ecological and carbon footprint, allowing us to \nimplement projects as funding becomes available. \nObjectives: \n● In the short-term, seek funding for pre-design services (including the development of \nbusiness cases) related to strategic priorities and major opportunities, or mandate both, \nto provide greater scope and budget certainty and to enable responses to new funding in \na timely manner.  \n● As greater levels of funding become available, seek a long-term funding envelope \nprogram that would include government contributions through capital and Climate \nLeadership grants as well as partnered contributions from internal sources where \npossible. \n● Provide strong and clear campus planning documents that are rooted in sustainability \nand leading urban design principles. The plans will seek to provide necessary direction, \nensuring academic program needs of the university are met, with careful consideration to \nthe expressed interests of the surrounding neighbourhoods and communities. \n● Reduce the university’s reliance on expensive, long-term commercial leasing with better \npre-design planning that anticipates the university’s quickly-changing space needs. \nC OMPR E HE N S I VE  I NS T I TU T ION A L  PL A N  -  DR A FT \n58 \nD\nR\nA\nF\nCapital projects, BLIMS and investment priorities  \nThe following strategic capital investment priorities are focused on improved asset utilization, \nreducing risk and deferred maintenance, repurposing existing infrastructure and reducing \nreliance on leased space while focusing on academic program outcomes in line with For the \nPublic Good.  \nFor each campus, two tables are presented. These include four in Lister Hall, and two in the \nCentral Academic Building.  \nPotential Projects with Carbon Reduction Opportunities in Support of Alberta’s \nClimate Leadership Plan \nThe following projects have been identified as potential contributors to reducing the university’s \ncarbon footprint in support of province-wide initiatives to reduce Alberta’s carbon emissions and \ncontribute to sustainability. \nCameron Library \nCameron Library has a significant building envelope problem with failure of exterior paneling \nsupports resulting in panels falling off the building face, presenting both a safety concern and a \ndirect impact to building operations. The condition of the exterior envelope is such that a full \nreplacement is required. \nThe project has been approached as a demonstration project showing that with student \nengagement it is possible to address deferred maintenance renewal of existing buildings in a \nmanner that trains the next generation of engineers and designers in the renewal of aged \nbuildings. Specific skills that students have brought to the project include; energy modelling, \ncreation of an intelligent Building Information Model, examination of various types of building \nenvelope solutions, solutions for embedded green poser and renewed mechanical systems with \nsolar thermal storage. Additionally, the project has a defined goal of 50 per cent reduction in \nenergy for an existing building with incorporation of solar thermal, photovoltaic, natural light \nreflectors and a thermal wall.  \nUniversiade Pavilion - Butterdome  \nThe building envelope for the Butterdome is deteriorating with significant rusting out of the \nexterior panel system. Reviews of the envelope have determined that the panel system will have \nto be completely replaced and the rain screen assembly renewed. A design review has been \nundertaken which incorporates retaining the identity of the building while supporting the \ninstallation of photovoltaic (PV) panels in the new wall system creating a significant green power \nsource and greenhouse gas reduction for the campus. The project has been planned to \nincorporate campus research for PV systems which provides demonstration projects for \nstudents on the skills required for a new workforce for new building envelope technologies. The \nproject will have a significant visual impact that can be pointed to as a great example of a \nbuilding renewal incorporating green energy. \nGreen Labs Program \nThe University of Alberta's Green Labs program supports our research community in building \nhealthy, safe and green teaching and research labs. Labs can use considerable amounts of \nwater, electricity and consumable materials. This program makes it easier for researchers to \nmaintain environmentally-responsible and healthy laboratory workspaces and engage in \npractices that reduce their ecological footprint, improve energy efficiency, conserve water and \nreduce waste. This program influences change through networking behaviour change programs, \nand incenting the use of ecologically preferable equipment.  \nSouth Campus District Energy System \nThe U of A proposes building a District Energy System (DES), incorporating combined heat and \npower (CHP), to meet the energy requirements of new facilities and to transition existing \nfacilities to the DES. It will have the ability to service government facilities in the future as the \nC OMPR E HE N S I VE  I NS T I TU T ION A L  PL A N  -  DR A FT \n63 \nD\nR\nA\nF\nopportunity becomes available. DES, including CHP, can make significant contributions to \nreducing GHG emissions. \nHighlights \nSummary of ongoing major capital projects \n● Research and Collection Resource Facility: $30 million (debt financed), turnover planned \nfor Sept 2017. On budget and \nschedule. \n● SIF: see above \nEnergy Management \nEnergy Management Program  \nGiven the success of the University of Alberta’s energy management program (EMP), the \nuniversity initiated a new round of energy projects for campus facilities. As in previous years, the \nnext generation of the EMP is financed through borrowing and paid back through resulting \nenergy savings. The accumulated impact of ongoing and significant investment in energy \nmanagement initiatives has aided in reducing greenhouse gas emissions and lowering operating \ncosts, added renewable energy to plant capacity, and contributed significantly in a partnership \napproach to the reduction of the deferred maintenance liability. For fiscal 2014-15 the utility cost \navoidance for the measures undertaken is estimated at $16,732,112. Since the inception of the \nUniversity of Alberta’s energy management program in 1975, the total accumulated cost \navoidance is estimated at $336 million. \nSustainability \nThe U of A’s Sustainability Commitment and Guiding Principles articulate our commitment to a \ncontinuous effort to instill sustainability into the many aspects of university life, on our \ncampuses, in our institution, and in the larger community of which we are part. The U of A \nstrives to manage all resources in harmony, recognizing the interconnectedness of ecological, \nsocial and economic systems.  \nThe U of A’s campus sustainability initiative is facilitated by the Office of Sustainability and \nguided by the goals and strategies outlined in both the Institutional Strategic Plan and \nSustainability Plan. The university monitors and reports our performance using the Sustainability \nTracking, Assessment & Rating System (STARS™). Developed by the Association for the \nAdvancement of Sustainability in Higher Education, this tool monitors performance in education \nand research, operations, planning, planning, administration, and engagement. The university \nattained a STARS™ Gold rating in 2014 (up from a Silver rating in 2012). The U of A will \ncomplete its third STARS™ assessment in 2017.  \nC OMPR E HE N S I VE  I NS T I TU T ION A L  PL A N  -  DR A FT \n64 \nD\nR\nA\nF\nUtilities \nThe District Energy System (DES), serving the U of A’s North Campus and other partners in the \nGreater Campus Area (GCA), has a number of priorities that address expansion and reliability \nissues. The first is to replace the aging electrical infrastructure in the Heating Plant. Phase two \nof approximately 8 phases has just been completed. This project is funded through Utilities \nreserves. The second major project is the expansion and upgrade of the electrical service from \nEPCOR’s Garneau substation to the U of A. This project is in preliminary design and regulatory \nphase and will require funding, for the GCA portion, from government to proceed. \nOn the U of A’s South Campus, in addition to the DES project identified above, the upgrade of \nbasic infrastructure, including the electrical service, needs to be addressed to accommodate \nfurther growth on this campus.  \nC OMPR E HE N S I VE  I NS T I TU T ION A L  PL A N  -  DR A FT \n65 \nD\nR\nA\nF\nAPPENDIX G \nINFORMATION TECHNOLOGY \nInformation Technology activities for the next three years will focus primarily on ensuring the \nadequacy and sustainability of institutional systems to support the university’s initiatives to drive \naccess, quality, coordination, and accountability and to implement the Institutional Strategic \nPlan, For the Public Good. There is a strong need to coordinate IT activities and acquisitions as \nIT infrastructure becomes fundamental to teaching, learning, research, data storage and \nreporting, and indeed living at the university. The university will pursue the following priorities \nover the next three year period:  \nInstitutional data and reporting \nThe university will pursue the continuous improvement and coordination of systems and \nprocedures through further development of the institutional data warehouse and associated \ninstitutional reporting, in support of the university’s coordination and accountability objectives. \nThis will be supported by base budget through the Campus Alberta Grant. \nBudgeting and Planning \nIn 2017-18, the U of A will implement new cloud-based capability for budgeting and planning. \nPhase one of the implementation will focus on the university’s operating fund, replacing the \nuniversity’s legacy budgeting systems and introducing best practices in budget planning and \nforecasting processes. Phase two of the project will allow faculties and portfolios to use the new \nbudget tool to budget across all funds at the university.  \nFinance and Student Systems Upgrades \nThe university is preparing for major upgrades of the financial and student information modules \nof PeopleSoft. The latter will enhance access and facilitate innovative teaching by improving \nflexibility in course duration, and therefore in program structure. For example, the “3-11” initiative \nat Augustana campus (splitting the traditional term into a 3-week and an 11-week segment, \nallowing for a 3 week intensive study period followed by a more traditional multiple course load) \nwill support strategic academic goals. Pre-upgrade assessments are required to define the \nscope of these two projects precisely; these assessments will be supported through the Campus \nAlberta Grant. The ensuing projects will require larger investments ($2 million to $5 million each) \nand may require incremental funding. \nStudent Life-Cycle Review \nInformation Services and Technology is currently leading a cross-institutional consultation on \nsystems and processes supporting the student lifecycle—from recruitment to alumni. These \nsystems underpin the university’s accessibility initiatives, including in recruitment, admission, \nand the provision of student supports. This project will lead to comprehensive system and \ncapability roadmaps and portfolio coordination beginning in 2017-18. The primary goal is to \nmeet medium-term institutional needs more cost-effectively by anticipating and combining \nrequirements across faculties and central units. Similarly, the projections of international enrolment as a percent of total enrolment is simply a \nreflection of 2015-16 actuals.\nGOA Credential GOA program\n2015-16 \nActual FLE\n2016-17
